The author of this book, having completed for you, O sons, this book which is a complete part of the science of medicine in its entirety and having reached the pinnacle of its clarity and expression, has deemed it fit to complete it for you with this article, which is a part of the practical application.
Because the practical application, being an improvement in our country and in our time, is completely absent, to such an extent that its science is studied and its impact forgotten. Only a few traces of it remain in the works of the earliest authors, who, through their hands, altered it, and its actual content became filled with errors and confusion, making its meanings obscure and its benefits remote.
I have therefore decided to revive it and compose this article, explaining, clarifying, and concisely summarizing its content. I will include illustrations of surgical instruments and other tools used in practice, as this adds clarity and addresses the needs of the subject matter.
The reason there are no skilled artisans in our time is that the practice of medicine requires extensive training, and one must first master the science of anatomy as described by Galen. So as to understand the benefits of organs, their structures, and compositions, as well as their connections, disconnections, bones, nerves, muscles, their numbers, origins, arteries, and veins, both moving and stationary.
Therefore, Albucasis said that many are called physicians, but few truly are, especially in the art of manual surgery, and we have mentioned something about this in the introduction to this book.
For whoever is not knowledgeable about the anatomy that we have mentioned is likely to make errors that cause people's deaths, as I have often seen many who falsely imagined themselves experts in this science.
I have seen a physician, ignorant of anatomy, attempt to cut a tumor in a woman's neck, accidentally damaging the neck's arteries and causing the patient to bleed to death in his hands.
I have also seen another physician, in attempting to remove a kidney stone from a man who had been stabbed, carelessly use an instrument that tore off a piece of the bladder wall along with the stone. The man died approximately three days later, and I had been called to remove the stone; upon examining it, along with the patient's condition, I estimated that this was the cause.
I have also seen another physician, who practiced medicine for a living, catering to some of our country's leaders.
I witnessed a physician, through his ignorance, hastily treating a young black boy who had a fracture near the ankle, along with a wound. The doctor, without understanding anatomy, tightly bound the fracture using iron bands and splints, completely obstructing the wound's air supply.
Then he released himself from his desires, and left him for several days, commanding him not to untie the bandage until his leg and foot had swollen, and he was on the verge of death.
Then I was summoned, and I quickly untied the bandage, bringing him relief and freeing him from his pains. However, the damage had already taken hold in the member, and I could not stop it.
The damage continued to spread in the member until he died.
I witnessed another physician treating a patient with a malignant tumor; it ulcerated after several days, and the condition of the sufferer grew worse. This is because if cancer is purely from black bile, then iron treatment should never be applied to it.
Unless it is in a member where the entire mass can be surgically removed.
Therefore, my son, you must know that manual work divides into two parts: the work accompanied by safety, and the work that leads to harm in most cases.
I have warned in every part of this book about the work that involves risk and fear; you must be cautious and reject it.
So that the ignorant do not find a way to speak or accuse, take firm and careful action for yourselves and gentle and stable care for your patients.
And employ the best path leading to safety and a favorable outcome, avoiding dangerous and difficult diseases.
Purify yourselves from what you fear may cast doubt upon your religion and world, for it is the stay of your honor and the elevation in the world and the hereafter of your destiny.
As Galen said in one of his precepts: 'Do not treat a bad disease and call yourself a bad doctor.'
This article is divided into three doors.
First Door.
In this door, we discuss the use of fire and sharp drugs, arranged from the ancient to modern times, with illustrations of instruments, cauterizing irons, and all that is needed for manual work.
Second Door.
In this door, we discuss incisions, blisters, cupping, bloodletting, surgeries, removing arrows, and other similar procedures, all arranged and illustrated with machines.
**Third Chapter**
In the chapter on reduction, dislocation, treatment of fractures, and similar topics, there is a systematic arrangement from ancient times to the present, along with illustrations of instruments.
**First Chapter**
**On Incision**
Before discussing the technique, it is necessary to mention the benefits and drawbacks of this procedure, as well as the temperament in which it should be used.
I say that discussing the benefits and drawbacks of incision is a lengthy and precise topic, containing a hidden secret, and several wise men have spoken on this matter.
They have differing opinions, and I have condensed their brief statements to avoid prolonging this discussion.
I say that incision is beneficial in general for all bad humors, whether with or without a material cause, except for two humors: the hot humor without a material cause and the dry humor without a material cause.
As for the humor with a material cause, there is disagreement. Some say that incision is beneficial in such cases, while others argue to the contrary, stating that incision is unsuitable for illnesses characterized by heat and dryness due to their inherently hot and dry nature.
It is impossible to cure a hot and dry disease with a hot and dry remedy, and he who says otherwise claims that burning with fire may be beneficial for a hot and dry disease occurring in the bodies of people. For when you add the human body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, it makes the human body cold.
And I say according to his saying, for experience has revealed this to me many times; however, one should not imagine this matter except for those who have become proficient and well-trained in the art of burning. And he [the surgeon] must be aware of the differences in the temperaments of people, as well as the nature of diseases within themselves, their causes, symptoms, and the duration of their course.
As for the other temperaments, you have no reason to fear them, especially cold and wet diseases. All doctors agree on this, and they do not differ in the benefit of burning in their treatment.
Know, O my sons, that the secret of treating with fire and its superiority to burning with a hot drug lies in the fact that fire is a single essence whose effect does not extend beyond the member that is burned, and it only slightly harms another connected member.
The use of caustic drugs may extend their effect beyond the intended organ, potentially causing a difficult-to-treat illness or even death. The fire, out of respect for its noble and generous essence, only does so when excessively applied.
This has become clear to us through experience, given the long service and care in the practice and observation of its facts.
For this reason, I have spared lengthy speech, and if it were not inappropriate for this book, I would have shared with you a secret, mysterious fire and its effect on bodies, as well as its ability to ward off illnesses, in philosophical, rational discourse that would astound your understanding.
Know, my sons, that there is disagreement regarding the optimal time for cauterization, and they consider spring to be the best season.
I say that cauterization can be applicable in every season, for the damage caused by time can outweigh the benefit gained from cauterization. Especially if cauterization is for essential, powerful, urgent pain that cannot be delayed, out of fear that the delay will lead to greater harm than the pain itself, which follows from the damage caused by time.
Do not be led astray, my sons, by the misconceptions of the general public and ignorant physicians, who believe that burning (cauterization) which heals a certain illness will never recur and make it a permanent solution.
And the matter is not as they thought, for cauterization is not like a remedy that alters the humor and dries up the moistures that cause pain.
Cauterization, however, is superior to remedies in terms of its swift success, powerful effect, and severe authority.
The illness may recur at some point in time, depending on the patient's humor, the strength and severity of their condition, and what is present in their body from the accumulation of impurities and neglect of self-care through diet and other causes.
Except in cases where the illness is mild and present in a body part with few impurities or moistures. Such as cauterizing a tooth for pain, it may not recur and this can be considered minimal.
And the statement of the general public that cauterization is 'the end of medicine' is incorrect, as they believe that no treatment, whether medicinal or otherwise, can be effective after cauterization, while in fact, it is not so.
And indeed, the cauterization is the last resort in medicine; for when we employ various remedies for a disease and they prove ineffective, then we use cauterization, which if successful, becomes the last remedy. Not as the common understanding or the ignorance of many uneducated doctors implies.
The ancients mentioned that gold cauterization is better than iron cauterization, as they stated, due to the moderation and noble nature of gold, and said that it does not cause pus formation in the site of cauterization, which is not entirely accurate.
Because I have tried it and found that it only works in some bodies, and cauterization with gold is better and more preferable than with iron, as they said, except that if you heat the iron in the fire, you will not be able to determine when it reaches the desired temperature due to the red color of gold.
Because it is quickly cooled down, and if you add more heat during fever, it melts in the fire and mixes, causing the practitioner to be occupied with that. Therefore, cauterization with iron has become for us faster and closer to the correct practice, God willing.
I have organized this chapter on cauterization into sections, from the head to the foot, in order to make it easy for the student to access what they need, God willing.
Chapter One.
In the treatment of the head, there is one basic operation.
This operation is effective against the moisture and cold that overwhelm the brain, causing headaches, excessive discharge from the head to the eyes and ears, excess sleep, toothaches, and sore throats. In general, it is useful for any illness that arises from coldness, such as paralysis, seizures, apoplexy, and other similar conditions.
The procedure involves first ordering the patient to induce vomiting with a purgative that clears the head, for three or four nights, depending on the patient's strength, age, and general health.
Then, he is instructed to shave his head with a razor and sit between your hands, with his hands on his chest.
Then, you place the palm of your hand on his nose, between his eyes, and where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then, hold the oil-soaked poultice as follows: ... (image or description of the poultice)
If the bone exposes itself to the extent of a tooth's root or a grain of coriander, raise your hand. Otherwise, if it cools, use the same iron or another one. Until you see what was mentioned to you about the bone, take some salt and dissolve it in water, then dip a cotton in it and place it on the area, leaving it for three days.
Then, take a cotton dipped in lard and leave it on the area until the blister from the fire disappears, then treat it with the quadruple ointment until it heals, God willing.
They say that the wound, the longer it remains open, the more pus it produces, which is better and more beneficial.
Some mention that the skin should be burned to the bone with a hot iron, until some of the bone's thickness is burnt, then the burnt part of the bone should be removed and treated.
Others say that the burning should be excessive, affecting the bone strongly enough for it to fall out like amber or a small seed, and they claim that this releases fumes from the head, keeping the wound open for a long time.
Then, it should be treated until it heals, and I do not at all recommend these types of burns, except in some cases and on risky grounds. Leaving it alone is better, if it does not heal, as the head weakens when its natural connection is broken, as we have observed in other parts of the body.
Especially when the patient's head is naturally weak, the first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion, and it is this that I have used and recommend.
The Second Chapter.
On the Head as Well.
If a chronic pain occurs in the head and persists for a long time, and treatments such as cauterization, cupping, poultices, snuff, ointments, and bandages have been tried without effect (especially if the patient has already undergone the described single cauterization without success), then consider the following. If the patient's head is strong in structure naturally and is not weak, and they experience severe cold, then cauterize a second time, slightly above the first one. Then cauterize each of the patient's temples until the skin thickness decreases and reveals the desired amount of bone. Cauterize the patient's head at the back, in the area known as the occipital bone, and be gentle with your hand to avoid exposing the bone. The patient will experience severe pain from this cauterization, unlike any other pain caused by the head's cauterizations. I will mention this particular cauterization in its proper place.
The iron used to treat the horns of the head and its back should be softer than the iron used for the middle of the head, and this is its form.
Chapter III
In the treatment of a non-chronic concussion.
If a fracture of the head causes pain with a headache that radiates to the eye, and the patient vomits, then the patient should be treated with purifying drugs for the head and all other treatments mentioned in the classification of diseases. If this does not work, there are two faces (or methods) of application. Either with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
As for the burning medicine, take a single clove of garlic, peel it and cut off both ends, then with a broad scalpel make a cut in the place of the headache on the temple, creating a space beneath the skin that fits the garlic clove.
It should be inserted beneath the skin until it disappears, then secured tightly with bandages and left for about fifteen hours. Afterwards, loosen and remove the garlic and leave the wound to heal for two or three days before dressing it.
On it, apply a cotton ball soaked in butter until the area discharges, then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you prefer, you can do this with some burning drugs as mentioned in the eighteenth article on burning drugs.
As for burning with iron, it is done in this manner: heat the iron with this shape.
It is called the nail iron because its head resembles a nail with some concavity and a small protrusion in the center.
Then place it on the affected area and hold your hand while you rotate the iron slightly, so that the amount burned is one-and-a-half times the thickness of the skin. Lift your hand to avoid burning the artery below, which could cause bleeding, then soak a cotton ball in salt water and place it on the area, leaving it for three days. Then apply the cotton ball soaked in butter, followed by treatment with ointment until it heals, God willing.
If you prefer, cauterize this wound with the sharp edge of the iron and be careful not to cut the artery in this acute wound, especially.
Chapter IV.
In chronic sisterhood.
If the treatment for phlegmon (chronic inflammation) as described above and as outlined in the classifications of diseases is ineffective, and one observes a condition that cannot be cured by the initial application of heat or by fire, then... Then, the surgical scalpel should be heated until it turns white, after which a line of about half a finger length is marked on it and drawn along the affected area. Leave it once and tighten your grip to sever the artery and reach towards the bone.
However, you must be cautious to avoid damaging the jaw joint's movement during chewing, which could burn the muscle or nerve controlling it, causing spasms.
And be careful and watchful for the blood loss from the artery you severed, as its vessels (the "garres" mentioned) are vulnerable. Especially for those who are unfamiliar with the procedure, it is best to abstain from acting if it's not a proven path, leaving the task to experts.
Further details regarding the treatment of spontaneous bleeding from an artery will be mentioned in its appropriate section in this book, God willing. If you observe a condition that cannot be cured by this application of heat, then...
And I saw the sick body susceptible to a cauterization in the middle of the head as we described, and treated the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you prefer, you can use the cauterization that we mentioned in the chapter on arterial bleeding with a two-bladed iron, for it is a better and more effective cauterization.
Chapter V.
On the treatment of ear pain.
If ear pain occurs due to cold and is treated with laxatives and other remedies mentioned in the classifications, but does not alleviate, then apply the iron, known as the point, in the following manner.
Then, after heating it, apply the iron around the entire ear, or both ears if the pain is in both of them, by moving it circularly. Move the cauterization instrument slightly away from the ear's base after marking the point with ink, and make the cauterization in each ear about ten points, or as needed, then treat the wounds until they heal, God willing.
Chapter VI.
In the name of strength.
The strength that is treated with cautery is of the type that occurs from phlegm, as I mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
And one should avoid cautery of the type that occurs from dryness and nerve spasms.
When cautery with arrows, poultices, and gargles does not cure this type of strength, the patient should be cauterized three times. One at the origin of the ear, the second slightly lower on his cheekbone, and the third at the junction of his lips.
And make your cautery from the opposite side of the afflicted area, for relaxation occurs only on the side that appears healthy.
The image of cautery is to make a single cauterization opposite the upper corner of the ear, slightly below the forehead, and another in the temple, with a length equal to that of the thumb.
Lower your cautery hand to burn at a depth of half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cautery, which is a type of scalpel as described earlier, but slightly milder. The knife should have a slight excess of thickness, then treat the wound as mentioned earlier until it heals, God willing.
Chapter VII
In chronic sores.
If the sore becomes chronic and treated as mentioned, but the treatment is ineffective and there is no fever, apply four cauterizations. Apply a cauterization to each horn of the head, as mentioned, and another in the middle of the head, as well as a cauterization at the back of the head, following the same method.
Apply the cauterization as mentioned, and you may also apply a cauterization on the stomach's opening, which is more effective, then treat as previously described.
Chapter VIII
On amnesia.
The patient suffering from amnesia should be given drinks to clear the lungs and cerebrospinal fluid, as well as herbs that purify the brain.
Then he shaves his whole head, and a poultice of mustard, as described in the article on poultices, is applied to his back. It is carried several times, for it is a form of cauterization.
And do this in the same order mentioned there, for if he recovers from it, otherwise, he will receive three cauterizations across the back of his head, aligned from the top of his head to the base of his neck.
And between each cauterization, make it the thickness of a finger. Then treat the cauterization with the remedies mentioned previously. If you wish to add more and the patient can withstand it, cauterize the middle one.
If you wish to add more, cauterize both ears, and treat it until he recovers, and the iron should be olive-shaped, as described previously.
Chapter IX.
For treating stroke and relaxing the entire body.
One should begin by cleansing the head with lye and what we have mentioned, then shave the patient's head, and cauterize it with one burn in the middle of the head, and one on each ear, and one on the back of the head.
Three on the neck vertebrae, if in need for treating a condition that requires more, and the patient is able to withstand it and the disease is strong and persistent, then apply four burns on the vertebrae of his back. And continue with the burn until most of the skin is burnt away, then raise your hand and treat it as mentioned earlier, so that it heals, God willing. The iron should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
On convulsions.
The convulsion should be burned only in the case of a seizure caused by phlegm. First, the brain should be purified with powerful purgatives and other treatments mentioned in the classifications, if the patient is elderly and able to tolerate medications.
However, if it is a child who cannot tolerate medications, then use gargles and a purifying mouthwash for the brain several days in advance, along with improving his diet.
Then he should shave his head, and apply one burn in the middle of the head as mentioned earlier, and another burn at the back. And on each side of his head, make a burn. If the patient is strong and can endure it, apply the burns mentioned for the one suffering from paralysis, relaxing the body along the cervical and dorsal vertebrae. The iron should be olive-shaped, as described previously.
If the patient is a child, make the iron gentle in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On the treatment of melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is putrefied humors and thick phlegm, apply the burns as mentioned for the one suffering from paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is a tendency towards blackness and the patient's body is filled with putrefied humors, cleanse his brain as described in the previous division.
Then shave the patient's head. Make a compact cake from linen fiber in the shape of a circle, and place it in the middle of his head while he sits cross-legged. Take one pound of ancient sheep fat.
Then warm it over a moderate fire until the finger can tolerate it when inserted. Pour it into the circular mold at the center of his head and leave it until it cools. Perform this procedure on the patient once a week, along with all other beneficial measures, until he recovers, God willing.
If you wish to cauterize, apply small, frequent drops without holding the iron with your hand, but rather using a gentle touch. This type of cautery moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cotton swab soaked in lard or chicken fat.
Chapter Twelve.
In the cauterization of water that falls into the eye.
If you notice the onset of water falling into the eye, as described in the previous sections, promptly treat the patient to clear the eye and protect it from all moisture, and sweat them in a hot bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave their head and cauterize it once in the middle of the head, and apply two cauterizations on each side (if water is falling into both eyes), or one cauterization on the single affected eye.
Then cut all the veins and arteries beneath the skin using the iron, making the cauterizations long and wide across both sides of the temples, taking care not to cause blood loss.
If you notice any of it, cut it off at the base with any treatment available to you, and we will discuss wisdom in clamping and cutting arteries while taking care not to cause bleeding.
A severe burn may occur on the nape, between two vertebrae.
Chapter XIII.
In the treatment of chronic tear discharge.
If the tear discharge from the eye is chronic and persistent, originating from the veins and arteries on the surface of the head, and you have determined that this is due to a thick, cold humor in the lymphatic vessels, then apply the cauterization described earlier to the affected area. Cauterize the middle of the head, two on each side of the face (on the temple), and two under the vertebrae at the nape. If necessary, add a further cauterization on either side of the eye, just above the eyebrow, using a small iron.
Chapter XIV.
On cauterizing the nose's mucous membrane.
If the treatment mentioned in the previous sections does not improve the condition, then proceed as follows: after three nights of treatment, shave the patient's head and cauterize the central area with a hot iron (zaitoni). Then apply the iron (koyat) twice over the eyebrows, slightly below the hairline, and take care not to cut the artery, as shown in this illustration of the iron.
**Chapter XV**
In the treatment of eyelid drooping.
If the eyelid droops due to disease or moisture, apply one ironing with this semicircular iron.
And if you wish, apply them above the eyebrows slightly, with two irons on each side, spaced apart from the temples, and make each ironing as long as the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the ironing, but apply it to the extent that one-third of the skin is burned, as shown in this illustration of the iron.
**Chapter XVI**
In the ironing of the eyelid.
If the eyelids turn inward, the treatment involves two types of ironing. Either by burning with fire or by burning medicine, if the treatment is by burning with fire, then order the patient to remove his beard, if he is one who pulls it, until it grows long and even.
If you trim it while it is still young, bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until it grows and becomes even. Once it has grown and straightened, place the patient's head in your lap, then draw on the eyelid with ink a mark shaped like an Asian paper fan.
The mark should begin near the eyelashes, then place a cotton ball soaked in white of egg or saliva under the eyelid, and heat it with an iron whose shape is as shown.
Then, in several applications, heat the mark you have drawn with the iron little by little until the surface of the skin burns and takes the shape of a paper fan, so that its entire appearance is like that of a paper fan. The sign of the success of your work is to see the eyelids tightened and the hair raised from the eye's flesh.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days, after which apply a cotton ball with butter to remove the stubble and treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If any hair returns after some time and the eyelid relaxes.
Then, apply the cauterizing agent to that same area as you did initially. If it is a hair in the lower eyelid, remove it until it returns to its natural position without damaging the eye, and for cauterization with a burning medicine, order the patient to leave the lashes intact until they grow long and even.
Then, make a paper from the bark of the *Aspa* tree and take from common soap and unquenched lime, each weighing a drachm or its equivalent, grind them all very well with a mortar, doing this quickly to avoid drying out. Then, spread it on the paper made from the bark of the *Aspa* tree, in the shape of an eyelid, and place a cotton ball soaked in egg white under the eye, while the patient lies on your lap.
Place your index finger on the application, gently compressing it, and move it as the patient feels the burn of the medicine, as they will experience a sensation like fire. As long as they feel the burn, leave the medicine and continue to move your finger. Once the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
Look, if you see the eyelid has lifted up as it does when exposed to fire or cut, then proceed. Otherwise, reapply the medicine to the area that was not affected and did not turn black until your work is complete and the eye is restored. Then, apply a cotton ball greased with butter over the burned area until the sensation of burning disappears.
Then treat it with an ointment of nettle or otherwise until it heals. In your work, be extremely careful not to let any of the medicine fall into the eye, for if the eyelid relaxes after several days and there is a fear of hair in the eye.
Then prepare the medicine for that relaxed part of the eyelid, especially as you did at first, and treat it until it heals. This is an image of the ase (a plant) leaf.
Know that people's eyes may vary in size and shape, so adjust your treatment accordingly, for the path to correctness is not hidden from those who have experience in this craft.
Chapter XVII.
On the treatment of the naphthal (a type of infection) that appears in the eye socket.
If you treat the naphthal with what we mentioned in the classifications of diseases and it does not respond to your treatment, then it should be cauterized in this manner.
Command the patient to place his head on your lap and have a servant hold his head with both hands, not moving or striking it.
Then place a wet cotton ball in egg whites or in the saliva of an ox, and heat the iron with this shape.
It is hollow in the shape of a bird's feather tube, with one end for cauterization and if you prefer, the other end can be perforated or solid like a plug, except that this hollow space is better for your work, God willing.
Then, if the abscess is open, squeeze it and remove the pus and dry it; or if it is closed, open it, extract the pus, then place the hot iron on it and hold it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
And move your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose when cauterizing, so that your hand does not err or the patient becomes anxious, causing the iron to fall into the eye and damage it; if it reaches the bone with the first cauterization, otherwise, reheat the iron a second time if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball with butter and treat it with a drying ointment until it heals; if it does not heal in forty days, apply a sharp, corrosive medicine to expose the bone and remove it as mentioned later in its section, God willing.
Another method for cauterizing abscesses, mentioned by some early scholars, involves incising the abscess and inserting a thin tube (this is its shape) into the same incision.
Pour this into a container with a capacity equal to that of a dirham, and melt the lead within it. Hold the crucible firmly with your hand, ensuring it does not move, lest the melted lead spill into the patient's eye. It is necessary to place a wet cotton ball over the patient's eye, either in egg white or water.
Lead, when applied to the site of a fistula, burns and heals it in a miraculous manner. If the fistula is healed through the mentioned burning and treatment, then there is no alternative but to use nasal surgery to restore the fistula to its natural course within the nose, as will be explained in detail in its designated section.
Chapter XVIII
On burning the fissure of the lip.
Often, a fissure occurs in the lip, known as the "hairline," especially on the lips of young boys. If this fissure is treated using the methods mentioned in the previous sections but does not heal, take a small, thin iron (as shown) and apply it to the affected area.
It should be shaped like the blade of a knife, then placed over the fissure while still hot, ensuring that the heat penetrates deeply into the fissure. Subsequently, it is treated with a poultice until healing occurs, God willing.
Chapter XIX.
On the treatment of the naphthic ulcer occurring in the mouth.
If a tumor appears in the gums, or on the soft palate, or at the base of the teeth, and it becomes ulcerated, and bursts, and pus flows, forming a fistula, and treatment does not take effect, then you should heat a poultice to fit over the fistula.
Then insert a protective cover into the fistula hole, hold on to it, and with the heated iron protected, reach its depth and end, and do this once or twice. Then treat it with the mentioned remedies until it heals, God willing.
If the material stops and it heals, otherwise, you must examine the place, and remove the rotten bone as mentioned in its section, God willing.
Chapter XX.
On the treatment of loose teeth and gums.
If the gums become loose due to moisture, and the teeth move, and treatment with medication does not help, then place the patient's head in your lap. Then heat a poultice that will be described later, after placing a tube on the tooth.
In this procedure, the iron is applied with a wheel and your hand presses lightly until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth. Then you raise your hand and repeat the application as desired. The patient should then fill their mouth with salt water.
And it is held for a time and then thrown, and the mobile tooth will be stabilized, the relaxed gums will tighten, and the corrupt moisture will dry up.
**Chapter 21.**
**On Treating Toothache.**
If the toothache is due to cold or has a worm and does not respond to drug treatment, there are two methods of application: either with butter or with fire.
As for the application with butter, one takes pure sheep butter, boils it in a cast-iron ladle or shell, then takes a cotton swab, wraps it around the end of a rod, immerses it in the hot butter, and applies it to the aching tooth with a wheel, holding it until it cools down.
Then repeat the application several times until the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth. If desired, one may dip a woolen swab or cotton in cold butter and apply it to the aching tooth, then place a heated iron on top to ensure the fire reaches the base of the tooth.
And as for burning it with fire, one should take a copper or iron pipe that has some thickness to prevent the heat of the fire from reaching the patient's mouth, then heat the iron until its shape is formed and place it on the tooth, holding the hand until the iron cools. He should do this several times.
The pain will go either that same day or the next, and after applying the burn, the patient should fill their mouth with good butter and hold it for a while before spitting it out, which is the shape of the iron.
One can burn with any side desired, according to what is possible, and this is the shape of the pipe.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On burning the pigs.
If the pigs are affected by phlegm and cold humors, and do not yield to treatment for maturation, and you wish to hasten their maturation, then heat the hollow iron with both ends as shown.
Let the smoke come out from the other end when burning, place it protectively on the same tumor once and again if needed, until it reaches the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, make the iron according to its size.
Then leave it for three days, and apply a cotton swab soaked in lard until the burnt area heals, then treat it with ointment and bandages until it is healed, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Three
On the treatment of hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If humors overwhelm the bronchial tubes, especially if accompanied by a cold temperament, the patient should first be purged with purgative drugs, then applied with heat to the throat at the lower point of the neck, near the base of the tongue.
Beware not to apply heat to the throat, but only to half of the skin, then apply another heat treatment at the joint of the neck, at its last vertebra, with a nail-like iron, in the manner previously described.
Then treat it as mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four
On the treatment of lung disease and coughing.

Then treat it with the methods mentioned until recovery is achieved. You can also make a cautery instrument with three prongs, as illustrated, to speed up the process, as you can apply three cauterizations in one go. This is the image of the cautery instrument.
Chapter Twenty-Fifth.
On the armpit cauterization.
If the head of the upper arm (humerus) becomes detached due to slippery moistures or does not become firmly attached when returned, making it a habit to dislodge upon even slight movement, as we have observed, then the joint should be reduced first. Then the patient should lie on their back or on their right side. Then raise the skin inside the armpit with the fingers of your left hand if the joint has dislocated inward.
Then heat the iron with two handles as shown here.
Then use the iron to burn the skin until it reaches the other side. The shape of the burn will be four burns, and it can also be burned with an iron having three handles, making the shape of the burn six burns, with the handles on the tender part of the wound.
And this is the image of the iron with three handles.
And sometimes one more can be added, making the burns eight. Then apply heated balls with salt and bind the patient still, without moving the limb for a while until it gains strength, although such dislocations usually occur downward.
Apply a single burn above the shoulder with a good nail or many burns that drip, as this strengthens the joint and removes moisture, allowing the patient to heal.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On the removal of the stomach
If, in the stomach, cold and excessive moisture occurs, to the extent that it alters its state, and numerous discharges (fluids) accumulate therein, and various treatments are applied without effect, the patient should lie on his back with his legs and arms extended, and then three burns (procedures) should be performed. A burn under the breast with a spoon-shaped tool, about the size of a finger, and three burns below on each side of the single burn, forming a triangular shape, and spacing them apart to prevent them from merging if they become inflamed, with a depth of two-thirds of the skin's thickness.
According to this image and this measure, without any excess.
And if you prefer, perform a single large burn in the middle of the stomach according to this image.
The tool used for this single burn, according to this image, is in the form of a circle.
The stomach may be burned with a point for those who are alarmed by this burn, which is to mark the stomach with dots using ink, then burn it with a pointed tool, and treat it with the previously mentioned remedies until healing occurs.
Chapter XXVII.
In the treatment of coldness of the liver.
If a pain in the liver is caused by coldness, moisture, or excessive heat, to the extent that it deviates from its natural condition and no improvement is observed after treating the patient as described in the preceding sections. The patient should lie on his back and have three burns made with a reed on the liver, below the ribcage where the elbow ends, in this exact shape and size.
After each burn, make a gap equal to the thickness of your finger, and make the burns straight along the body, without pressing the reed with your hand.
The amount burned should not exceed half the thickness of the skin. If the patient cannot stand, he should lie down with his legs extended and arms raised, following this image of the reed.
You can use a sharp reed to make these burns if you are skilled and careful, taking care not to burn the skin excessively, which could harm the stomach and reach the intestines, as the skin there is thinner. Be aware of this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight
In the treatment of a tumor of the liver with heat.
If a cyst in the liver presents itself, and you wish to determine whether it is a tumor in the liver flesh or in its capsule, then if it is within the liver flesh, the patient will experience a sense of weight and pain but without sharpness.
And if it is in the liver capsule, there will be a sharp pain with great difficulty in treatment; so the patient should lie on his back and then mark the location of the tumor with ink.
Then heat the iron, which is like a wedge, in the fire; this is its form. Apply it with a single burn to cauterize the skin entirely, and continue burning until you reach the capsule, so that all the pus is extracted. Then treat it as you would any cyst until healing occurs.
This type of burning should only be used by those who are well-versed in medicine and have practiced these procedures many times; otherwise, it is better to leave such actions to me.
Chapter Twenty-Nine
In the case of gangrene.
The earliest authorities mention the use of turpeth (a form of raw alum) in cases of gangrene, specifying that one should take a single, longer piece of dry turpeth, no thicker than a finger, immerse it in oil, and heat it over fire. Then apply a single burn between the clavicle and the neck, and two small burns below the jaw, slightly inclined towards the side under the beard, and two large burns above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
Apply two more burns between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined towards the back, a burn in the center of the chest, another one above the stomach, and three burns from behind, one between the shoulders and two on either side of the ribs below the burn between the clavicle and neck.
One must not insert the burn too deeply into the skin, but rather apply it superficially as a friction. Some early authorities mention that people used to employ an iron instrument shaped like a funnel, heating it and inserting it between the ribs until it reached the affected area.
The duration of this treatment, as we have mentioned, is in line with the size of the liver tumor. In this case of burning, there are risks; either the patient dies immediately, or a fistula may develop at the site, which cannot heal.
Chapter Thirty
On the spleen.

In the form of liver burns, with each burn spaced apart by the thickness of a finger or slightly more, and using the same technique as described for liver burns, without deep penetration. The patient is positioned lying on their back.
The other option involves making incisions in the armpit and lifting the skin opposite the spleen, where the patient's left armpit ends, and lifting the skin across the width of the body, so that the burns fall along the length of the body.
Then, insert the rods carefully until they pass through the skin from the other side, and remove the cauterization tool, leaving four burns, or, if you prefer, use the other tool with three prongs.
Then, treat the burn site after allowing it to drain pus for several days, as it is more effective than any other treatment mentioned.

Chapter 36.
On the removal of fistulas that occur in the buttocks and its surroundings.

Beware of burning a nerve, if present, or a substantial vein, if the fistula leads to the bladder or to the intestinal wall.

Chapter Forty-One.
Cauterizing the sciatic nerve.
If pain occurs in the hip joint due to cold and moisture, and the treatment mentioned in the sections does not work, leaving the condition unresolved over time, the patient should ease the heavy humors with putrid perfumes or sabra (a type of plant) or similar substances.
Then, apply the caustic to the affected area in two ways: either with burning drugs or with fire. The application with fire can be done in several ways, one of which is to burn three triangular incisions on the hip itself, as shown here.
It should have a suitable depth, and between each burn, the distance should be equal to the thickness of the finger. The cautery should be olive-shaped, and you can make one burn in the middle on the hip itself, which would be four burns. Alternatively, you can apply it in a circular motion as mentioned earlier for a single burn to dislocate the hip.
The circle should encompass the entire hip, burning all the skin thickness, this is the cauterization for sciatica when the pain is localized to the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh or leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, apply two burns on the thigh at the spot indicated by the patient as painful, and a single burn above the ankle with four fingers slightly towards the outer side. The cautery should be sword-shaped, and the depth of the burn should be equal to the skin thickness only.
If the patient indicates that the pain extends to the toes, apply cauterization at the indicated point three or four times or more if necessary. If the pain is below the knee and extends towards the leg, apply a single sword-shaped burn in that area.
Be cautious in all incisions to ensure that you do not cause the burning of a nerve or major artery, which could result in a severe injury or complication for the patient.
I have observed some cases where individuals, in their eagerness to burn above the ankle, have excessively burned the area, leading to a gangrenous condition that extends to the foot, causing it to rot completely. Consequently, severe diarrhea and death ensued. If the pain affects both sides, treat them in the same manner.
Some wise scholars have described a specific procedure for burning the hip, involving the creation of a metal cup with a diameter of approximately half a span (about 10-12 inches) at its mouth, slightly less than the width of a date palm kernel. Inside this primary cup, a smaller secondary cup is placed, followed by a third cup.
After placing each cup, there should be a space of about the width of a thumb between them. The cups are open on both sides and have a height of approximately one or two spans (around 5-10 inches). A handle made of secure iron is attached to these cups, as illustrated.
Then, heat the metal in a fire until it turns red and sparks. Place it on the hip of the patient, who should be lying on their right side. Burn three circular incisions at once with a hot iron, then leave it for three days, applying sesame oil as a dressing without covering the wound.
Many times, then treat it with a poultice until it heals. The author of this book states that this type of cauterization is rarely used due to its severity and the horror of its appearance, and there are few in Arabia who can bear it, except that it is effective cauterization for those who can endure it and suffer from it.
And as for cauterization with burning drugs, it is to make two half-circles, in which the door panel (reticule) is made of copper or iron.
It has a height of about two fingers or similar, and is open at the bottom and top, with some parts securely fastened together in this manner.
Then lower it onto the right hip of the patient, who is lying on their healthy side, securely fasten your hand, and pour between the circles water that has been slightly cooled over a short period of time from fire. Hold it for about an hour, and the patient will feel a pain similar to that of fire.
Until the pain subsides, then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe away the sharp water from the hip completely with clean water. Leave it for three days, applying butter to it until the burn's blackness disappears, and let it rest for several more days; this is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain spreads to the thigh or leg, make a mold for it to hold water in the same way as you did for the hip, and this is the description of the sharp water which I have discussed in the article on preparing medicines.
Take part of the saltpetre and slaked lime, mix them together in a new pot with a small hole at the bottom, and place another pot, sealed below, inside it.
Pour fresh water over the saltpetre and slaked lime, squeezing them well with your hand, and leave the pot until the sharp water has settled at the bottom of the sealed pot. Then gather all the water and pour it over new saltpetre and slaked lime.
It becomes then very sharp, acting in many medical tasks and in healing other members, as it behaves like fire itself. Among the remedies is what is used to burn the hip, such as thapsus, honey from blackberry, and lime mixed with soap.
Galen tells a story of an ancient man who had a remedy for hip pain, sciatica, and bone stiffness, claiming that it is so powerful that no other treatment is needed, and it heals in just one application.
Even if he is carried into the bath and comes out having healed, one should use green comfrey root if available; if not, use recent dry comfrey root, finely pounded with some fat and applied to the hip where the pain is or on the leg or thigh.
And bind it and leave it for three hours or until the patient feels the burning has subsided; then, place him in the bath. If his body is moistened, submerge him in the tub. The pain will go away and he will heal, God willing. If he does not heal, repeat the bandage after ten days.
He will heal, God willing. Note that no one should use heat with these medications unless the body has been emptied first. Dioscorides mentioned that goat's sweat, when applied with heat, is beneficial for sciatica.
The heat should be applied as follows: take wool soaked in oil and place it on the deep area between the thumb and wrist, closer to the wrist. Then, use a dry goat's sweat.
Ignite it over fire until it becomes a hot coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it cools. Take another one and repeat this process until sensation reaches the hip through the upper arm, and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of heat treatment is called "kay al-ba'ari.

Chapter Forty-Five.
On the poultice for hernias.

And be careful with your effort to prevent the intestine from protruding while you apply heat, otherwise he may suffer death or severe harm. Chapter LXXIII
On the types of nails, reversed and unreversed.
This ailment often occurs in the soles of the feet, a rough substance that hurts the foot, and it can be treated by fire or sharp water.

It often occurs on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet. One should take a tube of copper or iron or falcon's feather and lower it onto the nail or wart, then pour a small amount of sharp water into the tube.
