Then leave it, for the nail will come out with its roots. You should do this to each one, until you have removed all of them from the body. Then treat the sites after they have been freed, using the appropriate ointments that promote tissue regeneration.
Chapter XLIV.
On the Pince.
If a pince (a tool for holding or extracting) becomes frozen due to cold, or if there is an inflammation of the nerve, or a quartan fever (a type of intermittent fever), or any other condition, one should apply four or five burns with a hot iron on the spine, between each vertebra, and also on the chest. And apply a burn with an olive-shaped iron on the stomach, for the pince will calm down and expedite the maturation of the cold illness.
Chapter LXV.
On the Pince for Treating Abscesses on the Body.
When ugly boils appear in the body caused by cold, thick, corrupt materials, it is advisable at first sight to apply a gentle burn to each boil using a stick of fire, or the end of a long turmerik root, or a lens-shaped iron.
The boils, when first appearing, should be treated in this manner so that they do not increase or spread, and the healing power is exhausted, leaving the patient relieved, except that this should follow draining the patient through phlebotomy.
Chapter LVI.
In applying burns to stop bleeding from an artery.
Bleeding from an artery that has been severed often occurs when a wound is inflicted either externally or during the removal of a tumor, or when a member is burned and the like, making it difficult to cut.
If this happens to anyone, quickly place your hand over the artery's mouth, put your index finger on it and tighten it so that the blood is contained under your finger and does not escape, then take several small and large hot iron tools (possibly calamine irons), and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the place where the artery burst, and lower the iron on the same vein after quickly removing your finger. Hold the iron until the bleeding stops.
If, when lifting the finger from the artery's orifice and applying the iron, it extinguishes, take another iron with haste from among those prepared in the fire, and continue to do so one after another until the blood stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve, for if there is an opening, it will cause the patient further harm. Know that if the artery bleeds, it cannot be cut, especially if it is a large artery, unless by one of four methods: either by burning as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already separated. If, after amputation, the ends contract and the blood stops, either tie it tightly with threads or apply drugs that stop the blood flow and secure it firmly with bandages.
As for those who attempt to stop the bleeding with bindings, or by tightening rags, or placing burnishing objects, etc., such methods will not stop it at all, except rarely, if one does not have a doctor or medicine. In such a case, they should immediately place their index finger over the wound's opening as we have described.
Tighten it firmly until the blood stops, flows from above the wound, and onto the artery and finger, never removing it with cold water until the blood freezes and thickens and cuts off. During this time, examine what is needed for burning or medicine.
The Second Chapter.
In incisions, punctures, lances, and various surgical procedures.
As we mentioned in the first chapter, every disease that can be treated with fire and burning medicines, its causes, symptoms, instruments, and types of cauterization, I have discussed these in detail from ancient times to the present.
And I will follow the same approach in this chapter to make it easier for the student to understand their requirements.
Before I begin, you should know, O my children, that this chapter contains risks beyond what is found in the first chapter on cauterization.
For this reason, caution should be stronger in this chapter, as the work involves risks that can lead to blood loss, which is the source of life. This can occur when opening a vein, incising a tumor, draining pus, treating surgery, or extracting an arrow, among other procedures, all of which carry danger and fear, and often result in death.
I advise you to avoid anything that raises doubt, for in this profession you will encounter various types of people suffering from different ailments. Some may have endured their illness and become indifferent to death.
In cases where the patient's suffering is severe, his condition critical, and the disease persistent, indicating a fatal outcome, some may offer you their wealth in exchange for health, seeing their illness as an enemy. However, you must never seek help from such individuals.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and diligence, and do not undertake any of these actions unless you have certain knowledge that confirms the favorable outcome, utilizing all means of knowledge and warning in the treatment of your patients to ensure their safety.
For in this, you will find assistance in gaining praise, glory, and gratitude. May God guide you, O my sons, and grant you wisdom and success, for it is all in His hands, there is no god but Him.
I have organized this chapter into sections, following the order of the previous chapter on cauterization from the tip to the base, to make it easier for you to find what you need from it, God willing.
Chapter One.
On the treatment of water retention in the heads of children.
This condition often afflicts children at birth when the midwife exerts excessive force on the baby's head, or it may arise from an unseen underlying issue.
And I have not seen this condition except in boys, and all of them whom I have seen, death comes to them swiftly. Therefore, I have decided to abandon this treatment, for I have seen a boy whose head has filled with water, and the head increases in size every day. Even the boy could not sit because of the enormity of his head and the increasing moisture, until he perished.
And this moisture either collects between the skin and bone or under the bone on the peritoneum. The procedure is, if the moisture is between the skin and bone and the tumor is small, a single cut should be made across the middle of the head, about two fingers in length, to allow the moisture to drain. This is an image of the scalpel.
If the moisture is excessive and the tumor is large, make two intersecting cuts as shown:
And if the moisture is under the bone and you see the stitches of the head opening on all sides, and when you press it with your hand, the water sinks inwards and this is not hidden from you, then you should make three cuts across the middle of the head, as shown.
After the cuts, all moisture is drained, then the incisions are closed with bandages and tourniquets. Subsequently, compresses of wine and oil are applied for five days. Then the bindings are loosened and the wound is treated with twists and ointments, without neglecting to maintain a moderate tension on the head. The patient should be fed with dry, slightly moist food.
Until the member strengthens and heals, another method of incision is to observe where the bone of the tumor appears and the collection of fluid, as it may be more in the back of the head or at the front, or on the right or left side.
You intend by the incision where the tumor appears to you and the accumulation of fluid, so make an incision as possible without cutting an artery, which could cause bleeding leading to the patient's death due to loss of fluids.
Chapter II.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears, known as the sensitive ones.
When a sudden descent occurs to the eyes or chest, and this condition persists despite the use of drugs, the most effective treatment is to cut these two arteries.
Therefore, if you wish to cut them, you should shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to expose the artery. Tighten the patient's robe around their neck, look for the pulse, and locate the two depressions behind the ears, where they are most clearly visible in most people.
Then, mark them with ink and cut them with a sharp knife all the way to the bone, making a cut across the head's width. If you prefer, insert the knife from beneath the artery and move it upwards by cutting, ensuring a length of about two closed fingers.
If the sweat stops, the blood will flow out in a pulsating manner, advancing and receding repeatedly. If the artery is not visible to the touch, it should be located behind the ear, approximately three fingers from the surface, then marked with ink and incised into the bone.
The blood will flow in six waves, or perhaps more or less, depending on the patient's condition and the fullness of their arteries. Then, examine the wound. If there is any remaining tissue attached to the bone, cut it away.
To prevent a hot swelling, apply pressure with a cloth thread, then treat the wound with ointments until it heals.
Chapter III
On the arteries in the temple.
If a person suffers from chronic phlegm or acute attacks of fever in the muscles of the temple, or severe chronic headaches, and has undergone various medical treatments without improvement, we have found that cutting (or cauterizing) the arteries in the temple is effective for these conditions.
The procedure for treating the condition involves shaving the hair from the temple, focusing on the visible artery in the temple area. By observing its pulse, you can determine the state of the artery, and it rarely remains hidden except in unusual cases.
If the cold is severe and you cannot feel the pulse, the patient should tighten their neck with their cloak. Then you should rub the area with a cloth or apply hot water to make the artery clearly visible. Then take the scalpel whose image this is. Then use it to carefully peel the skin until you reach the artery. Then cast a hook into it and pull it up to extract it from the skin, freeing it from all adhesions below. If the artery is thin, twist it with the tip of the hook. Then cut from it a part, according to the distance between its ends and its contraction, without causing bleeding. For if it is not amputated or severed, the blood will not flow at all. Then drain the blood from six to three openings.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong, doubled thread. The thread should be either linen or string from a lute, so that putrefaction does not reach it before the wound heals, causing bleeding.
Then cut away the remainder between the ties. You should do this at that moment or later, and if you wish, brand it to the bone with a skewering iron, so its ends are severed, serving the same purpose or even better as described.
Unless the patient suffers from fever or mood alterations, as burning (from heat) is beneficial in expelling humors, it is the most useful course of action. After draining the veins, the wound should be filled with old cotton and bandaged tightly. Afterwards, dry medicines that nourish the flesh and twisting should be applied until healing occurs, God willing.
If, while working, bleeding occurs from an artery, immediately cut it either by burning or by filling the wound with wax and pressing firmly until the blood stops. If nothing is at hand, put your finger in the wound until the blood clots.
If the wound is washed with very cold water to calm the inflammation, and bandaged as necessary, one of the easiest ways to drain a vein is to burn the sweat with this two-bladed iron, after marking the locations with dye.
Remove it with a strong force until it reaches the bone, and cut the vein at two points to separate its ends; for this burning will never cause it to heal. This is the image of the iron.
It (the iron) should have two sharp blades, resembling small pincers, but less acute than a knife. For if they are as sharp as a knife, cold will come to them more quickly, and the flesh will not be cut off as swiftly. If there is some thickness in them, it will grip well against hot fire.
And I cut the flesh swiftly, and this action is better than every other, lighter, and easier, and let it be between the two knives as thick as a finger.
Chapter IV.
In the treatment of continuous, hot tear drainage into the eyes.
If the tears are continuous, and the drainage is from the veins on the skull from outside, and no benefit is derived from medical treatments with drugs, and you observe that the patient's face has turned red. He feels a tingling on his forehead, similar to the sensation of ants crawling, and his eyes are weak, moist, with dried-up eyelids, caused by the intense tearing. The initial treatment for this condition, known as "al-Siif" (the sword), involves commanding the patient to shave the hair on their forehead.
Then, make three parallel cuts on the forehead, each about the length of two fingers, aligned with the bridge of the nose in the center of the forehead, the second slightly away from the muscle movement in the temple, and the third on the other side.
Be careful not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from the jaw's connection. After each cut, ensure a distance of three fingers (with hands clenched) and have sponge pieces or dry cloth ready to absorb the blood.
Then enter the sharp scalpel with both ends, whose image is this. From the wound next to the temple to the middle wound, and by this all the skin between the two wounds, along with the peritoneum on the bone, is peeled away, and then you do the same from the middle wound to the third wound, then remove this scalpel. You also insert into the first wound another tool called a "scie" (scalpel) with one sharp end and the other smooth and not sharp, and this is its image: .
You position its sharp side towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle wound, cutting all vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without reaching the surface of the skin.
Then you do the same in the middle wound to the other, after allowing enough blood to flow, squeeze the areas of clotted blood, and then insert a cotton thread in each wound, placing a bandage soaked in a mixture of wine and oil or vinegar and oil.
To prevent a hot swelling, and on the third day, loosen the bandage and use plenty of hot compresses with warm water, then treat it with a paste of basil and rose fat and all other remedies used for wounds until healing occurs, God willing.
Chapter Five.
In the treatment of tears and discharge into the eyes from within the head.
When a person experiences frequent, severe, persistent epidaemics (outbreaks) that cause the eyes to appear emaciated and small, with weakened vision and irritated eyelids, leading to the loss of eyelashes. There is a sharp, painful sensation in the depth of the head, accompanied by frequent sneezing, indicating that these symptoms arise from numerous deep veins. The best treatment for this condition involves instructing the patient to shave their forehead and then make a single cut through the middle of the forehead.
There is a sharp, painful sensation in the depth of the head, accompanied by frequent sneezing, indicating that these symptoms arise from numerous deep veins. The best treatment for this condition involves instructing the patient to shave their forehead and then make a single cut through the middle of the forehead. Alternatively, make the cut slightly higher and extend it from one side of the temple (left) to the other (right), reaching the bone. Remove your hand away from the moving muscles of the temples when chewing, ensuring the cut reaches the bone and all blood is absorbed using a sponge.
Separate the edges of the cut using old cotton or a twist of linen, then secure it with bandages. Soak the bandages in a solution of wine and oil to prevent swelling. If, after healing, you notice that the swelling has subsided, but the bone is exposed, gently rub the bone until flesh begins to re-form.
Then treat it with the drying plan that makes flesh grow, such as taking two parts of flour from wheat and four parts of colphonia, prepare a paste from them, and use it to heal the flesh in such wounds.
Chapter VI.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
All that falls into the ear is one of four types, either a metallic stone or semi-stone such as iron and glass, or a plant seed like lentils and nuts, or something liquid such as water, vinegar, and the like.
And if it is an animal, when a stone or the kind of stone that does not increase in the ear falls into it, and you receive it with the ear towards the sun, then squeeze some violet or sage oil into it, and try to remove it by moving your head or sneezing. And plug the nostrils when sneezing comes, after placing a bandage around the ear with cloth or wool, and stretching the ear upwards. This treatment often removes the stone, but if it does not, try to remove it gently using an instrument of this type.
If it does not come out with the instrument, try a blind hook made of delicate wire with little curvature. If that does not work, make a tube from copper and insert its tip into the ear hole carefully.
Seal the area around the tube with softened pitch mixed with fat, so that the wind has no other path but through the tube. Then draw it strongly and frequently with your breath, and often it will come out. If it does not come out as described, take a small amount of gum from the Nabatean or the prepared gum used to capture birds, and place it at the end of the rod.
After wrapping it tightly with a cotton, gently insert it into the ear hole once the ear is dry. If it does not come out as described, hurry to make an incision before the hot swelling or spasm occurs.
The procedure involves first draining the patient's eardrum of fluid, if necessary, and then sitting the patient between your hands, turning their ear upwards, and making a small incision at the base of the ear, just above the cartilage.
The incision should be in the shape of a crescent, extending until the stone is reached, at which point it can be removed using appropriate tools. Then, quickly sew up the incision and treat the area to facilitate healing.
If the object falling into the ear is a small grain that swells and accumulates, attempt to remove it as described. If it does not come out, take a thin, delicate scalpel, as illustrated.
And try to cut that type of grain falling into the ear, but only do so if you are certain that the grain has been moistened by the steam of the ear, becoming many small pieces, and then remove them with a deaf hook or a delicate dry cloth or by sucking.
As we mentioned, it is easy to remove them. As for the water in the ear, the patient should first use a nosebleed device or something similar to fill their ear with cotton waste while lying on the affected ear, and if that removes the water.
Or, take many small, smooth stones and warm them slightly over fire. The patient should then insert one of these stones into the hole of their ear, bend on one leg in that direction, and hit the stone inside the ear with another stone.
They should continue this process with one stone after another until all the water is removed. Water may also be extracted using a reed or feather, inserting one end into the ear and heating the other end over fire until most of it burns away, then repeating with another feather.
Do this several times until all the water is removed or use a tube, as mentioned with the stone, to suck it out. As for removing any animal that may have entered the ear (such as a tick), treat it as described in the previous sections.
If the mass is large and visible, attempt to remove it using forceps and hooks; its extraction is easier than that of any other growth in the ear. However, if you have treated the earworm as mentioned in the section on parasites and it does not respond to your treatment.
You should examine the ear in sunlight; if you notice any worms, remove them using forceps or delicate hooks. If nothing is visible, use a tube with this shape.
The tube should be narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, with its thin end inserted into the ear as far as it will go comfortably. Then suck firmly on it several times to extract all the worms. If it does not come out, plug the opening around the tube with wax as mentioned earlier for the stone.
If none of the above methods work, use the parasites mentioned earlier, which the ancients have used to kill worms. You can find this in the section on parasites, and you should apply oils and medications to the ear using this instrument, as shown below:
As you can see, it is made of silver or brass, with a narrow bottom and a small hole, and a wider top. If you wish, the tube inside can be made of tight copper, or you can use a rod, wrapping a cotton ball tightly around its end.
Then, receive the fat or juice or whatever you wish from these medications in the tube while it is in the ear. Then, insert the curette with a cotton swab from above and squeeze it gently with your hand until the fat flows into the inner ear and the patient feels it.
Introduce the remedy into the ear, and if what you pour into it is warm from the fire, ensure that it is not too hot or too cold, for the ear cannot tolerate such extremes.
Chapter VII
Treatment for sudden blockage of the ear.
Some infants may be born with their eardrums unperforated.
Some people may also experience a blockage in the ear canal due to injury or flesh growth, and this blockage can be deep within the eardrum hole, making it invisible to the naked eye.
It may protrude slightly and be visible, but what is deep within the ear and invisible is usually difficult to treat. Therefore, for what is visible, place the patient's ear in the sun. Examine it, and if you see the obstruction is visible, open it with a delicate scalpel, and this is its appearance:
Its end should be slightly narrower, with both sides smooth to avoid damaging the ear. If the obstruction is due to a growth of flesh in the ear canal, hold it with a delicate hook and cut it carefully until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, take a mild, smooth curve and warm it slightly in the fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction along with the patient's hearing impairment, gently break off its tip.
Be careful not to injure the ear drum, then create a wick inside the ear of appropriate size, coated in Egyptian ointment, and do this for several days until you are certain the wound will not adhere or close. Alternatively, take a wick, soak it in water, and sprinkle crushed glass on it.
Use it if you see the ear has become swollen and hot. In this case, remove the wick and replace it with a new one coated in rose oil-based paste to alleviate the hot swelling, then treat it until it heals.
If there is bleeding, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, using all other treatments mentioned in the article on cataplasms to stop the bleeding.
Chapter VIII.
In the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry, and they should be seized with a file or hook and cut off at their bases with a scalpel. Then, a powdered glass should be applied to the site.
If blood flows from them, it is better to cauterize them with a gentle lens-shaped iron after cutting them off. They often return if any of their roots remain. If they are cauterized with fire, these roots are burned and they do not return.
Chapter IX.
In the treatment of cold that affects the eyelids.
It often occurs on the eyelids something that resembles cold in its severity and hardness.
It is therefore called "cold" (Ø¨Ø±Ø¯) due to the accumulation of thick moisture in both the upper and lower eyelids. If the cold (moisture) is prominent on the surface of the eyelid, moving to every side, then the treatment is straightforward; make a vertical cut (Ø´ÙÙ) across it.
Then, carefully peel it from all sides until you are free, then hang it on a hook and cut it. If you can only cut it after the eyelid has been entirely cut through, that will not harm the patient. If the incision is large, sew it together and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no need to worry, as the ointment will heal and seal it. If the eyelid is inclined inward towards the inner surface, turn the eyelid upside down and hang the lid with a hook without needing to cut, then trim it from all sides.
If the eyelid has been entirely cut through, it will not harm the patient. Then, wash the eye after cutting off the lid with salt water and treat the area to seal it until the patient heals.
Chapter X.
In the treatment of a fistula that occurs in the eyelid.
A fistula (or "Ø´Ø±ÙØ§Ù") is a flesh growth that occurs in the upper layers of the eyelid, most commonly affecting children. It can weigh on their eyes and cause discharge at night, preventing them from seeing sunlight. Due to the influx of tears.
Therefore, they rest and sleep with their faces down, and their eyelids are moistened by the eyebrows, with a swelling beneath them. The hernia is visible, and when you press on the area with your fingers, you can feel the hernia between them. The proper procedure is for the patient to lie with their head in your lap.
Then take a piece of linen cloth, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle with the wick around the hernia on all sides. Place it over the hernia and press from each side with your fingers to gather the hernia in the center of the circle.
Then, using a sharp scalpel, make a vertical cut through the moistened area, not larger than the cut made during phlebotomy. However, in terms of depth, you should cut through all the skin layers to reach the hernia.
In most cases, the hernia will protrude from the cut as a white piece of fat. Use a piece of cloth wrapped between your fingers to draw it out and rotate your hand to the right and left until it is free. Be careful not to increase the cut too much, or the scalpel may damage the eye.
If the hernia does not appear at first, you should slightly increase the cut gently until it emerges. Then, draw it out as we described and soak a cloth in vinegar and water, apply it to the area, and secure it with a bandage. Some people use salt as well.
He places it in the wound to dissolve what remains of that moisture, then treats it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs at the site, he treats it with soothing medicines until it heals.
**Chapter XI**
**On Types of Eye Twitching**
If hairs grow on the eyelid in an abnormal pattern beneath the natural lashes, and they become persistent, they damage the eye and cause various ailments such as constant tearing, drooping eyelids, white or cloudy appearance, and thickening, which can lead to visual impairment.
Eye twitching has four aspects: either by burning with fire, or with sharp drugs as mentioned in the chapter on burning, or through cutting and stitching, or with reeds, as I have previously explained.
You should place the patient's head in your lap and, with your left hand, turn the eyelid over. If it flips, otherwise, insert a needle with a thread underneath the eyelid and pass the needle through the thread from above. This should be done near the hair itself, and then pull the thread upwards to lift the lid.
Turn it with a spatula, then incise the inner eyelid without touching the surplus hair using an aseptic scalpel from the outer corner (margins) to the inner corner (margins). Then thread the needle and place a small pad of cotton or cloth underneath the eyelid. Finally, mark the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of an apricot leaf, but the shape should be adjusted to the extent required for raising the eyelid, as it may vary from person to person. Some may need a suitable amount cut from the eyelid according to its relaxation.
Some may need to cut less, adjusted to the eyelid's relaxation, then make incisions with a scalpel along the marked lines, starting from the outer corner of the eye (lamina) to the inner corner, with each incision close to the natural lashes, similar to the thickness of a thread.
Then, insert the forceps into one corner of the skin and completely peel it away. Then, sew the edges together with a needle and thin woolen thread, clean the blood, and attach any remaining threads to the eyebrows using sticky substances.
You may choose to do so or not, but the stitches and threads will remain for approximately three or four days, then you can treat it. Alternatively, if desired, you can leave the wound unstitched and treat it with drying and constricting agents, as the eyelid will raise when the wound heals and is sealed, and stitching is preferable.
This method of elevation (or 'tension') is mentioned in the earliest texts, and it is beneficial for the patient, being one of good practice with no danger. Another aspect of tension involves marking on the eyelid a shape similar to that of an apricot leaf, as we have described. Then raise the eyelid with three forceps, either spread apart or grouped in this manner:
Then cut the remaining eyelid with a small scissor, cutting it gently in this manner:
If you cannot hold the forceps, take a needle with a thread and insert it in the middle of the shape. Insert another thread near the larger eyelid and a third thread near the smaller eyelid. Then, gently gather the threads between your fingers and raise your hand slightly.
Lift the entire marked skin as we described, then sew together the edges of the wound and treat it until it heals. If a hot lump appears when you cut or slit, soothe it with cauterization and similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also experience a condition where its lashes turn inside out. In this case, one should use the techniques mentioned: cutting, stitching, and binding with fire or harsh medication, as these methods are more effective and less harmful for the patient compared to simply cutting and stitching it.
Binding with reed (or similar materials) is done as follows: turn the eyelid inside out, making a slit from within as described. Then create two thin strips or wooden splints, each as long as the eyelid and narrower than a surgical blade.
And I have borrowed from both sides of their extremities where the threads adhere, then gathered them gently on what remains of the eyelid and tightened the sutures from both sides tightly, leaving it for days; for the stretched skin will die and turn black and putrefy.
Until it falls on its own, if it is slow, cut it with a scalpel and then treat it until it heals; when the wound heals, the eyelid will rise and the eyelids of the eye will not be affected. And this is a picture of the sutures made in this shape and size, exactly the same length and width:.
Chapter XII.
Raising the protruding hair from the eye with the needle.
If it is a single hair or two, the work is to take a fine needle and insert a thin, smooth silk thread into it, then gather the ends and tie them with a very delicate knot. The length of the thread should be about an inch.
Then, you assemble another thinner thread in the bandage, shorter than the previous one, and tie its ends if desired. The patient should then lay their head on your lap, close to the sun so that the work can be clearly seen, as it is a delicate task. Then, insert the needle with the bandage into the root of the excess hair.
Perform this procedure on the eyelid with haste to extract it above the natural hair, then pull the thread over the ligature and insert that hair into the ligature, whether it is one, two, or three hairs at most.
Then, using your hand, you pull on the thread until the hair comes out with the ligature in the eyelid alongside the natural hair. If you pull the ligature without the hair coming out, you draw the ligature down using the thread it is attached to, until the ligature emerges from the upper hole.
Then, you put the hair back into the ligature and pull on it, until it rises in the upper hole and becomes clearly visible. At that point, release the ligature and thread, and close the eye, keeping it tightly closed for two or three days, until the hair takes its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, either add a longer natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows longer after some time, then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
On the treatment of the gap that occurs in the upper eyelid of the eye.
The conditions that are dealt with in this chapter are called "arnabiyah," and these conditions can be either natural or accidental. The accidental condition arises from the healing of a wound, tear, burn, or similar injuries.
The method of treatment involves incising the scab, separating its edges, and inserting a thread of linen between them, securing it until healing occurs. It is not advisable to use agents that dry and contract the skin in the treatment.
If you do this, the condition will return worse than before, and you should use agents that relax, such as horseradish, boiled in water with fennel seeds and flaxseed mucilage, mixed with a certain amount of fat, applied to the thread and used for treatment.
The essence of the treatment is to try by all means not to allow it to heal in the same shape as before. The procedure we have mentioned is only part of the repair; it does not aim to restore the area to its original state.
Chapter XIV
In the treatment of a wound on the lower eyelid.
The wound located on the lower eyelid, which is truly called a "shatra," can be either natural or accidental. The accidental condition arises from injuries such as a wound, tear, or burn.
The method of operation: Take a needle with a doubled thread and pierce it into the flesh, passing it through from the left nostril to the right nostril, so that the thread is at both ends of the flesh. Then, using the needle, pull the flesh up and cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what it should be, otherwise take a rod and place it on the site of the cut and turn the eyelid with it, and make two cuts inside the eyelid, and let the ends of the cuts meet at the angles of the incisions made until they form an angle. Until they meet, it will have the shape as follows:
It forms a Greek letter lambda, then remove that flesh as much as the sharp side is below the eyelid, then gather the scattered parts with two stitches using woolen thread, and treat it with the mentioned relaxing drugs and tourniquets until it heals.
If the opening occurred from a cut, stitching, or burning, then you should make a simple gap without affecting the hairs of the lid, and separate the lips with tourniquets as mentioned.
In summary, when treating an opening in the eyelid, whether from above or below, conduct the procedure according to what you find regarding the shape of the opening; it can vary significantly, and the surgeon must adapt their method as best as possible.
Until the shape returns to its natural state or approximates it, the same disease indicates what works for it and what tools are suitable in most cases.
Chapter XV.
In the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may prevent many people from moving their eyelid freely and impede it from performing its natural functions, requiring the insertion of a rod under the eyelid.
Then raise it above or extend it with a wire, and then cut the adhesion with a delicate knife that is not as sharp as surgical knives, but rather slightly blunt, to avoid disturbing the patient during the procedure and prevent the knife from causing harm to the eye with its sharpness, making the cut akin to peeling off the adhesion.
And expect that when the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion is completely healed, you should then pour salt water into the eye to wash it or apply a drop of ginger extract mixed with water, followed by separating the eyelid from the eye using a cotton thread.
Place a damp woolen cloth over the eye and, after three days, use the bandaged compresses until it heals.
Chapter XVI.
In cutting the tendon, care must be taken not to damage the surrounding flesh.
The tendon is of two types: either it is nervous, resembling a thin, rigid membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a hard, white substance that, when touched by metal or caught with a hook, easily breaks.
And if a hook does not adhere to it, both types begin from the larger joint and gradually become thicker, covering the eye and preventing light and eye movement. The method of cutting it is that the patient places his head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes, you raise the eyelid with your hand, catch the tendon with a slightly flexible hook, and pull it up. Then take a needle and insert a horse or cow hair and a strong thread into it, bend the tip of the needle slightly, and pierce it in the middle of the tendon.
And you pass the needle through it, binding the tendon with the thread, pulling it up, and peeling the side of the tendon next to the pupil with the hair, as if spreading it with the hair to the end. Then cut the rest at the base of the joint with a delicate scalpel or small scissors, leaving the natural eye flesh.
To prevent the constant flow of tears from the incision, the bleeder can be extended with a forceps alone or with a thread, as we have said, and then scraped with a smooth-ended scalpel, whose shape is depicted here: .
And be careful not to touch the scalpel to the cornea, which may cause a tear in the pupil, and then, after cutting, apply some powdered salt or zinc dust to the eye and leave it for another day, then treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the bleeder is non-nervous and cannot accommodate an needle or hold a forceps, this type of bleeder is often treated with medications as described in the sections, and it will heal as mentioned. If you decide to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a gentle, smooth, weak-bladed scalpel, as depicted here: . Disengage the bleeder from above gently. If you see that it is loosening, breaking off, and there is no deep cut from the disengaging, then apply some zinc dust or red dye or powdered salt to the eye and leave it for another day.
Then, repeat the process until all of it is gone, unless a hot, swollen lump appears in the eye, in which case leave it and treat the swollen lump until it heals, then resume disengaging until it heals completely. And if there is a flesh protuberance in the corner of the eye, treat it as necessary until it heals.
If the natu (eyelid) causes severe damage to the eye, suspend it with a hook and cut off some of it, but do not cut too much so as not to cause excessive tearing. Then apply red myrrh or zinc dust to the affected areas until healing occurs.
**Chapter XVII**
**On Cutting the Eyelid and Removing Excessive Flesh from the Eye.**
In some people, a red flesh may grow in the eyes, covering or almost covering the pupil, or extending beyond the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids may even turn outward, resembling the rose-colored narthex.
The procedure involves the patient lying on their head in your lap, then opening their eyes and using appropriate hooks to remove all the red flesh.
Or, using a scalpel or tweezers, cut off the flesh from the inner to the outer edge, ensuring all of it is removed while being careful not to harm the eye during the procedure. You can use either the instrument described for cutting the nail (scalpel) or small scissors. as shown in the illustration accompanying this text.
If the eye is injured and all the moisture is lost, fill it with finely ground salt or drop in a few drops of red powder or other caustic medications, and apply a white cloth soaked in egg white from the outside to prevent the hot swelling.
If there is some remaining moisture and you are overcome by blood, and you fear a hot swelling, leave the eye alone and treat it with something that reduces swelling, then resume treatment until it heals. The same goes for any excess tissue or meat that appears in the eye from this condition.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye naturally or through surgical intervention with iron, and do not put your hand into any of these treatments unless you are certain that the organ is capable of enduring such a procedure.
Chapter XVIII
On the removal of foreign bodies from the eye.
The condition, known as "the red threads," which tangle in the eye and obstruct vision, weakens the eye over time. You should first examine it: if the eye with the threads is strong and has no other illness besides these threads, then...
Then, the procedure is as follows: instruct the patient to rest their head in your lap, and use a hook (surgically sterile) to gently pull on these threads, one or two depending on your skill. The hooks should be flexible and curved in this manner: ...
Two double needles in a single body, as shown here:
Then, with a delicate scissors, carefully snip those vessels and wipe away the blood little by little until the eye appears free of them and the blood has drained. Be cautious around the eye, do not harm it with the scissors' edges, and conduct your work during the day, facing the sun.
Be very careful in your work so as not to cut any vessels other than those intended. When you are finished, apply eye drops of green or red iris water to consume any remaining foreign bodies with its sharpness; if you cannot remove all of it at once, then dress the eye with something to soothe the pain.
Leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the swelling goes down, then repeat the procedure on that same area following the same method until the condition improves.
Leave it [while the patient's head is resting on the student's lap, one person dries the tears and checks the vision in the front and back of the eye, and another holds open the eye with a fixed or triangular instrument that the student uses to perform the work]. The basic procedure involves opening the eye with instruments like fixed spreaders or triangular ones, which a student uses.
Be precise, with the patient's head resting on the student's lap, one person drying the tears and examining vision in the front and back of the eye, while you use a knife to cut the membrane. You can choose to start by cutting from above the eye or below; ideally, snip one thread at the very end of the eyelid and another at its beginning.
Grip it between the middle and index fingers, then attach pincers underneath them. Hold it again between the ring and little fingers, and gently lift your hand to see that the tendons have retracted. Cut the tendon from the smaller gastrocnemius with scissors or a delicate scalpel.
The surgeon pierces the tendon with a needle and probes until the entire tendon retracts onto the bone, ensuring it is not cut. If blood or serum splashes, it may cause harm.
Then, cut with scissors above and below the tendon's attachment to the Achilles tendon, ensuring nothing remains attached except for the calcaneal bone. If possible, lift it from the bone using the surgeon's knife, as this is preferable. In some cases, it may come off easily.
If the tendon is cut, it causes the flap to heal over several days, alleviating pain and reducing swelling, after which further work can be done on it.
Chapter XIX.
In reattaching the feather to the nose.
The feather, when treated with cauterization or sharp, burning medicine as described earlier, and does not heal, requires no other remedy than to open the abscess when it matures and extract all the moisture or pus inside.
Until the bone is exposed, if it is revealed and you see decay or blackness in it, remove it with this instrument, whose form is as follows:
The instrument called "al-khashnah al-ras" is made of Indian iron, and its head is rounded like a seed, possibly carved with the pattern of a knife or a scalpel, finely. You place it on the site of the bone's decay and rotate it between your fingers while slightly squeezing your hand until you feel that the decay has been removed.
You repeat this process several times, then treat the wound with drying, tightening drugs. If it heals and flesh grows, and no further issues arise, it is considered healed after forty days without recurrence or complications.
You shall know that it has healed, otherwise there is no remedy but to push the fistula back into the nose hole, revealing the bone again with iron or a sharp medicine. If the bone is exposed, take a splint in this shape:
The end of the iron should be triangular, and its wood part should be cone-shaped, as you can see. Place it on the bone, close to the nose and slightly away from the eye, then rotate the splint until the bone is inserted and felt by the patient.
By feeling if air escapes when the patient holds their hand over their nose, then treat the wound with tightening, drying drugs as previously taught. If flesh grows and hardens, the material that was extruding will return to the nose, which is lighter for the patient, God willing.
Chapter XX.
The repair of the eye's net (or retina).
If the eye's net (retina) is detached but no harm or loss of vision occurs, one should ease the patient then fix it in a specific position, followed by placing a weight on the back of the head without any condition, and suck gently.
Then, place on the eye a bandage made from date palm fiber, sap, and frankincense, and tighten it above with many ribbons. The method of preparing the ribbons is to take soft, thin threads, fold each into four parts, and create many from them.
Then, place them one on top of the other as much as the eye needs, and tighten it with a strong cloth, and repeat the sucking process without any condition. Then, remove the weight, leaving the eye tight for a day and night.
If the bandage relaxes during that time, it should be tightened again, and then remove the bandage after a day and night as mentioned. If you see that the eye has returned to its place, otherwise, repeat the bandaging, ribbons, tightening, and sucking process until it returns.
Chapter XXI.
In amputating the limb.
If a hernia occurs in the abdominal wall, then it protrudes outside the abdomen like a grape and disfigures the human form, and you wish to cut it off, then proceed as I describe.
Insert a needle into the base of the hernia from below upwards, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the opposite side and draw it through, leaving the first needle in place.
Then, cut the fold of the doubled thread and tie a portion of the hernia around the needle, pulling it tightly. Remove the needle and place a wet plaster of egg white over the wound, leaving it until the threads and hernia fall off. Then treat the wound to strengthen it.
Until the wound heals, tighten the hernia from all sides with a circle of cloth to promote healing. Then, use a thin scalpel to reach the depths of the eye, allowing the egg white moisture to drain, and lower the hernia, letting it rest until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Second.
On Treating Hemorrhoids.
This condition, known as the "coma," is merely a concentration of fluid in the eye similar to descending water but without it. The method of treatment involves having the patient sit on an upright chair and then holding their head with both hands from the sides.
You should move the patient until you observe the fluid flowing downward in the eye, then stabilize it so that it does not disappear. Light will then pass and the patient will be able to see objects as they were before. If the fluid does not flow downward, we know that it is not water; if it does not take the form mentioned, the treatment is incomplete.
If the above methods fail, place the patient between your hands and then take a thin scalpel to make a small cut above the cornea, where the sclera meets the cornea at the limbus, until the foreign matter is removed.
If the matter is expelled, wash the eye with hot water mixed with honey or water in which fennel seeds and honey have been boiled, then apply all other treatments until healing occurs.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
As we have discussed the types of water and their uses, choose the appropriate type for the treatment, explaining in detail. Then, position the patient between your hands, sitting them upright facing the light source, preferably near sunlight.
Secure and fasten the healthy eye, then raise the eyelid with your left hand if it is the watery eye (left eye), or with your right hand if it is the other eye (right eye).
Then place the end of the scalpel near the akrim (eyelid margin) with a sharp edge against the white part of the eye, on the side of the smaller corner of the eye, and push the scalpel firmly as you rotate it with your hand until it penetrates the white part of the eye, and you feel the scalpel reach something hollow.
The depth to which the scalpel goes should be equal to the distance from the cornea to the edge of the black part of the eye (the eyeliner). You will see the copper (presumably referring to a color or reflection) in the same way you see the eye due to the clarity of the corneal membrane. Then, move the scalpel upwards to the place where the water is.
Then press it down repeatedly. If water comes out, the patient will see what their vision has cleared and the scalpel in their eye. Then pause for a moment. If the water rises again, lower it a second time without removing the scalpel.
If it stabilizes and doesn't rise again, carefully remove the scalpel while twisting your hand slightly, then dissolve a small amount of pure salt in water and wash the eye from inside out. Finally, apply a moist cotton ball or cloth to the entire external surface of the eye.
Moisturize the eye with rose petal and white of egg, and bind it with a healthy eye. We and our contemporaries apply crushed cumin mixed with the white of an egg. If the point (of the instrument) does not enter the eye due to its hardness, for some people's eyes are very hard. One should take the instrument called the 'bayrid', whose image is shown here.
Punch only through the conjunctiva with this instrument, do not go too deep. Gently tap the point of the instrument onto the eye, then insert the instrument as mentioned. Upon completing the procedure, prepare a comfortable position for the patient to lie on their back in a dark room.
Avoid all movements and coughing. Make the patient's food soft to aid their condition. Do not move their head to the right or left at all. Keep the bandage in place until the third day, then remove it in that dark room and test their vision.
Show them objects, then re-bandage until the seventh day. Avoid doing this during treatment or after punching with your instrument, but instead, refrain from it until the water does not rise quickly due to intense staring.
If a hot tumor appears, unband the eye before the seventh day and treat it with something to alleviate the tumor. Once it subsides, gently release the patient's vision and place a veil over their face for several days in that dark room to help restore their eyesight.
It exits the house gradually and acts upon its causes, and know that in this art, the learner cannot do without observation multiple times before undertaking the work. I have been told by some Iraqis that he mentioned making a hollow puncture in Iraq that absorbs water.
I have never seen anyone in our country make this, nor have I read about it in any book of the first scholars, and it may be a recent invention. Here is an image depicting various types of punctures for those unfamiliar with them.
They are made particularly from copper, and their end has the same delicate shape, triangular with a sharp point.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
In the treatment of meat growths in the nose.
Different types of excess meat can grow in the nose, some resembling many-legged scorpions, and others may be cancerous, hard like stone, or soft and not discolored.
If the flesh is soft, neither malignant nor cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun with their nostrils open, and you should insert a hook into this flesh and draw it out, then cut what you have reached using a delicate, sharp scalpel on one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone, if anything remains, you will not be able to cut it off. Remove it with one of the delicate instruments gently so as not to leave any behind, for if blood overcomes you or a hot tumor appears.
If it is confronted with what is necessary or was a malignant tumor, then act swiftly and remove it until the blood stops and all flesh is gone. Then, after cutting, pour into the nose a void or liquid, for if the nose opens and moisture passes through to the throat.
Know that he has healed, for if moisture does not pass as it should, know that inside there is flesh grown around the bones, and the instrument did not reach it with the cut. Then you should take a thread of linen slightly thick.
And tie many knots in it, leaving a space of a finger or less between each knot. The patient then inserts one end of the thread into his nose using a rod or what he can make similar to a plug, and pulls it until it reaches the nostrils.
It emerges in a loop, and often children in schools do this action many times. It is an easy matter for whoever desires it. Then gather the two ends of the thread: one that emerged from the mouth and the other remaining in the nose.
Then, use the knife to cut through the flesh along the stitches in the thread, until you know that the flesh has been severed by the stitches of the thread. Then remove the thread and place it inside the nose after cleaning the blood with a cotton swab soaked in the Egyptian ointment, do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh, then it will become the last thing in the nose a lead pipe for several days until it heals. If treatment is needed, use it to dry.
And this is an image of the syringe that releases oils and medicines into the nose:

And if you prefer, make the tube closed like a pipe and the sponge of the syringe flat.
It has a handle at the end, as you can see, to hold it when heating oil or any other extracts or flowing substances.
Chapter XXV.
On the warts growing at the tip of the nose.
A benign tumor often grows at the tip of the nose, increasing in size over time until it becomes disfiguring.
Therefore, it should be cut off as soon as it appears and all of it removed, then either cauterized or treated with a burning medicine that acts like cauterization. If it cannot be cut off before it grows large, examine it; if it has become hard and stone-like, it is insensitive to pain.
Do not treat it with iron, for it is a cancerous tumor, and I have often seen that cutting out this tumor causes great harm to the patient, even if the tumor is soft to the touch and not painful. If cutting it all out is possible, do so. Then induce vomiting in the patient and cut it out without hesitation or delay, and treat the wound with a drying and contracting agent until healing occurs.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
In stitching the nose, lips, and ear. If the connection between them becomes detached from a wound or similar injury, know that once the attachment is broken in any of these cartilages, little benefit will be gained from treatment, except for some people.
When dealing with any of these conditions, examine if the wound is fresh and still has its blood; then close it by stitching the edges together and treat it until it heals. If the connection between the edges has separated and each gap is a separate wound, you should peel back each gap to the depth of the visible skin until it bleeds.
Then, close the edges with stitching and tighten it, and apply a mixture of myrrh and frankincense powder on top, followed by a layer of sealing ointment made from olive oil or any other binding ointment. Leave it for two or three days before untying it.
Change the dressing and let it be until the stitches dissolve on their own, then treat the wound with ointment until it heals. The method of closing a gap involves using needles to close it as described in the treatment of abdominal wounds, or using thread as mentioned earlier.
Chapter Twenty-Seventh.
On removing knots that appear on the edges.
Many people may present with small, hard lumps inside their lips, some resembling a pimple and others smaller or larger. You should turn the lip inside out, make a cut on each lump, and use a hook to remove it.
Cut through it from all sides, then fill the wound after cutting with powdered zaj (a traditional material) until the bleeding stops, and rinse with vinegar and salt. Treat the wounds with compresses that contract until they heal.
Chapter 28
In cutting off excess flesh in the gum.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums, which the ancients call 'abuls.' It should be removed with a hook or a scraper, cutting it at its base, allowing the bleeding to stop, and then applying powdered zaj (a traditional material) to the wound.
If the excess flesh, after treatment with a drying, contracting substance, returns, cut it again and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter 29
On the extraction of teeth with iron.
There may gather on the surfaces of teeth, both within and without, and between the gums, coarse, unpleasant scales that turn black, yellow, or green, causing damage to the gums and disfigurement of the teeth.
It is necessary that the patient should sit between your hands and head on your lap, and extract the tooth or root in which you have observed scales and something resembling sand, so that nothing remains of it, and do the same for blackness, greenness, yellowness, and other colors until it is purified.
If the initial cleaning does not remove it, repeat the process on a daily basis for several days until you achieve your desired result. Know that a tooth may require different types of tools with various shapes, depending on what presents itself to your work.
The tool used to clean a tooth internally is different from the one used externally, and also differs from that used between teeth, each with its own shape. You should have all these cleaning tools prepared.
Chapter Thirty
In the removal of teeth.
You should treat the tooth against its pain with all means and not hesitate to remove it if necessary, for it is a noble substance; even if removal is not an option, every effort should be made to alleviate the pain.
If the patient is determined to have a tooth removed, it should be done with caution until the painful tooth heals. The patient often deceives himself, believing that the pain is in a healthy tooth, so he extracts it, but the pain does not cease until the diseased tooth is removed, as we have seen many times from the actions of barbers.
If the painful tooth is indeed the one you are dealing with, then you should cut around it with a scalpel that has some force, in order to loosen the gum from all sides, and then move it slightly with your fingers or with delicate pliers until it becomes loose.
Then, firmly grip the tooth with both molars, placing the patient's head between your knees to secure it, and pull the tooth straight without breaking it. If it does not come out, take one of the tools and insert it from under the tooth on all sides gently and continue moving it as you initially did.
If the tooth is hollow or decayed, you should fill the hole with a cloth and tightly seal it with a thin rod to prevent it from falling apart during your grip on it with the pliers. You must also cut around the gum with precision on all sides.
Be careful not to break it, leaving behind some of the tooth, which could cause even greater suffering for the patient than the original pain. Do not follow the actions of ignorant barbers who extract teeth without using the techniques we have described.
Often, great harm befalls people, and among the least of these is a broken tooth, leaving all or some of its roots intact. Sometimes, it pulls out parts of the jawbone, as we have seen many times before. After extraction, rinse with a drink or vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs, which often does, immediately crush some glass and pack it into the wound.
If the glass does not help, use a delicate hook, initially moving the tooth. It should have a long shank and a short handle, thick enough to avoid bending when you grasp it.
They should be thick-handled, as you see, to avoid giving way or bending when grasped. The ends should be short, and they should be made of strong iron or steel, with teeth at the end that interlock for a secure, firm grip.
The ends can also be shaped like scissors, which provides a strong and secure grip.

Chapter 32.
In the extraction of teeth that have grown crooked among others.
If teeth grow in a position other than their natural place, it distorts the appearance, especially if this occurs in women or slaves; one should examine it, for if a tooth has grown behind another, it must be extracted.
If it cannot be removed nor returned to its place, and it is attached to another tooth, cut it off with this instrument, which has this form:
It resembles a small beak, and it should be made of sharp Indian steel with a very pointed end. It is used to cut through teeth in many cases due to their hardness, and to avoid damaging other teeth. If it grows back firmly, sharpen it with an Indian tool, which has this form:
It is entirely made of India, and its handle has a very fine engraving, similar to the tool used for making needles. You use it to gently sharpen the tooth over many days to avoid damaging it and causing it to fall out. Then, you smooth it with other rasps.
If a tooth has broken off and hurts the tongue when speaking, it should also be sharpened until the roughness of the break goes away, so it becomes smooth and does not hurt the tongue or disrupt speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
In the interweaving of mobile teeth with silver or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loose due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite into anything edible without causing them to fall out, having tried various constricting medications with no success.
The technique involves tying a gold or silver thread, with gold being preferable as silver tarnishes and corrodes over time, while gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium thickness and strong enough to fit between the teeth.
The imaging of the mesh involves taking the thread and inserting its bend between the correct teeth, then weaving both ends of the thread between the mobile teeth, one or more, until the weaving reaches the correct tooth on the other side.
Then, weave the thread back to the starting point, gently but firmly holding your hand to ensure no movement, and tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth to prevent it from slipping. Cut off any excess thread with scissors, gather it, twist it with a twister, and hide it between the correct and mobile teeth.
To prevent harm to the tongue, leave it thus tightened, and if it unravels or breaks, re-tighten it with another thread, allowing it to endure for all time. This is a depiction of teeth, showing the mesh in two correct and two mobile teeth, as you can see: .
A tooth, or even both, may return to its original position after falling out and form a mesh as described, remaining in place. This can only be achieved by an experienced dentist, who might also carve a bone from a cow into a shape resembling a tooth and place it in the missing tooth's spot. And it is tightened as we have said, so it remains and enjoys that state.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
In cutting the ligament that is exposed under the tongue, it prevents speech.
This ligament that is exposed under the tongue may either be natural, born with a person, or incidental, from a wound that has healed.
The procedure involves opening the patient's mouth and head in your lap, raising their tongue, and then cutting the nerve ligament running across it until the tongue is freed from its grasp. If there is some rigidity or complexity due to scarring from a wound, insert a hook and split it along its length until the ligament breaks and the knot unties.
Be cautious that the cut does not go too deep into the flesh, as it might sever an artery and cause bleeding. After making the cut, rinse with rosewater or a mixture of vinegar and cold water, then place a piece of cotton under the tongue for the patient to hold each night.
To prevent it from healing over, if there is bleeding, apply powdered glass to the area. If blood persists, use a lentil-shaped iron to suction the wound. Then treat it with all other treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five
On the removal of the toad that forms under the tongue.
Under the tongue, a tumor may form resembling a small toad, impeding the natural function of the tongue and potentially even filling the mouth with a hard mass. The treatment involves opening the patient's mouth facing the sun and examining the tumor.
If you see it as a black, solid mass that the patient cannot feel, then do not interfere, for it is cancer. However, if it leans towards being white and contains moisture, insert a hook in it and cut it with a delicate knife from all sides to extract it.
If blood interferes with your work, sprinkle powdered glass on it to stop the bleeding, then continue your work to completely remove the tumor, and finally rinse your mouth with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with all other appropriate treatments until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six
In the treatment of tonsillar tumours.
And what grows in the throat from other tumours may present itself as glands within the throat that resemble those that appear outside, called tonsils. If you treat them with what has been mentioned in the classification, they do not heal.
Examine it; if the tumour is solid, of a dark colour and slightly sensitive, do not treat it with iron. If it is red in colour and its origin is thick, do not also treat it with iron to avoid blood loss. Rather, leave it until it ripens, either to be extracted or to burst on its own.
And if it is white in colour, round, and its origin is thin, this is what should be cut off. The action involves looking before acting to see if its hot tumour has subsided completely or partially.
Then, sit the patient facing the sun with their head in your lap. Open their mouth and have a servant, holding an instrument in your hands, press their tongue down to the bottom as shown in this image:
Make an instrument of silver or brass, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue down with it and examine the tumour, take a hook and pierce it into the tonsil, drawing it out as far as possible without pulling any surrounding tissue.
Then, cut them with an instrument that resembles a pair of scissors, but both ends are curved and sharp, made from Indian iron or Damascus steel.
If this instrument is not available, then cut them with a scalpel that looks like this:
Sharp on one side and dull on the other, so after cutting one of the tonsils, cut the other in the same manner, then rinse the patient with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with boiling water mixed with pomegranate rinds or assafoetida or similar astringents until the bleeding stops, then treat it as mentioned for cutting the tonsils. After that, other growths in the throat, such as additional tonsil-like masses, should be cut in the same manner.
I treated a woman with a tumor that had grown inside her throat, affecting her ability to swallow and breathe, almost blocking the passage. The tumor was insensitive and prevented her from eating or drinking water.
She was close to death, had she not been treated immediately. The tumor had grown two branches extending out of her nostrils. I quickly inserted a hook into one branch and pulled it out, removing a suitable piece.
Then I cut it where I reached it through the nose hole, and I did the same for the other nostril, then I opened her mouth and pressed her tongue, then I inserted the hook into the tumor itself, then I cut some of it off, and only a small amount of blood came out.
Then the woman's throat opened, and she immediately drank water, then ate food, and we continued to cut the tumor repeatedly for a long time, but it kept growing back each time until it affected me and her.
I tried and squeezed the tumor inside the throat as much as I could, and it stopped growing. Then I left the place, not knowing what God had done to her after me.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On Cutting the Soft Palate Tumor.
If a tumor descends to the soft palate and swells, becoming white, elongated, and rectangular, it is called a "column." If it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called a "grapes." If treated as described in the previous sections, but without effect.
I observed that the hot tumor had subsided and she was thinner, so it should be cut. If it is rounded, aggregated, and not very long, and has a bloody or purplish color, or if it is insensitive, one should avoid cutting it.
In it there lies a danger for the patient, so when you see it in the mentioned form, white and long, the patient should be seated facing the sun, and his tongue pressed with the instrument described earlier, then the hook is inserted into the grape.
And you pull it down and cut it with one of the instruments mentioned in cutting the tonsils, and you should only cut what exceeds the natural state without excess, for if you cut more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after the cutting, you use what we described in cutting the tonsils and treat it until it heals. If the patient refuses to have it cut, you should use a trick to burn it without fear or caution, and the purpose of burning is with a sharp medicine, which is to place the patient's head on your lap.
Then, you press his tongue with the instrument mentioned, and take some of the sharp water described in the door of burning, which is not extinguished, and mix it with non-extinguished lime until it is neither too thick nor too thin, and fill the hollows of this tool with it. And this is its form:
The end of it, which holds the medicine, has a hollow shape like a spoon, and you place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, and the patient lies on his side, allowing saliva to flow from his mouth into his throat, so that nothing descends to harm him.
Then grasp the medicine in your hand and squeeze it on the tongue for half an hour until you see it turn black and the sting of the medicine cease. If you wish, take a cotton ball, wrap it around the end of a rod, soak it in the medicine.
Insert the rod with the cotton ball into the tube from above until the cotton ball sticks to the tongue. Repeat this process as many times as you need to achieve the desired effect of pressing the grape. Then leave it, and it will wilt and fall off after three or four days. If you need to apply the medicine again, do so.
After pressing, wipe around the grape with a cotton ball soaked in lard and dry it, then rinse with cold water. Treat externally with bandages and internally with gargles until it heals. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and removes fear.
The tongue can also be treated with milder methods than pressing and cutting, using incense as follows: Take fennel, rue, thyme, sage, chamomile, hibiscus, and other herbs, either all of them or some, and put them in a pot, submerging them in vinegar.
Boil it in a pot sealed with clay, and on top of the lid, place a hollow instrument made of silver or copper with a hole in the middle.
The end of the pipe that contains the sponge should be inserted into the patient's mouth until the steam rises to the pharynx through the tube, allowing the pharynx to enjoy the moisture. Repeat this process several times until it becomes limp, and beware of performing this treatment at the onset of the tumor.
If the tumor increases, it is often because of its hot nature; in such cases, this treatment should be applied when the tumor has subsided. If you do not have the pipe, take a tube and attach a thin egg shell to its end to avoid burning the patient's mouth.
Because the egg shell prevents the hot steam from burning the mouth, this is one of the good treatments, safe and effective.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On removing thorns and other objects lodged in the throat.
Often, the throat becomes lodged with a bone, fish spine, or other object. In such cases, one should remove whatever is visible after pressing the tongue with a tool against the roof of the mouth.
To examine what is in the throat, and if something is not visible but hidden, it is advisable to make the patient vomit before their food is digested in the stomach. Perhaps the lodged object will emerge through vomiting or the patient might swallow a piece of lint or a small foreign body.
If he swallows a bite of dry bread or takes a piece of soft sea sponge, ties it with a thread, and then swallows it, when it reaches the place of the foreign body, pull the thread quickly. Do this several times.
Often, the thorn or bone becomes stuck and does not come out as mentioned. If it does not come out, use a tool made of lead in this shape:
It should be slightly thicker than a rod, with a curved end that the patient gently inserts into a ring while raising his head and being careful not to touch his throat to avoid coughing, or the doctor can insert it with his hand.
And it is better for the patient to insert it, knowing the location of the embedded object, and pushing down or pulling his hand with the tool upwards, as much as possible, until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
In removing the lodged tick from the throat.
If the tick is treated with the mentioned remedies and does not improve, then look at the patient's throat in sunlight after pressing his tongue with the tool described to you.
If you notice a worm, draw it out with a small hook or a delicate, tight twig. If that does not work, take an empty tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the worm, then insert a heated iron into the tube.
Do this several times, and the patient should abstain from water for the entire day. Then, take a jug filled with cold water and open the patient's mouth, allowing them to rinse their mouth with the water without swallowing any of it. Occasionally, the patient should move their hand to stir the water.
If the worm senses the water, it will retreat to a safe place. If it does not exit as described, then dry the throat with the instrument used for drying the palate, repeating this process several times until it falls.
The method for using the inhaler involves taking a container with hot coals from a fire, covered by a lid with a small hole in the center. Attach the end of the inhaler to this hole and place the other end near the patient's mouth, closing their mouth to prevent the smoke from escaping until they are certain the smoke has reached their throat.
If the worm retreats to a safe place, repeat the process with the inhaler until it falls. The patient should endure thirst and eat salt and garlic while avoiding water; this treatment will undoubtedly force the worm out using these means. They resemble hooks as you see, except for this bend that enters the throat, and their ends resemble a bird's beak with the roughness of a file. If you press something with them, they do not release it.
