Chapter Forty
It is used to facilitate speech in those with tumors or lesions, making incisions in them.
The tumors are numerous and varied, each with its specific characteristics as mentioned in this book. They differ in their shells (Ø¨Ø·Ù) and incisions (Ø´ÙÙ) based on two factors: the nature of the tumor itself and the substances it contains.
The second type is determined by the locations where they occur in the body, as a tumor in the head differs from one in the abdomen, and a tumor in a hot spot (likely referring to a specific area of inflammation or infection) varies from one in a joint, each with its own rules regarding treatment.
Some tumors should not be drained until the pus within them has matured and reached its full potential, while others should be drained even in their raw, unripened state, such as those close to joints.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and its condition persists until it putrefies the surrounding area, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, leading to dysfunction of the member or the tumor might be close to a vital organ.
Because if you delay draining it until the pus matures, you will harm that vital member or it might be close to the anus, and you should drain the tumor while it is still raw and unripened, near the anus, lest the pus corrupt the tissue inside the anus, making it a fistula.
It becomes, in a sense, incurable, and you should know the time to drain tumors that have fully ripened when the pain subsides, fever dissipates, redness and pulsation decrease, the head of the tumor and other symptoms become apparent.
You should drain it at the lowest possible point of the tumor if feasible, to facilitate the flow of pus downwards, or in the thinnest part of the tumor with the most distinct ridge, and the drainage should be done along the length of the body if the tumors are located around the hands and feet.
And the locations of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries, in general, in all flat areas with no curvature, and if they are curved, then press them according to that area. If the tumor is in the fleshy parts, it is better to leave its pressure.
Until the pus has fully formed as we have mentioned, if you press it before that, the discharge of pus will be prolonged, causing great harm and filth, and its opening may harden, and some tumors may be pressed across the body's width when necessary or according to the needs of the organ.
For small tumors, use a single pressure, and for large tumors, a wide pressure or several cuts according to the size of the tumor, and some tumors may require the skin to be flapped and cut if they have become like a cloth.
It becomes, to some extent, dead as is often seen in many abscesses and cysts, some of which are incised with a three-angled cut, and others have part of them cut off like the shape of an olive, the typical Arab tumor.
Some may require a circular or crescent-shaped cut, or other cuts, and for tumors without a head, like smooth, flat ones, only simple pressure should be applied. If the tumor is large and has collected pus over time and you press it, do not rush so as to expel all the pus at once.
Not only should you drain some of it, but also compress the tumor and repeat this process gradually until all the pus is drained.
In particular, if the patient is weak or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an elderly person, the animal spirit (vital force) is often lost with each discharge of pus, and the patient might die without your knowledge. Therefore, be extremely careful in this matter.
After draining these tumors, you should clean the wound and examine it. If the tumor wound is small or the split is simple, use old linen or cotton thread. But if the tumor is large and the splits are numerous, consider other methods.
You should insert a thread in each split, connecting them together, and if the tumor has torn off some skin or formed an ulcer, stuff it with old cotton or hempen hair without moisture and secure it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat the wound with appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during the treatment, use cold water and vinegar. Soak a piece of old linen in it and press it on the bleeding area several times.
If the bleeding persists, you must employ the measures and treatments we have described in various chapters of this book, depending on the location and nature of the tumor. If it is winter and the area around the tumor has many nerves, soak bandages in a hot drink and oil.
And place it in the right position. If it is summer and the affected area is hot, then the compresses should be soaked in water and oil, or a drink and oil, all of which should be cold, so that on the third day, as we have said, you should dissolve and clean the swelling.
And in its treatment, you use things similar to it until healing occurs; this is what you need to know about the treatment of tumors in general. As for the detailed method, each tumor has been described with a summary of how to treat it.
Chapter Forty-One
On the incision of tumors that occur on the scalp.
On the scalp, small tumors may occur, which are of the type known as "sclera," containing bags with their own specific conditions, similar to a chicken's egg. There are many types, some fatty and others containing a liquid resembling mud.
Some contain moisture resembling butter, curds, or soup, and others are solid and hard. All of these pose no danger when incised and removed, unless a vessel (artery) is encountered during the incision.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe with an instrument, which will be described below (called a "diss"), to determine what they contain. If they contain moisture, make a longitudinal cut along the tumor, as shown in the figure: a simple, straight cut.
Begin by making an incision from line ~B~ to line ~C~. If the moisture has dissipated, peel and completely cut the sac that contained it, leaving no remnant whatsoever; for often, if any part remains, it will recur.
Then, dip a cotton ball in pure honey if available, or in salt water, and fill the wound with it, leaving it for another day; for it will consume what remains of the sac. Then, repeat the process with the cotton ball in pure honey a second and third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that no part of the sac remains, then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the swelling contains a fatty substance, open it in this manner:
Make a cross-shaped incision and pry open the wound with forceps, peeling it from all sides. Strive to remove the peritoneum that contains it; if you encounter a blood vessel, follow the described steps after addressing it. Similarly, act in the case of a hardened mass from the incision.
The treatment is the same as mentioned, and the incision on a hardened mass is easier because it contains little blood or moisture. Some of these masses in the head may have no moisture at all, for I have opened a mass in my own body that was devoid of any fluid.
The head of an old woman: I found the tumor like a hard stone, rough and white, unable to break it, even if someone threw it, it would only crack the skull, and this type was different from other tumors that occur in the head, like those that appear on the heads of children.
At the bases of the ears, make a simple incision through them all and turn their flaps downwards to facilitate the flow of material downwards, then treat them with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter Forty-Two
In the incision on pigs, these tumors often occur in the neck, under the arms, and in the groin, and they can also appear on other parts of the body. Some may be single, while others are numerous and some may arise from one another.
Each pig has a sac of its own, similar to the sacs in fruits, and tumors within this sac, as described for head tumors. There are numerous types: some are hard, others contain fluids, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
I have not seen a good case upon examination, as it appears similar to skin in color and moves in all directions, not attached to the neck's nerve, tendon, or artery, and is not sunken. You should peel it from all sides and expand the skin flaps with a hook or two or many hooks, as needed, as we mentioned regarding tumors of the head. Gradually extract them, leaving a small portion on the neck, taking care not to cut any blood vessels or nerves. The scalpel should not be too sharp.
To prevent excessive cutting or causing the patient anxiety, do not cut what is not necessary. If you cut a blood vessel or artery and are hindered from completing the task, apply powdered ash or some flour to the wound to stop the bleeding.
Secure the wound and leave it until the swelling subsides and the wound relaxes, and if it starts to putrefy, then the blood flow will cease. Then return to your work until finished. Afterward, inspect with your index finger to see if any remaining smaller clots or debris are present, and remove them carefully.

If the whole pig is cut, one should gather the edges of the wound and sew it up immediately, realizing that no remnant has been left behind. If there is a remnant due to the pig's bone, you shall remove it.

Chapter 34.
In the incision of the throat, regarding a tumor occurring within the pharynx.
The earliest mentions described this incision in the throat, but I have not seen anyone perform it in our country, and this is the pattern of their speech. As for those with strangulation, they should avoid the incision in the throat, as it is useless to them.
In order that all the veins and lungs are injured, and for those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no illness in the trachea, it is necessary to use the incision in the throat to escape the damage that occurs due to strangulation.
If we wish to do so, we should make a cut in the throat below three circles of the trachea or four small cuts across the width between two circles, to the extent that the cut is in the peritoneum, not in the cartilage. This position corresponds to the cut.
Since it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant, if the healer is timid, he should pull the throat skin with a hook and cut the skin until he reaches the trachea, avoiding blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should cut the peritoneum that we described, and he can identify the cut in the trachea from the mucus that comes out of it, along with the feeling of a stab and leaving the wound open for a while. If the time when he was afraid of suffocation passes, he should close the edges of the wound with skin.
He should operate only on the cartilage, then use medications that promote tissue regeneration until healing occurs. According to the author of this text, when they see that a patient's throat is blocked by one of these mentioned tumors, this is what all the aforementioned steps are for.
When a patient is on the brink of death, and he himself feels that he cannot breathe, they should operate by making a cut in the throat so that the patient can breathe through the wound, thus saving him from death. They are instructed to leave the wound open until the illness passes, which usually takes three days or so.
When then they ordered the wound to be stitched and treated until it healed, which I myself witnessed: a servant took a knife and cut through part of her lung, so she was called for treatment, and I found her gasping as if she were being slaughtered, so I exposed the wound.
I found the blood that emerged from the wound was thin, confirming she had not cut an artery or vein, and air was exiting from the wound, so I cleaned it and treated it until it healed, without any further harm to the servant aside from a slight sound.
After several days, she recovered to her best condition, so from here we say that cutting the throat poses no danger.

In the incision on the tumor that appears in the throat from the outside and is called the phlegm of the throat.
This tumor, called the phlegm of the throat, is a large tumor resembling the color of the body. It is frequent in women and has two types.
It can either be natural or accidental. Chapter Forty-Five
On Cutting According to Types of Merchandise.
Merchandise comes in many varieties, and all types have been mentioned in the classification. Here, it is essential to distinguish between merchandise and a discharge (or excretion), which is complex. I say that a discharge is characterized by heat, fever, and severe pain until the excess fluid subsides. When the discharge (or excretion) completes its process, the fever and severity subside. In contrast, merchandise does not exhibit heat, fever, or pain. It is enclosed in a sac-like membrane, which serves as its special casing, and resembles the color of the body. Its initial appearance is similar to a pimple, and it develops into a blister-like form, becoming larger or smaller in size.
They are of two types: either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture vary, as mentioned in the classification. When treating merchandise, it is essential to first explore and examine it with a tool called a probe, as depicted in the image following this text.
Determine the size of the tumor, then remove the probe and observe what comes out. If it is a liquid secretion, of any color, cut it open as I have mentioned for other tumors. If no liquid comes out after the probe, know that it is fatty.
Make a cross-cut on it as I have taught you, hang it with pincers and carefully peel the skin from all sides. If possible, preserve the bag and remove it intact with the goods.
If the bag tears while working and you cannot remove it intact, this often happens, so cut it into pieces until nothing remains, for if any part is left, whether small or large, the goods will be affected by it.
If you are unable to remove it and only a small part remains, cut the wound with some sharp, edible particles, tighten the wound, and place something that soothes the hot tumor on top. Treat it with all other remedies until it heals, and if the goods are large, mark its edges and treat it with what binds.
If you encounter a pulsating or non-pulsating vein and bleeding occurs, immediately cut the area with powdered glass and leave it tight for two or three days until the wound putrefies and the blood fever subsides, then proceed to remove what remains of the goods.

Chapter XLIX
In the incision of the tumor that is exposed by the artery or vein, called aneurysm.
If the artery is injured and the skin above it heals, a tumor often develops from this, as well as a bulge and swelling in the vein.
The signs by which it is known whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or a vein: if it is from an artery, the tumor is rectangular and located deep in the body, and if you press the tumor with your finger, you can feel a clicking sensation.
And the one that is from a vein, the tumor is round on the surface of the body; the incision on these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, and neck, and in many places on the body, and they can be very large, so you should avoid treating them with iron.
And what was from them in the limbs or head, you should avoid; for what was a bulge of an artery, make an incision in the skin longitudinally, then open the wound with forceps, and then remove the artery from the adhesions.
Until it is exposed, then insert an needle under it and penetrate it to the other side, tighten the artery with a doubled thread at two points, as I told you about the arteries in the temple, then use a scalpel to strike the place between the ligatures until all the blood comes out.
The tumour dissolves, then the treatment that produces pus is employed until the bands fall off, after which it is treated with suitable ointments until healing occurs.
If the tumour is located next to a vein, you should grasp as much of the tumour with your hand as possible, along with the skin, then insert an needle from below the site where you are holding, and pass it through while containing a doubled thread within until you emerge from the opposite side. Then, tie it well to the tumour as I have mentioned to you in tying the grape from both sides with the ends of the thread. If you fear that the threads may unravel, then insert another needle with a new thread beneath the entire tumour at the intersection of the first needle.
Pull on your threads in four places, then make an incision in the middle of the tumour. Once what is inside comes out, cut away any excess skin and leave what was attached. Then apply a dressing that has been soaked in a mixture of wine and oil. Then employ the treatment involving twisting and ointments until healing occurs.
Chapter Fifty.
On tumours that appear due to nerve twisting.
As the tumor appears in the artery and vein, so it appears in the nerve if it is struck or occurs due to excessive fatigue or similar, and this is most common in the wrists and ankles.
In every location where a joint moves, there is a firm tumor that resembles the color of the rest of the body. In most cases, it does not cause pain, and if pressed firmly, the patient feels a sensation similar to numbness. The tumor is not typically located deep within the body.
Instead, it is located beneath the skin and moves in all directions, neither going forward nor backward. As for what is found in joints, it should not be treated with iron, as it may cause permanent damage. And as for what is found in the head or forehead, it should not be treated with iron either.
Make a cut in the skin with a scalpel. If the tumor is small, use a dissector to remove it from its origin; if large, hang it on a hook and peel it off, then remove it entirely and sew up the wound until it heals.
Chapter Fifty
On cutting the warts that occur on the abdomen.
They may occur frequently in some people on their abdomens and other parts of their bodies, known as warts that resemble mushrooms due to their thin base and thick head. Their outer layers may split, and they can range from small to very large in size.
And I observed a man with two growths in his abdomen, similar to mushrooms, with no discernible difference between them; their bases were thin and white. Their lips had turned inwards and they were constantly exuding moisture. So I cut them off, weighing one at approximately eighteen ounces and the other around six ounces.
And when performing the incision, if the patient is moist and the growth is white and damp with a thin, soft base, cut it with a broad blade; have hot iron at hand, as blood often flows abundantly upon cutting.
If blood becomes overwhelming, apply hot iron; if the patient is timid and frightened of having their growth cut with iron, take a tight lead string and secure it around the growth, leaving it for two days, then increasing the tension on the lead string daily until it is removed.
Whenever the growth is affected until it disconnects and falls out on its own without assistance, there is some risk, especially when located in the abdomen. It may be possible to cut off half or part of it.
Then apply hot iron to prevent its return; then treat the wound until it heals. Be cautious when cutting a growth that is pale in color, lacking sensation and unappealing in appearance, as it may be a cancerous tumor, which we will discuss later.
Chapter LXXV.
In the treatment of navel hernia.
A navel hernia can result from various causes, either a tear in the peritoneum that allows the omentum or intestine to protrude, as is the case with other hernias, or from blood originating from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned, or from gas accumulating within.
If the hernia is due to a tear in the peritoneum and the protrusion of omentum, the mass will be similar in color to the body's tissue, soft without pain, and its position will be changeable. If it is due to the protrusion of intestine, its position will differ significantly from what we described. If you press it with your fingers, it will disappear then return, and there may be rumbling accompanied by severe pain, especially when entering a bath or after strenuous activity. If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
It will not disappear if pressed with your hand, and its size will neither increase nor decrease. If it is due to blood, the mass will appear black with these signs. If it is caused by flesh that has grown in, the mass will be firm and maintain a consistent size.

In the treatment of an adenoma with a pedicle.
The pedicle is a tumor twisted with some torsion, similar to a cluster, with relaxed antheia and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise, and walking. In the treatment of this condition, the safe method is as mentioned earlier.
However, it is necessary to mention the procedure as done by the pioneers. I say that the patient should sit on a high chair, then push down the antheia ligature, and grasp the scrotal skin with your fingers along with the vessels close to the penis.
And another assistant should hold it, stretching it firmly, then make a curved incision with a broad, sharp knife alongside the vessels until they are exposed. Then, as mentioned to you before, peel it from all sides, just as you would do with the arteries in the groin, and insert a needle with a doubled thread into it.
Tie it in the first place where the pedicle was exposed, and also in the last, then make a straight incision through its middle along the body's length, and remove any accumulated purulent, foul-smelling fluids.
Then treat the fistula with the treatment of other wounds that require a period for the vessels supplying one of the ovaries to fall, which is not harmful. If the duct affects all the vessels, one ovary should be removed along with the vessels, otherwise the egg will lack nourishment. By cutting the vessels, they wilt and cannot be used.
Chapter LVI
In the treatment of intestinal adenoma.
This adenoma occurs through an opening in the peritoneum extending across the abdomen towards the hips, causing the intestine to protrude through this hernia into one of the ovaries.
This hernia can either be a tear in the peritoneum or an extension of it, and these types are caused by numerous factors such as a blow, a jump, a shout, or lifting a heavy object, etc. Its sign is that if it's due to an extension of the peritoneum, it develops gradually over a long period of time.
It does not occur suddenly, and the tumor is flat towards the depth as the peritoneum squeezes the intestine. Its sign is that if it's due to a tear in the peritoneum, it begins with severe pain all at once, and the tumor becomes clearly visible under the skin near the site.
And thus, when the intestine protrudes and exits the peritoneum, it may carry with it adventitious tissue, which is then called intestinal and adventitious, or it may contain feces that become trapped there, leading to the patient's demise.
Because it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially if pressed, treating this condition with iron is dangerous, so one should be cautious not to resort to it. The treatment involves commanding the patient to return the intestine to its original position within the body if possible.
Then the patient should lie on their back between your hands, raise their legs, and you should stretch the skin next to the testicles upwards. Then, cut the entire length of the scrotum's skin and insert forceps into the incision to open it as needed.
The incision should be of a size that allows the egg to be removed. Then, peel the membranes beneath the scrotal skin until the white, solid peritoneum is exposed on all sides. At this point, insert your index finger into the space behind the testis.
Between the white peritoneum beneath the testis and the second peritoneal layer, loosen the adhesions behind the testis. Then, with your right hand, bend it inward towards the scrotal skin, while simultaneously lifting the white peritoneum upwards with your left hand, elevating the testis along with the peritoneum towards the incision.
And command the servant to extend the egg upward, and you release the adhesion from behind with a complete release, and inspect with your finger to ensure there is no twisted intestine in the rigid white membrane; if you find something, push it down into the abdomen.
Then take a needle with a thick thread twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the peritoneum that lies next to the incision, then cut the ends of the thread bends so that there are four threads. Then arrange them one on top of the other in a cross-like manner and tie the peritoneum, which is under the scrotal skin, with a strong ligature from both sides; then also twist the thread ends and tie them tightly so that nothing can reach the vessels that nourish it. To prevent this, leave a space the size of two fingers between the ligatures; after these two ligatures, leave part of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin, as large as a finger.
To prevent this, leave a space the size of two fingers between the ligatures; after these two ligatures, leave part of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin, as large as a finger. Cut the remaining part round and remove the egg, then make a slit below the scrotal skin from which blood flows, as described previously; then use wool soaked in oil, place it in the wound, and apply the ligature as described.
And if the white peritoneum, which we mentioned after cutting it carefully to prevent bleeding, often occurs, leave the ligature until it falls on its own. If its fall is slow, pour hot water on it and carry what causes it to sweat until it falls.
Then treat the wound with all the treatments for surgical wounds until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Six
In this gaseous adenoma, I have seen no one dare treat it with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be made as described for the adenoma accompanied by a daly (a type of plant), namely, to bind the vessels after making the cut gently below.
Then make a cut in the middle and treat the tumor to open it, so that the vessels fall, and treat the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Six
In the hernia that occurs in the groin.
The hernia in the groin may present itself as we mentioned, filling the area without any descent of the intestine into the scrotum. If there is a descent, it is slight and returns to its place at all times.
However, if time passes, the tear in the peritoneum will increase until the intestine and testicles descend, and this is due to the extension of the peritoneum in the abdomen as we have mentioned. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and the organ drops, twisting, and its treatment is by cauterization as we have previously described.
It may also be treated with a metal instrument in this manner: the patient lies on his back between your hands, and you make an incision across the bulging hernia, about three fingers wide, then cut through the peritoneum beneath the skin.
Until the white peritoneum beneath it is exposed, take a rod and place it on the area where the peritoneum has separated, pressing it deep into the abdomen, then sew the two sides together, one against the other, using stitches to hold them in place.
Then take the end of the rod, without cutting the peritoneum or touching the egg or any other organ, as we taught you in treating intestinal hernias, and treat it as a regular surgical wound.
If the stitches break, clean the wound and dress it until it heals; then the peritoneum contracts and the hernia does not increase in size.
The best course of action in this case is preferable because it is closer to safety.
Chapter LXXXVI
On the relaxation of the scrotal skin.
The scrotal skin often relaxes in some people without the muscles inside relaxing, which makes it look ugly. Therefore, whoever wishes to treat it should put the patient on his back.
Cut all the relaxed skin from the thick skin, then gather the edges and sew them together with three or four stitches, making sure to secure the stitches, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it like any other wound until it heals, and the stitches fall out.
Chapter LXXIX
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
For this reason, I should not mention it in this book, but I have included it for two reasons. One is so that the physician, when asked about it, will know the treatment for those who encounter it. The other reason is that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
As with lambs, goats, cats, and similar animals, I say that castration is done in two ways: either by pressure or by cutting and severing. In the case of pressure, we make the animal sit in hot water until its female organs relax, soften, and hang down.
Then, you smooth and relax them with your hand until they become soft and no longer resistant to touch. For castration by cutting, you must hold the animal and, using your left hand, grasp the scrotum and then tie the testicles before making a single cut on each testicle.
Once the testicles are exposed, cut them after having removed the outer covering, leaving only the thin membrane covering the vessels intact. This method of castration is superior to that by pressure.
Because pressure may leave some residual sensation in the female organs, causing the animal to desire copulation, then you should treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy
In the treatment of hermaphrodites.
Hermaphrodites in men are of two types: one where it appears below the perineum or within the scrotum, having a shape resembling a female vagina with hair, and urine may drain from the one within the scrotum.
And as for women, there is one type, where it appears above the vagina on the pubis, resembling a smaller male phallus protruding from outside, similar to a man's; both are feminine in nature. Treatment for all three types.
The two types of men and the one type of woman should have the excess flesh cut away, so that its mark is removed, and then treated like any other surgical wound until healing occurs; however, for the second type of man from whom urine drains through the scrotum, there is no operation, and it will never heal.
Chapter 71.
On cutting the clitoris and the flesh extending from the vulva of women.
The clitoris may grow larger than the normal size, becoming distorted and repulsive in appearance; in some women, it may swell to a degree comparable to that of men, leading to sexual intercourse.
It is necessary to hold the clitoris with your hand or a forceps and cut it, without overdoing the cutting, especially at its base, so as not to cause bleeding.
Then treat it with surgical treatment until it heals. As for the tissue that grows, it is tissue that grows inside the uterus, filling it.
And it may protrude outside, like a carbuncle. Therefore, some early scholars call it the "carbuncle disease," and it should be cut as you would cut the clitoris, using a suitable instrument and treating it until it heals.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of hernia.
Hernia is when a woman's vagina is either completely closed or has a small opening, either due to birth or an accidental injury. The accidental hernia can be caused by excess tissue or a growth.
Or it can be a thin or thick peritoneum, located deep within the uterus, or on its sides, or above or below, preventing intercourse, conception, and childbirth. It may also cause amenorrhea (absence of menstruation). This should be determined by an experienced midwife.
If the blockage is visible, examine it; if not, use your fingers or a probe. If the blockage is made of thin perineum tissue and is close to the labia, act quickly to incise it, making cuts resembling a comb on the labia, then place your thumbs from both hands underneath.
For a woman, lie on her back with her legs spread apart. Then force open the labia to puncture the thin perineum tissue, allowing the blockage to open. Take a cloth, soak it in oil, and apply it to the area. Have sexual intercourse with the woman daily to prevent the wound from healing and sealing again.
If the perineum tissue is thick and dense, use a wide scalpel similar to a banana leaf to cut through it. If the blockage is made of flesh, hang it with pincers and cut it. Have on hand medications to stop bleeding without causing pain, such as calamine, chamomile, and frankincense mixed with egg whites.
Then use a wide lead pipe to prevent the wound from healing quickly. Grip it for several days and apply a dry cotton string as a dressing. Treat it with all remedies to promote healing, but be aware that another meat-like growth may occur in the uterus; if so, cut it in the same manner.
Unless it is a benign tumor, the cancerous growth in the uterus should not be treated with iron under any circumstances.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
In the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red sores that occur in a woman's genital area.
As for hemorrhoids, it is an inflation of the veins' openings such that they discharge a significant and constant amount of blood. If hemorrhoids are left untreated, they transform into warts, and I have mentioned in the previous section their types, signs, and symptoms.
As for what accepts treatment and what does not, I say that hemorrhoids and warts, if they are located deep within the uterus and have not appeared to cause discomfort, then they do not respond well to direct treatment with iron. However, those that are found in the opening of the uterus, which do cause sensitivity, are the ones that can be treated.
A woman should enter a cold house, then use a scalpel or a rough cloth to remove the warts and cut them back to their roots. Then, she should apply pressure over the wound to stop the bleeding from one of the cutting edges without causing further injury.
She should apply similar remedies like fennel, calamine, and myrrh, and then elevate her legs against a wall for a while. If the bleeding persists, she should immerse them in cold water. If the bleeding continues, seat her in a bath of sumac, pomegranate peel, and gallnut, and finally, prepare a dough with barley flour, honey, and vinegar to apply to the affected area.
Apply a dressing to the back, and if the blood stops, then fasten the splints to the ribs and breasts without any condition. Then take a cloth and dip it in an infusion of tramel or an infusion of camel's tongue or an infusion of date palm branches with oak bark juice, and apply it to the wound.
If the tumor subsides, treat the area with a ointment until it heals. As for the red pustule, it resembles the heads of hair, rough to the sight, so you should cut what is visible of it, as you would with warts, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter LXXIV
In the case of an abscess in the fallopian tube that appears in the uterus.
Various types of tumors may occur in the uterus, including cancer, fibroid tumors, ulcers, abscesses, festering sores, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors. All these conditions and their types have been described in detail in the previous sections.
In this discussion, we should mention the hot tumor that appears in the uterus, especially if it is one of the tumors that persists for a prolonged period. You should examine it; if the pain from the tumor is acute at its onset, with pulsation, inflammation, and fever, then consider treating it accordingly.
I have seen that when the sense of touch perceives redness, do not hurry to bind it. Treat it with what aids in maturation. Until, when these symptoms subside, then the woman should sit on a chair with two seats and lean back.
And gather her legs towards the lower abdomen, opening her thighs, and place her arms under her knees, securing them with a suitable band.
Therefore, the midwife should sit on the right side and use the instrument that opens the cervix, whose image will be described later. If you intend to use this instrument, you must assess the depth of the woman's uterus to avoid inserting more than necessary.
If the instrument causes harm because it extends beyond the depth, one should place pads on the cervix's edge to prevent its entire insertion into the uterus. The thread that is drawn through the instrument should be from the upper side.
While you hold the instrument, the assistant turns the thread until the cervix opens. If an effusion appears and feels soft when touched with the hand, it should be incised at its base with a wide scalpel after allowing sufficient time.
A soft thread soaked in rose fat or green oil with some tension should be placed in the wound. The thread should emerge from the cervix incision and be wrapped around the uterus and vulva, using pure wool soaked in boiled water.
Then, it is treated after two days with the ointment that is suitable for it until it heals.
The womb and the wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water in which the root of the herb or long fennel has been boiled, and finally apply the ointment, for if the abscess is hidden internally within the womb.
One should refrain from treating it with iron, but instead, treat it as we have mentioned in the classification.
Chapter LXXV.
In teaching midwives how to treat living fetuses that have emerged in an abnormal form.
The midwife should first know the signs of normal childbirth, which include if you observe the woman straining downwards and longing to breathe in fresh air, and finds it easier despite her postnatal state.
And if the baby comes out quickly, know that this is a normal delivery; the baby should come headfirst, and the placenta may be with or hanging from the umbilical cord. If you observe these signs, squeeze the woman's stomach to expedite the baby's delivery.
If the foetus descends with the placenta, it is a natural and desirable delivery. However, if the foetus emerges with its legs first, or its hands before its head and legs, it is an abnormal and condemned birth.
If one hand or leg emerges first, or if the head and hand, or even the foetus is curled up and potentially flips over on its back, these are undesirable positions. The midwife must be skilled and careful in handling all such situations to avoid mistakes.
I will illustrate each of these positions and explain the techniques to recognize and manage them. If the foetus emerges with its head first in a normal position, but the mother experiences difficulty and her strength wanes, I will have her sit on a chair while other women support her.
Moisturize the mother's vagina with cooked fennel and wet oils. Then, the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers, cuts the placenta, allowing the moisture to flow out, and presses on the woman's abdomen until the foetus is delivered.
If the foetus does not descend, the woman should be given a mixture of fennel saliva and sherbet oil. After this application, she should sneeze and blow her nose while holding her mouth and nose shut. This often facilitates the foetus' descent. If the hands emerge first, they should be gently pushed back, little by little.
If the baby cannot be delivered, place the woman on a bed and raise her legs above her head. Then shake the bed on the ground while she holds onto it to prevent herself from falling. If the hands do not deliver the baby and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the remaining fetus.
If the baby is positioned with its legs up, tie the woman's hands with a cloth and gently pull them to help deliver the baby. If the baby comes out on its feet, you should raise them slightly and turn the baby slowly until it is in a flat position. Then take one of its legs and stretch it out gradually.
If the baby's legs reach the hips, instruct her to cough and blow her nose gently (using a handkerchief), as this often helps deliver the baby. If this does not work as described, slowly turn the baby until it is in its natural position; it should then come out smoothly. If none of these methods work, try turning the baby again until it is ready to be delivered naturally.
Take some gum from the date palm, horse radish, and althaeas, mix them well in a mortar, then apply this mixture to the vagina and lower abdomen, and have her sit in warm water until the contractions begin.
If you notice that her contractions are not effective, prepare a mixture of honey and create a vaginal tampon for her. Once she feels it and leaves it in place for an hour, have her sit on a chair, then gently cough and press on her nose and mouth while lightly massaging her lower abdomen; the baby should come out naturally at this point, positioned with its knees and hands.
If the patient is in this condition, insert his hands a little at a time, then have the woman lie on her stomach and lower her legs while lying on a bed, and bring her arms together. Then press slightly above her abdomen a little at a time. Then remove the cushions under her legs so they are pointing downwards. If the baby does not come out, take both her legs and move them vigorously. Then press above the perineum little by little until the baby ascends. Then the midwife should insert her hand and gently adjust the baby.
Order the woman to move until the baby is born, with the baby's hand extended and grasping one of hers. If she cannot withdraw her hand, hold the woman upright and have her walk. If walking is not possible, have her lie down.
Then place her on her bed and vigorously shake both her legs. Use the instruments described from the birthing kit. If she experiences strong contractions, sit her down on a chair, treat to withdraw the hand and adjust the baby to its natural position. Have her sneeze and order her to move until the baby is born.
When the baby is born on its back with its hands extended and facing its mother's spine, the midwife should grasp the baby's hand and gently turn it, straightening it carefully. Once aligned, she should support the baby with the oil and lubricants as described.
Then make her move little by little. If she does, sit her on the chair and order her to lean to the right side, then instruct her to stretch and sneeze with the handkerchief, for it will come out, just as the fetus comes out upright on its side.
If it descends into the cervix in this manner, with the placenta, cut the placenta with your nail until it is free from moisture. If it attaches to the face and neck of the fetus, cut it from the umbilical cord to prevent suffocation and death. Then push the fetus back inside until it is in the correct position.
Instruct the woman to sneeze several times, for it will facilitate the delivery of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born together, sometimes three or four, and they can survive, though this is rare. Five, however, is beyond nature; they never live.
They are surrounded by a single placenta, and they are separated by a membrane that connects them at their umbilical cords. They can be delivered naturally and easily, like a single fetus, or they can be delivered poorly as we have described.
If some become difficult at birth and proceed to the conditions we have described, treat them as we have mentioned, for the midwife must be gentle, act with caution and patience, and use all means at her disposal to ensure safety.
Describe the fetuses that are imagined in the womb and then fall, it may be one or two or three or four or five or six or seven or more than ten. It is confirmed with me that a woman miscarried seven, and another had fifteen, all of them were imagined as such.
By the grace of the Wise Creator, all of this should be known by the midwife. She may one day encounter such a case and handle it as she should.
Chapter LXXVI.
In the removal of the dead fetus.
If you treat the dead fetus as mentioned in the classification of treatments and it does not come out, forcing you to use your hand, then you should consider: if the woman is strong and free from any symptoms that could cause harm, she should lie down on her back.
Her head should be inclined downward, her legs elevated, and a helper stabilizes her from both sides or ties her to the bed to prevent her body from tilting when extending the fetus.
Then, the cervix is moistened with damp oils, saliva from the chamomile flower and fennel seed, and flaxseed. The midwife should then rub her hand with these oils and salves and gently insert it into the womb. She should look for a suitable place to insert the forceps to deliver the fetus.
Look, if the fetus is positioned so that it descends on its head, then hooks should be inserted into its eyes, or nape, or mouth, or palate, or under its chin, or in the places near the ribs, or beneath the sternum.
If, however, it descends on its legs, then in the groin and middle ribs; hold the hook with the right hand and make its fold between the left hand's fingers, and gently insert the hand and hook into some of these places mentioned.
Until you reach something hollow, then insert another hook opposite it, or a third if necessary, to ensure an equal pull and prevent any leaning towards one side; then extend it levelly, not just straight, but move it in every direction.
To facilitate its exit, as is done with a tooth when it is knocked out, and in between, the extension should be relaxed; if any part becomes trapped, the midwife should grease some fingers and insert them to turn what is stuck; if some of the fetus comes out, then move the hooks to other places.
It should be slightly raised so that she does this until the entire fetus is delivered; if a hand comes out before others and cannot be pushed back due to compression, it should be wrapped with a cloth and then pulled until it comes out completely, then cut off.
Whether in the wrist or the forearm, and similarly in the other hand and feet, if the fetus' head is large and experiences pressure during delivery, or if there is water collected in its head, then you should insert a sharp instrument between the fingers.
And you should make an incision with it to expel the water, or use the instrument called a 'mushdakh', whose image is presented with the images of other instruments in the following chapter.
Similarly, if the fetus' head is extremely large, you should make an incision or use the mushdakh as we mentioned to crack the skull, then extract the bones with pincers. If the head emerges and becomes compressed at the shoulder, it should be cut until the moisture in the chest drains.
If the chest compresses, then if it does not yield and cannot be delivered, cut as you are able. If it is below the abdomen and swollen or contains pus, you should press it until the moisture drains from it.
If the fetus is in a breech position, it is easily pulled and aligned towards the womb. If it becomes compressed at the abdomen or chest, you should grasp it with a cloth wrapped around your hand and make an incision at the abdomen or chest until the contents drain.
If all other members are removed and the head is withdrawn and retained, the midwife should insert her left hand. If the cervical opening is open, she should insert her hand into the cervix and seek the head with it, then draw it towards the cervical opening with her fingers, after which she should insert a hook or two and draw it with it.
If the cervical opening is closed due to a hot, painful tumor, it should not be forced open, but rather cold, moist, greasy substances should be applied, along with remedies and sitting in water that relaxes and lubricates.
If the fetus is on its side and can be aligned, use what we mentioned for a live fetus; if that is not possible, it should be cut and removed, ensuring no part of the placenta remains inside. Once you have finished treating the patient, apply the remedies mentioned for hot tumors in the womb.
If bleeding occurs, it should be treated in a contraction bath and with all other remedies; I have seen a woman who became pregnant again after her previous pregnancy resulted in the fetus dying inside her.
Then, after a long time, another tumor appeared in her cervix, which swelled until it opened and started to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not heal, so I applied some strong, drawing ointments to it.
Then, after some days, a bone emerged from the site, and another one after that. I was amazed, as the abdomen is not a place for bones. I presumed it to be from the bones of a dead fetus, so I examined the wound and extracted many bones from the fetus's head.
I continued to do this until I extracted numerous bones from it, and the woman was in good health and lived for a considerable time, during which a small amount of pus formed at the site. I have only shared this rare case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for the skilled physician who uses his hands for treatment.

Chapter 87
In removing the placenta.
If the placenta becomes stuck after childbirth, instruct the patient to grip herself tightly, then cough forcefully while you press on her mouth and nostrils. If it passes out this way, otherwise take a tool (a forceps or similar) and carefully extract it.
Make a hole in the cover and insert therein open herbs such as fennel, chamomile, mint, camomile, sage, endive, and artemisia, or some of them. Moisten them with water and place over fire. Then place a silver tube on the hole in the cover, with the other end in the cervix.
Hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, then sneeze as mentioned. If the placenta still does not come out after this treatment, the midwife should dip her left hand in shiraz oil or elephant's spit.
Then insert it into the vagina and examine the placenta. If it is contracted, grasp it and slowly stretch it until it comes out. If it is attached deeply within the uterus, insert the hand as described earlier, find the placenta, and gently pull it with caution.
So as not to cause the uterus to fall with excessive pulling, it should be gently moved to the sides, right and left. Then the amount of pulling can be increased, and it will respond and release its adhesion at that point. If the cervix is closed, the treatment has already been described through sneezing, the pot, and herbs.
If it does not come out with all the treatments described, avoid excessive pulling. Instead, tie any remaining part to the cervix and apply a four-ingredient ointment (as described) which will soften it after a few days.
It dissolves and exits, except that if it putrefies, a foul odor ascends to the stomach and head, harming the patient. Therefore, smoke should be used that is appropriate.

It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or copper, with the narrow end inserted into the vagina and the wide end over the fire, where incense is placed on the embers, held until that incense disappears, then replaced with another.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
In the treatment of hemorrhoids without perforation.
It may happen that many boys are born with hemorrhoids not perforating, but blocked by a thin membrane. The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger, or otherwise, she should tie it tightly with a sharp scalpel, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then, she should place a woolen pad soaked in wine and oil on it and treat it with ointments until it heals. If she fears that it may get blocked, she should insert a lead pipe into the opening for several days and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
The blockage of the anus may also be caused by a healed wound or a tumor, which should be incised and then treated with the remedies and plaster we have mentioned.
Chapter Eight
In the treatment of hemorrhoids occurring in the lower regions.
Hemorrhoids occurring in the lower regions are a complication and thickening that occurs near the anus from the outside or in the space from one side, and the hemorrhoid may be one or more.
If this complication persists, it will open and release a white watery or thin pus-like discharge, and some of these hemorrhoids may protrude into the rectum and colon, but if they are not protruding, they can be identified by what is discharged with the patient's stool and flatulence during defecation.
And sometimes worms may emerge from them, and they can be hemorrhoids if they are in the space and protrude into the bladder or penis, or they can extend to the hip joint and tailbone, and what is known as a hemorrhoid that protrudes into the anus without being connected to anything else.
Insert your index finger into the anus and use a thin needle made of copper or iron to enter the hemorrhoid if it is not curved, but if it has a curve, insert a fine needle made of lead or a horsehair into it.
If you do not feel the probe or a hair in your finger, and you do not sense anything at all and no excreta or odor or worm emerges from the wound as we have mentioned, then know that it is non-infectious and you should proceed with treatment.
If the fistula is open to the bladder or urethra, its indication is the passage of urine through it and its inability to heal with medication. However, if it is open to a joint in the thigh or to the coccyx, its sign is the reach of the probe to those areas.
If there is no curvature towards the bone, and pain in a specific direction, and pus always emerges from it, and no treatment takes effect, and it does not heal with ointment, then there is no cure for these types of fistulas.
Treating them is a futile endeavor for those who attempt it without knowledge, as for the fistulas that are not open or chronic, healing can be achieved with iron, as I will describe and as I have experienced, by having the patient lie on their back with their head lowered.
Have the patient raise their legs and tilt their knees towards their stomach. Then, insert a copper or lead probe into the fistula if there is a curvature, until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it towards the anus, you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel that the probe has penetrated all the way through your finger, exposed and without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and it, then know for certain that it is penetrated and do not tire yourself trying to treat it, as we said before.
They say that it heals in some people rarely, and the treatment that is hoped to be beneficial includes applying a hot, thin iron according to the size of the fistula, as mentioned before, and inserting it into the fistula carefully until it reaches near the anus, then withdrawing it and repeating this process twice or three times.
Until you know that all the excess flesh accumulated, resembling a bird's feather pipe, has burned, then treat it with a wet twist in butter, to remove the burnt flesh, and afterwards treat it with healing ointments, if it heals.
If the probe does not penetrate your finger in the anus and there is a thick membrane of flesh or membrane between them, and you see the fistula below the skin surface, then make an incision at the beginning of the fistula. As you pass the probe through the incision, while it is in the fistula, until you reach where the tip of the probe ends, remove the probe and discard it, then clean away the accumulated flesh resembling a bird's feather pipe, leaving nothing behind.
Then treat it with adhesive plasters until it heals. If blood overcomes you and prevents your operation on those meats, the best thing to do and most beneficial is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire combines two good qualities: it burns that excess flesh.
Cut the blood and dry the humors, then treat it with moist cataplasms of butter or powdered sulfur mixed with oil, until the site purifies and those burnt meats come out in the pus, then subsequently treat the wound with adhesive plasters that promote the growth of hard flesh.
These are the medicines that contract and dry; if they succeed, healing is complete and relapse is unlikely. If the fistula has penetrated deeply into the rectum and receded from the surface of the body, insert your finger into the rectum and explore it.
If you feel the probe and there is a barrier of membrane or flesh between it and you, and it is close to penetrating, then the only course of action is to be ambitious and hopeful, using one of three methods: either cauterize it as we have said.
Or cut it until you reach its bottom, so that you can insert cataplasms and treat it from close range, but do not overdo the cutting lest you sever the muscle surrounding the rectum, causing the patient to suffer from involuntary defecation. Then treat it as we have mentioned; perhaps it will heal as we said.
Either you penetrate and pierce that veil after splitting it near the anus, as we have mentioned, either with a probe or with another sharp instrument, and then clean all the coagulated flesh in the wound to where you can by probing.
Then, close the wound and the incision with all its openings until it heals, leaving the fistula open inside the anus, which will be lighter for the patient, and the fistula may be formed in this way.
If you insert the probe into the fistula, which is on one side of the anus towards the body surface with the skin and ring of the anus, take then a probe with a pointed end like a shoemaker's needle in this shape: .
Insert a threaded thread made of five or more threads into it, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom. If it is drained into the edge of the anus from inside near the surface.

Whenever the thread cuts through the flesh, tighten it well until the tissues between the thread ends cut off and fall away, then treat the wound until it heals and recovers.
If the fistula's orifice is not draining, drain it as best you can, but be aware that it may extend deeply and you should not attempt to cut into the muscle to do so, then proceed as we have mentioned until it heals.

Chapter Eighty-One.
In the treatment of hemorrhoids from which blood flows, cutting them, and healing fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the rectum, resembling small red blowers, or they look like grapes, with smaller and larger ones, and blood always flows from them.
They can be single or numerous, and they may be outside the rectum at the extremities, but those outside are usually less moist, releasing yellow fluid or a little blood, always flowing, and resembling the color of the body.
For the treatment of those inside the rectum, you should instruct the patient to defecate and move around until the anus opens, revealing the hemorrhoids, then quickly grasp them with forceps or your fingernail and cut them at their base; if they do not stay in place due to their moisture and laxity.
Remove the skin with a coarse cloth or a piece of gauze, pull it with your fingers, then cut it and sprinkle some sharp particles on it to act as a cauterizing agent, or use a fako (a surgical instrument) as mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat it with all other remedies until it heals, if the hemorrhoid does not respond to treatment and come out, inject the patient with a solution that causes a slight irritation to wash out what is in the hemorrhoid and quickly withdraw when the patient moves. The treatment of hemorrhoids outside the anus is easy and simple.
Take it with your fingernail or hook it to a fishing line and cut it, then treat it as mentioned until it heals, and if one dislikes cutting with iron, they should use a needle with a twisted thread.
Then pull the wart upwards and push it out with the needle at its base from the other side, wrap the ends of the thread under the needle while it is perpendicular, tighten the wart firmly, then tie the thread and remove the needle, do this for all warts, leaving one untouched.
To drain any excess blood, then place a cloth soaked in rose fat or asphalt on the hemorrhoid and instruct the patient to rest, leaving it until it falls off. Once the warts drop, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they heal.
And as for the treatment of cracks, it often arises from dryness and a stagnation in the humors. If it persists and no remedy is effective, then you should remove it with a surgical knife or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper layer that prevents its healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals as it should. If it does not heal, repeat the process with greater force, causing it to bleed and slough off. Then treat it again, for it will heal.
Chapter LXXVIII.
In the treatment of reversed nails, unreversed nails, warts, and dry moles.
A nail is a round knot, circular in shape and similar to the body's color, found throughout the body, particularly on the soles of the feet and between the toes, causing pain while walking.
One should make a cut around the nail, using a hook or a hook-and-line tool to extract it from its root, then treat it. If desired, as mentioned previously, you can apply heat to it. As for warts (thallus), each one is a small, rough, round protrusion above the surface of the skin.
And often, warts are treated by stretching and cutting them, or drilling them with a silk thread or a hair, until they fall off. If you wish, you can cauterize them with fire or sharp medication as previously mentioned.

Chapter Forty-Eight
In the treatment of wounds.
Having discussed in detail the treatment of abscesses, I shall here outline their management by manual and medicinal means, as follows: surgical procedures vary according to the nature of the wound and the part of the body it affects.
The causes of wounds are numerous, such as a blow from a stone, a sword slash, a knife cut, a spear stab, or an arrowhead, among other things.
As for wounds according to their location on the body, there are those affecting the head, neck, chest, abdomen, or liver, among other organs.
I shall now describe the treatment of certain wounds as a guide and rule for all others, beginning with simple head wounds, as their complex varieties will be discussed in the initial chapters of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head, not resulting from a bone fracture, and it is merely a contusion from an object such as a stone, and the skin is broken but there is no deep tissue damage, and one fears the development of a serious swelling, then one should promptly apply a cold compress to alleviate the pain. Do not delay this and drain the blood from him according to its strength if there is nothing preventing the phlebotomy, especially if the blood that comes out from the wound is slight, and let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinking, and heavy foods; if there is something preventing the phlebotomy, he should reduce his food intake and avoid meat and drinks, and apply a hot compress soaked in rose fat alone or with a drink that has a contracting effect.
If the hot tumor is not present, apply pressure to the wound if it is still fresh with its blood and has not been changed by the dry air that I will describe below; otherwise, use some ointments.
Until pus is formed, then treat it until healing occurs; if the wound is large, from a sword cut or similar, and its edges do not meet with sutures, then sew it up as I will describe in the stitching of abdominal wounds.
If the skin has exposed the bone and is hanging loose, with only a slight attachment to a hook, cut it off and treat the wound with medications that strengthen the flesh, replacing the skin, and instruct the patient to eat foods that are firm like heads, skulls, etc.
If there is decay in the skin that does not adhere to the bone, cut off all the decaying parts and throw it away, then treat it. If, in these wounds, a vein or a vessel is bleeding and the blood does not stop with drugs, examine the vein.
If it is affected but does not fall apart, cut it off with a scalpel and tie it. If, in the event that what we mentioned does not help, it needs to be scraped until the blood stops. If these wounds are small and simple, their treatment is easy and requires only applying this powdery substance to stop the bleeding.
Before it is affected by the air, the recipe for the powdery substance is to take a part of resin, two parts of camphor, and three parts of slaked lime or unslaked lime, grind them all together, sift them, and fill the wound with this mixture, tightening it well to ensure a good seal.
It is then sealed with blood and further secured from above with bandages. Leave it as is without loosening it until the swelling subsides, for the treatment will not leave the wound until it heals. The lime itself may be sufficient if resin and camphor are not available.
This method can also be effective with vinegar in many deep wounds if it is applied skillfully and secured, and the wounds are fresh with their own blood, unless they have been affected by air, especially during summer.
There is no effective treatment for this condition other than applying some well-prepared ointments or a porridge made from barley flour with water and honey to promote the discharge of pus, followed by treating it with all other remedies until healing occurs.
If a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound, and it is minor, then draw it with gauze, as I have mentioned the treatment for fractures of the skull in the section on neck surgery. If the wound is in the neck, there is no difference in its treatment from a simple wound to the head.
If a nerve in the neck has been cut, or an artery (if it is a nerve), there is no remedy other than avoiding treatment with anything that causes severe contraction like tongs or similar objects, as they can harm and spasm the nerve.
Never apply anything cold to the wound, as the nerve's essence is cold and its connection to the brain, which is the most noble of organs, requires gentle treatments such as warm compresses with water or oil infused with rose petals and saffron.
If you also wash it with pure water and other metallic stones in the same manner, along with moist ointments, and for a large wound, use stitching or binding it with bandages, then treat it until healing occurs.
If a wound has depth and there is an accumulation of pus at its base, drain it in the lowest point. If a vessel (artery) is severed within the wound and its blood is flowing, cut or tie it off or sew it if necessary.
If the wound has severed some or all of the pharyngeal muscles and the jugulars are intact, sew the edges of the skin together over the pharynx using a catheter (laryngoscopy) without touching the larynx. Smooth it out and return it to its natural shape, then tighten it firmly and leave it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that have a drying and constricting effect until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, open it and remove any fragments of bone that can be extracted or, if you cannot, leave it until the wound becomes gangrenous and eventually heals, making it easier to remove.
If the wound is still fresh, sprinkle it with the described powders to prevent infection. If, however, the wound becomes gangrenous and forms a fistula, treat it as described in the relevant section (on chest and shoulder surgery).
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see that it has depth, check if air comes out when the patient breathes; if so, it is a mortal wound. If there is no depth and the wound is still fresh with blood, do not immediately sprinkle it with powders or tighten it, as this may cause the blood to become trapped in its depths.
It is directed towards the heart, killing the patient, but make it with an attractive balm and dissolve it in the day twice. If a balm is not available, place a worn-out cotton ball in the wound to absorb the secretions and let the patient sleep on the wound.
If the accumulation [of pus] has occurred, and even if it has been three days or more since the wound was inflicted, and there is no spasmodic pain, no poor circulation, or difficulty in breathing, and you observe that the patient's overall condition is good, then know that the wound is healing and treat it with tourniquets and other remedies until it is cured.
If it cannot be seen and the discharge continues constantly, then know that it has become a fistula. Treat it from its opening, and if the wound is on the chest or back, treat it as mentioned before with sutures.
If it is large or involves multiple fragments, whether small or large, if it has affected the bone and cut off pieces of it, examine the wound and promptly remove those fragments if they are clean. If they are not clean, leave them until they heal.
If the wound becomes gangrenous, it will be easier to remove the affected parts. As for other wounds in various members, their treatment should follow the same guidelines as mentioned.
Chapter LXXXVIII.
In abdominal surgery, removal and suturing of the intestines.
The wound that exposes the abdomen may be large or small or medium, and a large one might expose the intestines or several parts of the intestine, making their reinsertion and suturing more difficult and challenging.
And while a small wound may also be difficult in another way, as it requires more effort to return the intestines to their position, if they are not immediately reinserted, they can swell and become stiff, making their insertion even more challenging; thus, the medium-sized wound is the best, as it does not present the same difficulties in returning the intestines as the larger or smaller wounds.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen has four aspects; two general sutures suitable for suturing the abdomen and other body wounds, and two specific sutures that are more suited to abdominal surgery.
I will describe each step in detail, starting with the first: if the wound is small and part of the intestine protrudes, but its reinsertion is difficult, this can be due to one of two reasons: either the wound is indeed very small as we mentioned, or the intestine has become inflated due to previous exposure to air.
If this is the case, then you should warm it by immersing a sponge or wet cloth in lukewarm water alone, or water in which rhubarb, fennel, and anise have been cooked, and pass it through the intestines until the bloating resolves.
And it may do this through a drink that has a constricting effect, which is better than water alone in resolving bloating. If you see the bloating has dissipated, moisten the intestines with water in which fennel or rice has been cooked, for it will facilitate its entrance with ease.
If, after this treatment, it does not return, make a small incision in the bandage using the instrument used to cut pipes, and the initial treatment is better if it can be accomplished. However, one only resorts to making an incision after necessity arises and all other methods have failed, and here is an image of the instrument.
It has one curved side with a limited range, while the other side is not limited. The thin end is not as sharp as the scalpel but is slightly convex. It is a tool similar to a small spear, as you will see. If the wound widens and the intestines enter, it should return to its natural shape.
Its specific position is best if possible, and if the incision is wide, especially in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, they should raise their head and chest higher than the rest of their body.
If the incision is on one side of the abdomen, always intend and aim for the side where surgery is higher in rank than the other. This is to be applied both to major surgeries and intermediate ones.
In minor surgeries, place the patient as you are able to, then have a helpful assistant hold the incision with both hands, gathering the edges and revealing them to the one who does the stitching one by one.
And this is the general method of simple stitching: take a needle or several needles suitable for the wound's size, leave a margin from the edge of the incision equal to the thickness of the thumb, and sew with one needle without threading it with thread on both edges of the skin. With the edges of the peritoneum underneath the skin, from inside to the point where it penetrates on that side, having gathered the edges of the skin and peritoneum, creating four folds, then tie with a doubled thread around the needle several times on both sides until the wound's edges are firmly sealed together.
Then, leave a margin equal to the thickness of a finger and sew with another needle, interweaving it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue doing this until you have treated all parts of the wound, using needles of varying thicknesses between the firm and the delicate.
Because very fine needles cut meat quickly, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin, they should be of intermediate softness and thickness, and the needle should not be inserted at the edge of the skin near the flesh.
To prevent the meat from tearing quickly and the wound from opening before healing, and also to avoid excessive stitching which may hinder the wound's closure, the ends of the needles should be cut off to prevent harm to the patient during sleep, and cloth strips can be attached to each side to hold the needle ends.
Leave it until you are certain that the wound has healed, and this type of stitching with needles is most effective for minor wounds, as a single needle or two may be sufficient to sew them up.
As for the technique of stitching for a second-degree wound, it involves bringing together all four edges (the skin and peritoneum) in a single pass with a needle containing a moderate length of twisted thread suitable in softness and thickness, then, once the edges have been joined, the needle is returned from the direction it started.
To ensure the thread forms a mesh across the wound, creating stitching similar to that used for bags to secure goods, and to place a space between each stitch, approximately the thickness of a small finger - this type of stitching is familiar to everyone.
And with this stitching, a surgical case was presented to me of a man who had been cut by a knife, and the breach in the surgery was more than an inch, and about two inches of the middle intestine had protruded, and the wound was located in the middle of the abdomen.
After 24 hours, I managed to reduce the intestine back into the wound, and it healed in about 15 days. I treated him until he recovered completely and lived for many years afterwards, performing all his activities, while doctors had judged that he would never recover.
It is remarkable that I did not treat it with a salve, as I was in a place where there were no medicinal supplies. I would apply old cotton to the wound twice a day and ensure it was washed with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the specific types of stitching, I will mention it as described by Galen: Start with stitching through the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside. Once the needle has passed through the skin and muscle in a straight line across the entire length of the abdomen.
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side where I inserted the needle, and passed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside in the other layer of peritoneum. Once done, I re-inserted it a second time into this same edge of the peritoneum, but this time from outside to inside.
Abandon the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and insert the needle from within to without through the other edge, and as you insert it into the peritoneum, also insert it into the edge of the omentum opposite to it, until it has entirely penetrated it.
Then, starting from this same side, trace it along the edge of the peritoneum on the opposite side, and remove the needle from the skin next to it, then insert it back into that skin, following the edge of the peritoneum on the other side with this edge from the omentum.
Remove it from the skin on his side, and repeat this process, doing so multiple times until the surgery is entirely sewed shut, with a stitching technique similar to that used for sewing fur to fur.
By sewing the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin, leaving it open on the other side until it is empty; as for the second type of stitching, which Galen also mentioned, his exact words are:
It involves sewing it in a way similar to how certain healers sew, by bringing together each part with its corresponding counterpart, naturally aligned. This is done by joining the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the omentum to its opposite edge.
The needle should be inserted into the margin of the peritoneum closest to you from the outside and pushed through solely into that margin, leaving the other margin of the peritoneum untouched. Then, the needle is withdrawn and inserted again from the outside into both margins of the peritoneum, and then withdrawn once more.
Then, it should be pushed through from inside to outside in the other margin of the peritoneum on the opposite side. This method of stitching is superior to the general, simple stitching mentioned, as this is what Galen described, and some have said that a stomach stitch can be done with just two needles. This is done by inserting a single thread and beginning with the needle from your side, pushing it through to the other side, and then inserting the other needle from the opposite side, following the same stitching method as for joining sheets.
Know that if the wound is in the middle of the belly, stitching it is more difficult than stitching other parts of the abdomen. As for treating the wound, its treatment is like that of all other wounds, for if you succeed in reaching the fresh wound with its blood before air changes it and return the intestines to their place, tracing and stitching them tightly, then apply hot cauterization.
If the wound has been altered by air, apply some preparations that you have made to it until pus forms and the stitches fall out, and the peritoneum and the peritonium heal, then treat it as you would any other wound until it heals.
If medicines are not available, apply a compress with old cotton pus and change it twice a day as taught, until healing occurs; in most cases, you will not need further treatment if the surgeries are simple, but if you fear that the wound might involve vital organs causing pain.
You should dip soft lint in warm oil or rose fat and apply it around the areas between the groin and armpit; if there is pain or putrefaction in the bowels, this often occurs, so inject a hot black astringent drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestines and become an open wound into the abdomen, know that what was thick in the intestines heals more easily, while what was thin is harder to heal.
As for the cecum, known as the "psoas," it does not heal from surgery at all, due to the abundance of its blood vessels, their thickness, the fragility of its substance, and its proximity to nerve nature; however, if the debris from the wound is fresh and moist, return it to its place, regardless of whether it's the cecum or another part of the intestine.
If it has been some time and it has turned green or black, you should tighten a thread above the place that turned black to prevent bleeding, as there are veins and arteries in the debris; then cut the tie below that point.
And make the ends of the thread attached from below the surgical incision, extending outside of it, to facilitate its removal when the wound pus drains and the wound heals, referring to the wound that occurs in the intestine, if there is a tear in the intestine, small in size.
In some cases, I have encountered individuals with a wound in the abdomen caused by a spear thrust, and the injury was on the right side of the stomach.
