However, I was concerned that a complication might occur within the patient's body, so I closely monitored them, and thankfully, no further complications arose, and their condition remained excellent. Some experienced individuals have noted that when a small tear occurs in the intestine, it should be sutured accordingly.
This involves taking a live ant with its head intact, gathering the edges of the wound together, placing the ant with its mouth open on the wound, and when it bites down, its jaws will stick, ensuring it doesn't let go. Then, another ant is placed nearby.
Continue to do this by counting several layers of fat around the wound, then close and sew it. These heads will remain attached to the intestines until they heal and there will be no harm to the patient, it is even possible to close the intestines with a thin thread.

In this, the thread of the intestine is used to close the intestines and then returned to the abdominal cavity. This type of stitching with fat and intestine is done in pursuit of success and hope.

Chapter LXXXVI.
On the treatment of the common cold and fistulas.
Know that every wound or swelling that persists and becomes a ulcer, and does not heal and continues to produce pus, is called, in general, a fistula, and we call it a 'common cold' (in this context).
It is indeed a complex, solid, white mass without pain, with a cavity like that of bird feathers.
Therefore, some have named it a "feather," and sometimes it can be moist, constantly discharging pus, and at other times the moisture may disappear. It can be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
In the classification when discussing surgical wounds, it has been stated that every ulcer that does not heal or develop good flesh is due to one of nine reasons: either a lack of blood in the body, its poor quality, or because there is hard flesh inside or on the edges that prevents the growth of healthy new tissue. Either the ulcer is deeply infected and filthy, or it is inherently putrid with bad material, or the treatment is inappropriate, or there is a breakdown in the town due to an epidemic, or some specific condition affecting the town.
Either the ulcer is deeply infected and filthy, or it is inherently putrid with bad material, or the treatment is inappropriate, or there is a breakdown in the town due to an epidemic, or some specific condition affecting the town. To the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of a composite joint such as the hand or foot, and some extend close to a major organ, and so on.

And such signs and causes of the tumor, if you stand before them as facts, then turn to treatment with confidence, that is, to observe, for if it be so.
If the fistula appears near or in a healthy place, far from a joint or nerve or artery or vein or any of the places mentioned above, then make an incision in the fistula as described above and remove the necrotic tissue and excessive flesh.
And if any flesh or papilloma grows in its mouth, treat it until it heals. If the fistula is deep and straight, make a deep incision into it as much as possible, then clean it of all necrotic flesh.
Then use the foul-smelling drugs in hot applications and insert them into the depth of the fistula, which you can reach with iron, do this multiple times until that sharp drug consumes all the remains of the corruption in the fistula's bottom.
Then force it with ointments that promote tissue regeneration until it heals. If it does not heal in this way, cauterize it as described earlier. If the cause of the fistula is a bone and this is confirmed, examine it and make an incision as I have instructed you, unless prevented by some obstacle.
If it reveals a nerve, vein, or main member as we said, and the bone is exposed with some corruption and blackening, remove it until the corruption disappears, then treat it with what binds the flesh until it heals. If it does not heal and pus continues as before.
Know that you have not completely removed the corruption, so uncover everything once more and examine and purify it with all your effort. If it heals, then know that this corruption is deep and far from other corruptions, so uncover it again.
If possible, examine and purify it with all your effort. If it is a small corrupt bone that can be pulled out, then use delicate hooks suitable for the task.
If there are many bones, examine how to draw them all without effort, and do not leave any behind. If something prevents you from drawing them, such as a ligament, and cutting it poses no danger, then cut the ligaments and flesh to free the bones.
If it is not possible to remove the bones at once, then apply something to the wound that will preserve it from putrefaction and leave it for several days until the flesh and ligaments around the bones rot. Take care to keep the wound open so that the gap you created does not heal.
Instead, if you are concerned about this, apply a cotton ball soaked in powdered sulfur with oil or a cotton ball soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. If these ligaments rot and the bones become loose, it will be easy to remove them.
If it is a single large bone, such as the tibia or femur, and only the face of it is corrupt, then remove it thoroughly until that darkness and corruption disappear. Then close the wound. However, if a large part is corrupt and the corruption has reached the marrow of the bone, then it must be split open.
Cut it entirely at the point where corruption ends, then treat it until it heals, and I will tell you of a case that befall a man in his leg to serve as an example and aid to your treatment; this man was young.
Around thirty years old, he experienced pain in his leg due to a movement within the body, causing the materials inside to reach the leg and swell greatly. It was not caused by anything external, and as time passed with incorrect treatment by doctors, the swelling opened up.
Much material drained from it, and it was poorly treated, causing the leg to become gangrened with numerous openings that exuded pus and bodily fluids. It was treated by a group of doctors for about two years, but there was no skilled surgeon among them.
He came to me, and I saw the leg with great drainage from these openings, and the man's body had wasted, and his skin had turned yellow. I inserted a probe into one of these openings, which reached the bone, then I examined all the openings.
I found that they opened into each other from all sides of the leg, so I incised and split one of these openings to expose some of the bone. It was rotten, consumed, blackened, and putrefied, with holes that went through to the marrow, which I exposed.
Removing a Decayed Bone: I believe that the only corruption of the bone is what I have cut and spread, and having excised it, I then applied healing remedies to prolong the healing process. However, it did not heal, so I reopened the wound to examine the bone once again.
I found the corruption attached to the bone, so I spread what was visible of that corruption, and then tried to force it to heal, but it did not mend or fuse. I then examined it again, and also removed small pieces of the bone, attempting to heal it, but even after cutting off about an inch of the bone, it still would not heal.
I then extracted it with its core and healed it with remedies, which fused quickly and cured him. This process needed to be repeated due to the patient's frail condition, lack of endurance, and the risk of death, as he would often suffer from severe fainting spells caused by excessive vomiting.
And the flesh grew firmly in place of the bone, and its condition improved in his body, and his strength returned, and he was able to move about as usual, without any further issues affecting his walking. If a new bone forms in a broken spot, it will heal securely.
One should proceed as follows: take a bandage, tighten it around the new bone, and have someone pull it upwards, creating another bandage made of thicker wool than the first.
Then, bind it to the flesh beneath the bone and extend its end to draw the flesh downwards while exposing the flesh in the area you wish to cut, so as not to harm the saw with the flesh, and place a board or a plank underneath the bone firmly from below.
Because if you do this, the saw will not be hindered from cutting the rotten bone, and the cut should be slightly above the area of rot to avoid further corruption in the bone cavity, which may not be visible to the eye, requiring a repeat cut.
If the bone is rotten and not separated but connected, with rot in its center or parts, then expose the flesh on all sides, place a board underneath, and cut from the first side where the rot is.
Once you have finished cutting, cut from the other side, keeping the cut slightly away from the rot as mentioned earlier. If the rot is in a joint, then cut off the rotten joint itself and cut the bone where it connects on the other side.
If the rot affects the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but to remove it. If the rot is in a finger or toe joint, its treatment is very difficult, but you should peel away the rot as best as possible and clean and expose the healthy bone.
By what means can you succeed, if a vessel or nerve does not impede you? Chapter LXXVIII.
On Amputations and Bone Disarticulation.
The extremities may rot either from a cause outside or inside, and if this corruption is treated with medicinal remedies but does not improve, and you see the corruption spreading throughout the member without restraint, then there is no cure.
It is necessary to cut off the member where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved from death or a great affliction greater than the loss of the member; and a sign that this has occurred is when the member turns black as if it were burned by fire.
Or the corruption may turn black, and this decay spreads to what follows that member, taking over the body, so hurry to cut it off; likewise, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of certain creatures like a sea scorpion, or a snake, or a spider, or the like.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of a finger, cut off that finger without delay; and if it has spread to the hand, then cut it at the wrist joint, so as not to allow the corruption to spread further until it reaches the forearm.
And if, when taking the arm, you cut off the arm at the elbow in the same joint, if the damage is permissible and you see it extending towards the shoulder, never cut off the shoulder; for that would cause the patient's death, and use other treatment methods to the best of your ability.
And the same goes for the foot if decay spreads to a finger, cut off that finger at one of the joints; and if it affects the sole of the foot, cut off the entire foot, but if it reaches the knee, cut off the leg at the joint of the knee.
If the decay has reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to leave it and let the patient accept death; for the treatment of cutting off or amputating a member, tie a ligature below the point where you intend to cut, and another ligature above that point.
The attendant holding one ligature should extend it downwards, and another attendant should hold the upper ligature upwards; then, using a broad knife, remove the flesh between the two ligatures until all the flesh is exposed, and cut off or amputate, taking care to apply a piece of linen in all directions.
So that the saw does not touch the correct place, causing the patient additional pain and a hot swelling; if bleeding occurs during your work, quickly apply pressure to the site or hold some sharp materials to stop the bleeding, then return to your treatment until you are finished.
Then bind the injured member with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals, and I will tell you of an example that occurred to a man in his foot, this very condition that I describe, for there appeared on his foot a darkness accompanied by a burning sensation like fire.
This corruption was the first sign in his toe, and it spread to the whole foot, so the man decided on his own to cut it off at the joint, and it healed.
After a long time, the same corruption appeared in his index finger, so he came to me, and I treated it with the medications I had prepared for the hand after cleaning it, but the corruption did not stop and spread to the other fingers.
Until the corruption took over his hand, he called me to amputate it, but I refused, hoping to contain that infection and fearing for his life upon cutting off his hand, as his strength was diminishing, so he gave up and returned to his hometown.
I was informed that he succeeded in cutting off his hand entirely, and it healed. I have related this story to serve as a help in cases of this kind of illness and as a guide for action.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
In the treatment of abscesses and the method of injecting them with medications.
If a tumor occurs in some fleshy members and the tumor persists for a prolonged period, until it ruptures or drains, completely emptying the space it was occupying as if it were a container.
And the skin covering it is like a cloth that has become loose and was not deeply corrupted, affecting any bone, nerve, or ligament; hence, it is called an abscess, not a fistula, unless the duration of its corruption prolongs to affect any of these members.
At such a point, it is called a fistula or ulcer, and its treatment involves cutting off all that skin completely, as it flakes off, especially if it has become thin and fragile and no longer adheres to the wound due to its corruption.
If you wish the skin to adhere, but it has not yet reached that level of corruption and there is still thickness of flesh, treat it with injection; that is, examine the abscess. If it is large and the pus emanating from it has a foul smell, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
Which is a mixture of vinegar, oil, honey, and ginger in equal parts, boiled together over fire until the medicine thickens like honey; then take as much as needed, dilute it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess, sealing its opening and leaving the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then, remove it with an era (potter's tool) and do so for several days until the infected area is cleansed and the bad odor disappears. If this medicine is not available, you can use ashes of date palm wood or oak wood, mixed with water.
Then, strain it and inject it until you are certain that the infected area has been cleansed. If neither Egyptian medicine nor ash water is available, inject a mixture of water and honey with some powdered ginger.
Or, you can inject a mixture of honey and drink because honey purifies and washes, while the drink sticks to the infected area, especially if it contains residual drying and astringent properties. If you do this several times and are certain that the infection has gone, then inject a remedy that promotes tissue growth.
For example, take some palm butter and mix it with rose fat and astringent drink, or inject it with other medicines and ointments that we have proven their properties in the article on ointments. If the opening of the infection is narrow and does not accommodate the syringe needle.
If the opening is narrow, expand it slightly with iron, or insert a small, contaminated string in Egyptian ointment or soriconic ointment to make it wider. If the opening is also wide, sew its edges together and leave enough of an opening that the syringe can enter without excess.
If the mouth through which pus escapes is elevated, make an incision in the lowest part to allow the pus to flow downwards, for if pus accumulates in the depth of the wound, it prevents tissue regeneration.
If it is not possible to make an incision in the wound to allow pus to flow downwards, then form the tissue around the wound so that the pus can escape easily, and ensure that no pus accumulates within it.
As for the healing drugs that are applied to the wound from outside, take one of the adhesive pastes suitable for the purpose and apply it to a cotton cloth, ensuring it covers the entire wound. Then, using scissors, make a hole in front of the wound's opening, slightly wider than the wound itself. Apply a soft paste to another cotton cloth, covering the entire area, and place it over the opening, ensuring it is spaced apart from the wound's edge. Remove the large cloth only after some time has passed.
For several days, the cloth covering the wound's opening should be changed regularly to allow any accumulated pus to escape and to observe the discharge, determining its quantity and maturity.
In addition to the above, examine the wound itself for any pain or discomfort experienced by the patient, and look for signs of swelling or abscess formation. If the treatment continues as described and the amount of discharge at the wound's opening is minimal, then consider the healing process complete.
Know that the abscess has adhered or is about to adhere, then dissolve it on the second or third day. Once you have opened the wound, replace the dressing with a new one and apply fresh ointment. If after a long time a thin pus emerges from the abscess, do not despair of its healing.
Its healing is near, for there are people whose flesh takes a long time to heal in their wounds, and others the opposite. If after many days a non-mature pus emerges from the abscess, know that the abscess has not adhered, if its healing is slow.
If the condition prolongs, increase the drying medications, and let them be in a consistency that is moist and strong like the nettle ointment if it has been enriched with more of the distillate. The nettle ointment is the most effective treatment.
You may substitute oil of rose, sprinkled with a moderate amount of ancient drink (a mild liqueur), mixed together and applied. If neither nettle ointment nor honey is available, you can prepare a mixture by boiling salt, pepper, and galangal, then crushing them all and adding them to the honey while it is still on the fire. Then apply it to a bandage and secure it over the abscess, or coat it with the thickened honey you have cooked, sprinkle medications on top, and cover it with a bandage, securing it tightly.
In such cases, one may use long ziraund, the root of the sweet violet, and fine mistletoe, either alone or combined, crushed and sifted over honey as described and used. If the condition persists after this treatment, know that there is corruption within.
It may affect bones, or other solid nerves, or ligaments, and its treatment is similar to that of fistulas, as mentioned before.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
In the treatment of corns, crushed nails, amputation of surplus fingers, and incising fused fingers.
A corn is an excess of flesh that grows under the thumb or other fingers' nails, and if left untreated and neglected, it becomes a swollen, hot tumor.

Chapter Ninety
In the removal of varicose veins.
Varicose veins are thick, twisted vessels filled with dark blood, most commonly found in the legs, especially in the thigh, calf, and ankle areas. Initially, one should employ a strong rubbing of the body to remove the dark blood.

Chapter 91.
Removing the venous flux.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot countries like Hijaz and Arab lands, and in arid, infertile regions with scarce rainfall, and it may also be generated in other parts of the body, not just the legs, due to putrefaction occurring under the skin.
As in living bodies, worms and parasites occur within, it is necessary to pound and remove the worms that develop between the skin and flesh. The beginning of this condition is marked by a severe inflammation in the leg, followed by a discharge at the site, after which the sweating begins from that point.
If a plant or animal grows from it, when its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead, weighing between one and two drams, and then tied, leaving the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it, wrap it in lead and tie it. If it continues to grow for a long time, cut off some of it and wrap the rest, but do not cut it from its root before it has completely emerged, as cutting it too soon may cause it to retract and enter the flesh, leading to swelling, pus, and a bad ulcer.
For this reason, it should be gently pulled until it completely exits the body, leaving no trace. In some people, this condition may cause a growth as long as five or ten handspans.

Chapter Ninety-Two
In the incision on the worm generated under the skin, called the cow's disease.
This disease is called "cow's illness" in some of our countries, as it often affects cattle, and it is a small worm that develops between the skin and flesh, moving up and down throughout the body.
It is perceived by the sense of touch as it moves from one part to another, until it breaches the skin at a certain point and exits, forming abscesses due to the putrefaction of certain humors, as occurs with worms, snakes, and pimples in the abdomen.
The harm caused by this condition is such that if it moves throughout the body and reaches the head, then to the eye, it may open it up and exit, causing permanent damage to the eye. This occurs frequently. If one wishes to treat it and extract the worm, it must be done while it is moving and can be felt.
It is necessary to tighten what is above and below it with a strong ligature, then make an incision on it and extract it. If it sinks into the flesh and you do not find it, then apply heat to the place with fire until it burns, for the greatest fear of damaging the eye as we have said.
If you see it has moved to the head near the eye, tighten under it on the forehead tightly, then make an incision and extract it. The patient should maintain the purification of their body with drugs that facilitate the elimination of putrid, bad humors and be cautious with foods that cause putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
On the incision for the condition known as "nafar.
This condition, known in our land as "nafar," is a pain that appears in certain members and then transfers from one member to another. I have witnessed it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in a certain desert region, where I examined her arm and saw a slight swelling in the veins of the forearm. After remaining there for an hour, I observed that swelling spreading along the arm towards the elbow with incredible speed, similar to mercury.
If the pain shifts from one place to another, it disappears from that location and stabilizes in the shoulder, then I sat for an hour, after which it spread throughout the body until it reached the other arm. She told me that her entire body was turning as I observed.
I was amazed at the speed with which it moved from one part of the body to another, and I had never before seen this disease in such a manner or to such an extent; however, I had observed groups of people experiencing pain that would shift from one member to another, but not as I saw it in this woman.
I only realized that this could be due to the woman being from a desert region, with dry skin and visible veins, which is why the sensation of the moving air was perceptible. This observation does not apply in the same way to those living in comfort, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it and the patient feels pain, then the pain will manifest as we described, so apply pressure above and below with speed and make incisions until the trapped air is released, then bandage the wound.
If you do not see it, treat the body with things that drive away air and open up the pores, such as camphor, turpentine, and other similar medicines.
Chapter ninety-four.
In Removing Arrows
Arrows may vary according to their types and the positions they occupy in the body. As for their variation by type, there are large and small ones, with hollow and solid glass, some with three angles and others with four.
Some have blades, and others have fragments. According to the organs they penetrate, they can be divided into two types: those that affect vital, hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lung, kidney, intestine, bladder, and similar organs. If an arrow penetrates one of these organs and you observe the signs of death that I will describe, it is best to avoid removing it; for in most cases, death will follow. If these bad signs do not appear and the arrow has not become lodged deep within the organ, remove it and treat the wound. As for the brain, if a bone-tipped arrow penetrates it and injures the peritoneum covering the brain, severe headaches, vertigo, and dizziness may occur.
If these bad signs do not appear and the arrow has not become lodged deep within the organ, remove it and treat the wound. As for the brain, if a bone-tipped arrow penetrates it and injures the peritoneum covering the brain, severe headaches, vertigo, and dizziness may occur. Redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, stiffness, mental confusion, spasms, and bitter vomiting. Blood may issue from the nostrils or ears, speech may become confused, and a white, mucus-like discharge may exit from the wound, akin to congealed meat juice.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not been extracted. As for the sign of the arrow reaching the heart, being close to the left breast, and feeling as if it had become lodged in a hard object rather than in empty space.
If the arrow's movement resembles a pulse and black blood flows from the wound, followed by cold extremities, cold sweat, and fainting, know that death is inevitable. And if the arrow wounds the lung, frothy blood emerges from the wound.
If the vessels below the neck swell, and the patient's color changes, and they breathe heavily and request cold air, then if the arrow has pierced the pleura in the chest, it is close to the small ribs, and breathing becomes extremely difficult with intense pain.
The patient sighs, and all the organs in the rib cage move. If the arrow has pierced the liver, intense pain follows, and blood similar in color to the liver is discharged from the wound. If the arrow has hit the stomach, food that is undigested may emerge from the wound.
The case is clear: if the arrow has struck the abdomen and become lodged there, or if it has exited through a wound, a rupture, or an intestine, there is no hope for treatment, nor can the arrow be removed. However, if the arrow has pierced the bladder and urine exits, there is still hope.
If a protrusion appears and the pain intensifies on the patient, know that death is imminent. As for the rest of the members, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar members, they are generally safe.
When the arrow does not hit an artery or nerve and is not poisoned, I will share with you some observations on these arrows to guide your treatment, for I have seen a case where an arrow struck a man in the eye socket at the base of the nose.
He removed the arrow from the opposite side below the earlobe, and it healed without any harm to his eye. I also extracted another arrow that had struck a Jew in the eyelid below the lower lash, and only a small tip of the arrow remained embedded in the wood.
It was a large arrow from a composite bow, made of square iron without barbs, and it healed the Jew without any eye complications. I also removed another arrow from the throat of a Christian; it was an Arabic-style arrow.
This arrow had barbs, and I cut it out between the ribs, as it had pierced his throat. The Christian survived, and I carefully extracted the remaining arrow from his abdomen, but he was still considered close to death.
When the arrow had remained embedded for about thirty days without any change in its condition, I cut it open and removed it, and he recovered without any subsequent ill effects. I also observed a man with an arrow in his back, which had healed over.
After seven years, the arrow emerged from the hip joint. I also observed a woman with an arrow in her abdomen; the wound healed over, and the arrow remained without causing any harm to her daily activities.
I also observed another man with an arrow in his face, which healed and did not cause him much pain. There are many such cases. I removed an arrow from a commander-in-chief that had pierced his nose, slightly leaning to the right.
The arrow completely disappeared, and I was called to treat it after three days. I found the wound from the arrow very narrow; using a thin probe, I did not feel it, but he experienced prickling and pain under his right ear from the wound.
I assumed that the prickling was due to the arrow's tip, so I applied a compress with strong adhesive to draw and mature the swelling, hoping that the area would become swollen and reveal the arrow's track. However, nothing happened, and no signs indicated that the arrow had reached the location.
I left the bandage in place for many days, and no complications arose; during that time, the wound healed, but the patient remained bedridden and hopeful of its removal until one day he felt a sharp pain inside his nose. Upon hearing this, I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for several days.
Until it opened and I examined it, I felt the fine tip of the arrow embedded in the wood. Then, I continued to open the wound with that sharp medicine until the arrowhead appeared visibly and stayed with me for a period of time, about four months.
Then, as the wound widened and I was able to insert hooks into it, I pulled and moved them, but they did not come out. So I continued to manipulate and devise ways with various tools until one day I caught them firmly with hooks, as shown in the final door image.
And the doctors judged that his nose cartilage would not heal, so I closed it, and the wound healed completely, without anything hurting him at all. And I will tell you how to remove some arrows, which will serve as a measurement and proof for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts and details of this craft cannot be fully described in words or captured in a book; rather, the skilled craftsman measures with precision, applying what is present to what is absent, and devises new work and tools upon encountering rare occurrences.
If you withdraw from this art, I say that arrows leave the body from the members in which they are embedded in two ways: either by being drawn from the place where they entered, or from the opposite direction. They exit as they entered.
If the arrow is embedded in a hot spot, it will be drawn out; if it does not come out on its own, you should leave it for several days until the flesh around it rots, making it easier to pull out and remove, especially if it is lodged in a bone.
If it does not come out after a few days, you should also leave it for some time and then try to pull it and move it every day; it will eventually come out. If it still does not come out after several days, you should drill around the arrow in the same bone with a gentle drill on all sides to widen the opening and then pull and remove it.
If the arrow is embedded in the skull bone and has penetrated one of the brain cavities, and symptoms like those mentioned above appear, stop pulling the arrow and leave it until the condition improves after several days.
In the case of an arrow that is deeply embedded and does not come out when pulled, use the drill around the arrow as described, then treat the site until it heals. If the arrow has withdrawn into a part of the body, then proceed accordingly. If sensation is absent, examine it with a probe. If you feel it, draw it out with appropriate instruments. If you cannot do so because of the wound's narrowness or because the arrow is deeply embedded and there are no bones, nerves, or vessels nearby.
Make a cut until the wound widens and you can grab the arrow. If it has ears (i.e., is embedded in tissue), release the flesh stuck to it from all sides using any means possible. If you cannot do so, break and untwist the ears to free it.
If you attempt to extract the arrow from any position, use your hand with pliers in all directions until you free it. Handle it gently to avoid breaking the arrow, which would make extraction more difficult. If you cannot do so within your time frame.
Leave it for several days until the flesh around it decays. Then, treat it again; at this point, it will be easier to extract. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments mentioned earlier, but be careful not to cut any vessels, nerves, or tendons and employ other means instead.
In Every Way, You Can Remove the Arrow with Delicacy and Patience, Securing It as Described, and When Drawing Out the Arrow, Position the Patient As It Was When the Arrow Entered, for This is More Beneficial.
If This Is Not Possible, Use Whatever Means You Can, and As for the Arrow Emerging from the Opposite Direction, It Either Protrudes Something Outside or You Feel the End of the Arrow Close to the Skin's Surface, Emerging from Above.
Make a Cut on It, As Much as the Claw Will Allow, Then Pull; It Will Be Easier to Come Out. If It Sticks to a Bone, Twist Your Hand in a Circle Until the Arrow Affects and Expands the Bone for Its Exit, Then Pull.
If It Does Not Come Out After Some Days, Re-examine and Treat It Until It Does; If the Arrow's Barbed End Is Still Inside, Pushing It Out with a Matching Instrument Is Effective. Insert This Instrument into the Arrow's Shaft and Drive It Out.
If the Arrow Is Hollow, Use an Instrument Fitting Inside to Push It Out; This Facilitates Its Exit. If the Arrow Is Poisoned, Cut Off the Flesh Containing All the Poison if Possible, Then Treat the Wound Appropriately.
If an arrow has penetrated the chest, abdomen, bladder, or side and is close to what can be felt with a probe, make an incision and be careful not to cut any blood vessels or nerves, then remove it.

In the draining of veins.
The veins that are usually drained in the body number thirty, with sixteen in the head, the two pulsating veins behind the ears known as the sensitives, and the two arteries in the temples, visible, and the two veins at the base of the eyes known as the lookers.
The sweat that stands out in the middle of the forehead, and the sweat at the tip of the nose, and the two glands on the neck, and the two veins on the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins on the lower lip, these are known as the "jharak" (veins), and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins that discharge in the arm and hand, there are five, one of which is the 'qifal' (biceps vein), located on the lateral side, commonly known as the 'vein of the head'. The akhol (median vein) is the middle vein, composed of a branch from the basilic vein and another from the qifal, often referred to as the 'body vein'.
The basilic vein, located on the lateral side (in this case, the human side), is also known as 'al-ibti' (axillary vein) and commonly referred to as the 'vein of the abdomen' or 'the tendon of the arm'. It attaches to the ulna and appears prominently above the thumb.
This is the vein located between the ring and pinky fingers, with two branches. In the leg and foot, there are three veins: one beneath the knee cap on the lateral side, a second (the popliteal vein) at the ankle on the medial side, and a third (the tibial vein) at the heel on the lateral side.
In the other leg, there are three veins similar to these: The veins behind the ears are beneficial for draining chronic effusions, migraines, ringworm, and persistent head sores. I will describe how to drain them accordingly.
It is that you shave the head of the patient and rub his nape with a coarse cloth, rubbing it well, then strangle the patient's neck with a turban until the veins become visible, located behind the ears in the lower parts of the head. Inspect them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse beneath your finger, mark them with ink.
Then take a scalpel, the one known as the 'lever', and insert it under the vein in the skin until the blade reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin above, cutting the vein along with the skin, making a clean amputation, with a length of about two joined fingers.
In this manner, it sends the desired amount of blood, then ties them with bandages and leaves them until healing occurs. You have previously discussed in the first part of the book how to cut and bind them. Chapter 69
On cupping and its application.
The cupping cups may be made of horns, wood, copper, or glass. There are two types of cupping: one for drawing blood (with a specific mechanism) and the other for non-invasive treatment.
This non-invasive cupping therapy consists of two methods.
Surgeries of the Neck, Chin, Shoulders, Hip, Arms, Legs, and Ankle.
Lymphatic drainage is the primary function of cupping, which draws blood from the delicate veins scattered throughout the flesh. Therefore, the strength of cupping should not be used to induce vomiting in conditions characterized by edema, and cupping should not be applied with or without heat in any disease that causes excessive swelling.
If cupping is necessary due to illness or as a habit, it should be performed at any time of the month, whether the first, last, or middle, and at any hour of the day.
Some people, when their blood volume increases to the point where cupping is required to remove it, experience a sensation of heaviness and headaches. Others may notice swelling, redness in the face, neck, and head, or itching on the face, sides, and eyes, or darkness and pain in those areas. Some even feel itching at the site of the cupping.
Some individuals may experience increased laughter, a metallic taste in their mouths with bleeding gums, excessive sleeping, or vivid dreams featuring blood, redness, injuries, and similar imagery. These symptoms should be considered when determining the appropriate timing for cupping therapy.
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we command cupping after two or three hours have passed from noon. The benefit of cupping on the point is that it alleviates the heaviness in the head and releases the water flowing to the eyes.
However, this should be done after the body has entirely emptied. This cupping can be a substitute for draining the brain, but it is advisable to avoid using it for those with cold-blooded dispositions or those suffering from a fever, as it may cause severe harm.
Therefore, the elderly or those with cold-related conditions in their heads should avoid using it, as well as anyone who frequently uses it, as it may lead to memory loss.
Therefore, the cupper should lower their hand with the cup slightly to prevent memory loss. Cupping on the nape is a substitute for draining the eye sockets and the basilic vein.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, headaches, coughing, and chest congestion. The cupping on the nape should be slightly raised, as placing it too low may cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping on the temple areas is effective for headaches, conjunctivitis, migraines, throat pain, and toothache. It serves as a substitute for draining the basilic vein. The cupper should avoid going too deep with their hand to prevent cutting into a blood vessel, which may cause bleeding.
And as for leeching beneath the chin, it is beneficial for cavities in the mouth and corruption of the gums, and similar diseases of the mouth, and may serve as a substitute for fistulation of the hemorrhoids on the lips; and leeching of the shoulders is beneficial for palpitations due to fullness and heat.
And as for leeching of both zendis (a term referring to specific anatomical areas), it is beneficial for what fistulation of the three veins â basilic, acetabular, and sagittal â accomplishes, as it draws blood from all those delicate veins in the flesh and draws those delicate veins from other, thicker veins below them until the attraction reaches the three thick veins.
The leechman should not excessively suck, for the area is devoid of flesh and beneath lie nerves and arteries; however, a single leech applied to the anus (or 'epixys') is beneficial for hemorrhoids and ulcers in the lower regions.
You should instruct the leechman to use a large, copper leech, as the area requires a strong suction, and a glass leech may break easily; moreover, leeches for the legs are insufficient in their sucking ability.
For they draw blood from the entire body and are beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, wombs, and bladders, induce menstruation, and cure pustules and abscesses, serving as a substitute for fistulation of the sinews and tendons, albeit they exhaust the body greatly and fainting occurs in most people; leeches for the tendons have nearly the same benefit as those for the legs.
The Method of Applying Catheters: Place the catheter first while empty, suctioning gently and not prolonging the application. Quickly insert and remove the catheter to allow the humors to enter the site evenly. Repeat this process repeatedly until you observe the site turning red and opening up.
When the redness of blood appears, tie it and gradually increase suction, then observe the condition of the bodies. If a person has soft flesh with porous pores, you should apply only one catheter to avoid ulceration at the site.
The surgeon should widen the tie, deepen it slightly, and adjust the suction gently and carefully. If the blood is thick, the catheter should be tied twice: first to open the way for the gentle flow of blood and its liquids, and second to completely remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, repeat the tie a third time to achieve the desired effect. In general, if you want to remove a small amount of blood, one tie is sufficient. If you aim to extract a large quantity of blood, use more ties, especially if it is thick.
One should tie a deep catheter with a moderate suction, only reaching the depth of the skin. No fat or oil should be used when applying catheters, nor should one use excessive water. This is what the surgeon should avoid. However, for those with thick, hard, dry skin and narrow pores, more ties and stronger suction may be required.
In summer, the incision sites should be lubricated with open, softening, and dissolving fats such as khayri (a type of fat), rose petal fat, sweet almond fat, or pumpkin seed oil.
However, in winter, they should be lubricated with fat from daffodils, lilies, chamomile, or narcissi, and if the excess is thick and cold, use cinnamon fat, nutmeg fat, panax fat, or mentha fat instead.
If the incision is wide, fresh, and fleshy, one should avoid applying fat. These individuals should wash their incisions after cupping with rosewater or cold water, grapevine water, cucumber water, or horsetail water, etc., especially if they have an abundance of moisture in their blood.
They should wash their incisions with vinegar or water mixed with sumac, etc. For those with thick excess, washing with old wine, marjoram water, mint broth, or chamomile, etc., is recommended. One must be cautious about performing cupping in the bath or immediately after bathing.
Instead, it is recommended to perform cupping one to two hours after bathing. No one should sleep immediately after cupping, and both the patient and practitioner should take precautions before and after the procedure.
If the patient has jaundice with blood characterized by sharpness and inflammation, then cooling agents such as pomegranates, dandelion, vinegar, lettuce, skullcap, and similar plants should be given, along with a diet of frugal meals, mutton, and fruits like sumac and others.
For those with a cold temperament, honey drink, mib (or skullcap) drink, and moderate perfumed wine between old and new should be given. They should be advised to eat less and consume frugal meals of artichokes, wild cabbage, sparrows, and pigeon chicks as purifiers. On the day of cupping or leeching, the liquid intake should surpass food consumption.
In some cases, certain people should be given a purgative such as the distinguisher elixir or musk medicine or silitha before cupping or leeching to strengthen the vital organs and purify the blood. However, it should not be given to those in a feverish state.
Cupping procedures that are unconditional are those applied to the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidneys, and hip bone. These areas cannot endure any conditioning, but the purpose of these cuppings is either to draw blood from one organ to another.
When we apply cupping to the breasts for conditions like nosebleed, or to release a cold, stagnant atmosphere from an organ, similar to placing cupping on the abdomen and navel, it loosens the organ, warms it, and alleviates pain by expelling that stale air.
If applied to the kidneys, with a blockage or stone present, the pressure may open the blockage or dislodge the stone. Similarly, when placed on the liver and spleen during a wind disturbance, these vessels can be used solely through suction.
It may also be used with fire or filled with warm water in cases of constipation. The vesica should be filled with hot water alone, or with water that has been boiled with certain herbs suitable for the purpose.
Then, filled with the mixture, it is applied to the affected area, gripped, removed, and repeated as needed. This is an illustration of the vesica used with fire:
It has a capacity equivalent to two fingers when opened, approximately half a span in depth, and features a small hole on one side, about the size of an needle, made from brass or yellow copper with a thick edge.
Smooth, even, and polished to prevent injury to the organ when placed upon it, with a central rod of copper or iron to hold the candle for heat treatment. Such a vesica can be made larger or smaller depending on the condition.
The skill of the user is crucial, as the vesicas for boys and thin individuals differ from those for men. For these purposes, a small wick or candle made from flax or pitch is lit and placed on the central rod within the vesica.
To ensure the fire does not reach the patient's body, it should ascend towards the lower part of the flask. Then, place the flask over the member and place a finger on the hole we mentioned. So, when we grasp the flask, we do not need to remove the finger.
The steam escapes through the hole, and the flask adheres to the member. Then, bring the wick to the surface and repeat as needed. For the flask used in treating the condition known as "shusah" with water, it does not have a rigid rod or hole.
It is only used by filling it with water and placing it over the member, and this is its appearance: .
The larger the flask, capable of holding much water, the better it is.
Chapter Seventy-Nine
On the treatment of hemorrhoids.
The treatment known as "al-'allaq" (the leech) is primarily used in cases where the application of flasks (mayh al-kamm) is not feasible, either due to the small size of the area, such as the lip, gums, and similar regions, or because the member is exposed without any covering of flesh, like the finger, nose, and so on.
Method of Using It: Take leeches from clean, fresh water free from impurities, and leave them in the water for a day and night until they become starved, with nothing left in their bodies. Then, the body is first purged through leeching or cupping, and then the affected member is washed.
Until it turns red, then apply it. If it becomes saturated, it will fall off; if possible, suck the area with a cupping glass for greater benefit. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar and then with plenty of water, massage and squeeze. If the bleeding persists after the leech falls off, soak a cotton cloth in cold water.
Place it on top until the bleeding stops. If there is excessive blood, sprinkle powdered gypsum, or rhubarb, or similar astringents until the bleeding ceases, or apply half of a dried and peeled calamus root.
Leave it until the calamus root sticks to the area, and the bleeding will stop. If leeches need to be re-applied, do not use the same leech if possible; if they refuse to adhere, clean the area with fresh blood.
Or, pierce the area with a needle to draw out some blood, and then apply it. If it feels for something, it will stick to the surface; if you want to remove it, sprinkle dust of frankincense or salt or ash on it, and it will fall off.
Chapter Three
In algebra.
This chapter also deals with the necessary skills in surgery, particularly the reduction and fixation of fractures in bones.
Know, O sons, that this chapter is the domain of physicians and the common people; whoever has not perused a book or read a letter from the ancient texts in it.
For this reason, the knowledge of this art has become non-existent in our country, and I have never encountered a proficient person in it. I have only gained from it what I have learned through lengthy study of the texts of the ancients and my diligence in understanding them, until I extracted the knowledge of that from them; then, I devoted myself to experience and practice throughout my life.
I have outlined for you in this chapter all that my knowledge encompasses on the subject. My experience with it has been reflected upon you, after bringing it close and refining it from unnecessary details, summarizing it to its essence, and clarifying it to the utmost extent. As it is from further explanation as I did in the preceding chapters, and strength is only with the Most High, the Great.
Chapter One.
In this chapter, after presenting general principles regarding bone fractures and dislocations, before discussing specific broken and dislocated members one by one, it is imperative to initially mention certain key statements and sections. You must first understand and comprehend their true nature, whether you are yourself inclined to learn this noble art, or others who seek to do so.
Therefore, I say that when a bone is broken, dislocated, sprained, or there is a fall, you must first rush to sever or straighten it, or both. If there is no impediment such as weakness of strength, or if what occurred is a young child or an old, frail person, or if the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, one should restrict their diet to cold legumes, bird meat, and venison, while avoiding drinks, hearty meats, overeating, and any food that fills the veins with blood, until the swelling is relieved and there is no risk of any discharge to the site.
When the patient has returned to his initial treatment, if the broken bone is set, the patient should be given foods that are rich, thick, and nutritious, such as porridge, rice, meat, and cow entrails. Eggs, soft fish, thick drinks, and similar foods help speed up the healing process of fractures and promote better union. However, keep in mind that broken bones in strong young adults or elderly individuals may never fully heal to their original state.
The fragility and hardness of their bones, and sometimes they can unite and heal, but what was once soft like the bones of young children, nature produces on the broken bone from all sides a substance similar to glue that becomes thick and sticks to it, holding them together.
They stick together, becoming as strong and secure as they were originally, so that nothing hinders their functions.
For this reason, the patient's diet should include foods that are firm, viscous, and thick as mentioned earlier. Keep in mind that fractures vary depending on the affected body part; a fracture of the leg bone differs from a fracture of the skull, and a chest bone fracture is distinct from a back bone fracture.
And likewise, all other members are contrary to one another, and we will mention each type of fracture, fully explained in its own section, without any confusion, for the type of bone fracture may also differ within itself.
Because it may be a fracture without chips, and it may be a longitudinal fracture with chips and protrusions that are either healing or not, and the fracture may occur with a wound and a breach in the skin, and it may be a slight fissure.
And each type has a specific method for its treatment, which will be detailed in its place, and among the ways to recognize a bone fracture is its deformity, displacement, and prominence, as well as its sensitivity and tremor when touched with your hand.
If there is no visible deformity, or sensitivity, or tremor when palpating the bone, and the patient does not experience severe pain, then it is not a fracture, but possibly a sprain, a minor break, or a slight fissure. Therefore, one must not attempt to move it by stretching or touching it at all.
Instead, apply the treatments mentioned later, suitable for the location, and then gently wrap it. Be aware that if a bone breaks into two pieces without chips, the fragments may have detached from each other entirely.
It is your duty to correct and set it right from the beginning, before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling does occur, leave it for some days until the heat subsides, then treat it as best you can with kindness and skill.
Beware, for setting it right and mending it is easier than a bone that has fractured with pieces separated, then it should be tightly bound as will be mentioned later; if the bone has fragments, it is necessary to extend the broken limb from both sides, whether it is a hand or a leg.
Either use your hands if the member is small, or two strings, or a string and a hand; place it on a level surface in its natural shape. Once the broken bone extends, then cut off the excess parts and reshape them as best you can with kindness and skill.
Take care not to cause pain or discomfort to the patient with your actions; make every effort to align one bone with its counterpart in the best way possible. At this time, you should touch and feel them with your hand; if you find anything amiss, correct and set it right as best you can.
Beware of excessive stretching and strong squeezing, as many ignorant people do, which often causes hot swellings or pain in the member, as you have seen from their actions numerous times.
Then, after adjusting and perfecting it, and tightening it, he commanded that member to rest and warned the patient not to move it during his wakefulness or sleep, when turning over or disturbed, or when defecating, or in all his movements, within his power, and to strive for the member to be positioned in a way that prevents pain.
If he feels pain or discomfort in a certain position, he should change it to a different one where he does not feel any pain, ensuring that the new position is level and straight.
To prevent any deformity in the member if it is bent, and regarding the way to tighten a broken member, as I have described to you, know that broken members vary in size and shape, such as the arm, finger, forearm, and the like, which can be small or large.
The bandages should be soft and flexible, and for parts like the thigh, back, and chest, which are large, they should be wide and firm, as a broad strap fastens and tightens the large member evenly, without any gaps.
Then, after finishing the adjustment, begin to apply the appropriate plaster. In a gentle compression, apply a layer of plaster on the fracture site, then start wrapping the strapping tape around the broken member, typically three or four layers, depending on the member's needs, and slightly tighten it with your hand, then move it upwards from the fracture site.
With less tension than you would apply to the broken site, then gradually separate by winding slightly away from the fracture location and loosen the tension little by little until you obtain a suitable amount from the correct position, then take another bandage and wind it as well over the broken site.
Then, wind it downwards towards the fracture, maintaining the tension and relaxation as we mentioned in the first winding, then place between the windings a soft splint or cloth to equalize the crack's deformity.
If there is a deformity, do not add anything, then wind another bandage over it and adjust the tight bandages from your set if there is no swelling or hot inflammation in the member.
If there is swelling or hot inflammation, apply what soothes the swelling and dispels the inflammation, then leave it for some days before tightening the bandages; they should be from half-widths of robust cane, carved meticulously, or made from wood shavings.
made from cedar or palm fronds, or heather and kelp, or any similar readily available materials, each bandage should be of this size and shape.
Except for the splint placed directly on the fracture, which should be slightly thicker and wider than the others, the length of each splint is determined by the size of the fractured member, as follows:
Then, you should tighten the bandages with another strip according to your initial tension, and then tie them securely from above with strong stitches as we have mentioned regarding the tension, ensuring that your tension is on the fracture site more. The further away from the fracture, the less tension should be applied.
The threads should be of medium thickness and softness, and they should be particularly wet linen threads. Avoid using thick threads as seen by the ignorant who make their stitches from rolled flax strips, which is a great error.
Because excessive tension causes deviation from the desired outcome, and thin threads are also unsuitable as they do not provide adequate tension. There should be no less than a finger's width between the bandage and the next.
If the patient is harmed by the edges of the bandages after proper tensioning, place under them soft padding or fluffy wool to prevent further damage. If there is a fracture with a wound and a breach in the skin, we will discuss that separately.
Know that not every fractured member should be bandaged from the first day. If the member is large, do not apply bandages until after five or seven days, or longer, to avoid acute swelling.
And this is the description of dressings that were used by the ancients to set broken bones, joints, and dislocations, a general and concise dressing that sets fractures and suits most conditions, especially for children and women, as it is free from heat and cold.
It is to take flour dust, the finest part of the flour that clings to the walls of the mill when it turns, and knead it as it is without sifting it with egg whites, making the dough neither too thick nor too thin, then rolling it out.

Grind all together and mix with rosewater or distilled water or paint, depending on the part's temperature and condition. According to Hippocrates, in his book, he did not mention placing anything on a broken part during its healing.
Except for the caustic made from wax and oil alone, it should be of a medium consistency between hardness and softness. Galen, however, suggested applying substances with a drying effect, such as honey, frankincense, and resin, along with some heat, when setting a broken bone.
A poultice of various ingredients, including lint, chickpeas, human hair (or bird feathers), and salt, is beneficial for weakness and pain. It should be prepared by mixing and kneading the components into a paste and applied as a compress. The amount of binding material to be used is dependent on the size of the wound.
Then, it should be evaluated. If no pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone occurs, do not remove the binding for several days. However, if there is severe itching, persistent pain, or swelling at the site, the poultice should be removed.
Act swiftly and remove the poultice at the appropriate time, under no circumstances should it be postponed. Take a soft cloth or sponge and soak it in lukewarm water, then gently clean the affected area to relieve itching and pain. Allow the member to rest for an hour afterward.
Then, apply a compress of wool soaked in vinegar and oil (or rose fat) and secure it with a light bandage for the night to prevent further swelling, alleviate pain, and reduce inflammation. Afterward, reapply a lighter binding and a thin poultice.

Then loosen it with warm water every time you remove the bandage, and do so every three days, and slightly reduce the binding... For this action causes food to flow to the member, leading to quick healing. As for what the ignorant do, breaking the member again with excessive force.

Chapter II.
On fractures occurring in the head.
The types of fractures occurring in the head are numerous, and their forms vary, while their causes are diverse. Some fractures result from a sword strike, completely shattering the bone down to the underlying dura mater. As the impact does with wood.
This type of fracture is therefore called "comet-like" (ÙØ¯ÙÙÙÙØ§), as the sword may break off a portion of the bone, severing its face but not penetrating through to the end. This is known as a complete fracture (ÙÙØ¹), and such wounds can range from minor to severe.
Some fractures are simple cracks (ÙØ´ÙÙ) or contusions (Ø±Ø¶ÙØ§), caused by striking the head with a stone or falling onto a stone, etc. These can result in either a fracture that penetrates close to the underlying dura mater or one that affects only the surface of the bone.
The wounds caused by these fractures can also be either large or small, and some fractures are subtle, hidden within the bone, appearing as a thin split, like a hairline crack.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called poetic, and some fractures occur due to a fall or the impact of a stone or similar object, causing a part of the bone to penetrate the tissue and create a depression similar to what happens when copper is struck.
This is most common in the soft bones of children's heads, and all types of fractures may have either detached or attached fragments. We will discuss the treatment of each type in its appropriate place and identify all these fracture types through examination.
Examine them with probes and remove any decayed flesh, while the poetic fracture is identified by revealing the bone and marking it with ink; the fracture will appear black. In treating a fracture, one must first consider the patient's symptoms.
If you observe symptoms that clearly indicate fear, such as vomiting bile, restlessness, loss of consciousness, hoarseness, fainting, fever, red and bulging eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat them. In these cases, death is imminent.
If you observe less severe symptoms and wish the patient well-being, then proceed with treatment. If the wounded person reaches you soon after being injured, especially during winter, one must act quickly.
It is necessary to make every effort to remove the bone before the fourteenth day, in any case. Even in summer days, it is advisable to hasten the removal of the bone before the seventh day, so as not to corrupt the membrane beneath the bone, thereby exposing those symptoms we have described.
If the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is crushing or bruising, you should cut off the broken and bruised part as I have described, which is to shave the patient's head.
When examining the bone, if you discover bleeding or a hot swelling, treat it as necessary, depending on the patient's condition and the nature of the wound.
This involves packing the wound with cloth soaked in a mixture of rose water and fat, cleaning the wound until the swelling subsides and bleeding stops. Then, you proceed to trim and extract the bone, which can be done through one of two methods.
The first method is to cut the bone using a delicate, narrow blade. Here is its illustration:
Then, use another blade that is slightly broader than the first one, as illustrated here:
Then, use another blade that is broader and sharper than the previous ones. You should have several blades of varying widths and lengths, all made from high-quality iron or steel.
Use gentleness in striking the wound to avoid dislodging the head and causing harm, for if the bone is strong and hard, it should be drilled around it before using the chisels, which they call non-sinking chisels, not sinking chisels.
Because the chisel's tip is rounded, resembling a ring or small circle beneath its sharp head, it prevents it from sinking beyond the thickness of the bone.
You should have a variety of these chisels, each suitable for the thickness of a particular bone, so that you have a chisel for every skull, with its sharp end matching the length and shortness of that skull, and here are three types of images. 1. Large chisel
And as for the method of drilling around a broken bone, it is to place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated, then transfer the drill to another location and make sure there is enough space between each hole, or something similar.
Then, cut with scissors between each pair of holes and do this as gently as possible, as we mentioned earlier, until the bone comes out either by hand or with a specialized tool.
Use tools similar to forceps and tweezers, and be extremely careful not to touch the drill or scissors with the peritoneum. Once you have strengthened the bone and freed it from the peritoneum, if it is still attached, remove it.
Smooth the roughness remaining on the bone with another tool similar to scissors, but it should be thinner and gentler. If any small fragments or shavings remain, carefully remove them with the tools at your disposal.
Then, treat the wound with bandages and ointments as mentioned. The other aspect of the work is straightforward and free from danger, as Galen described and highly praised. First, you must begin by exposing the portion of the bone where the fracture occurred. Once this section is exposed, place one end of the lens-shaped tool underneath it.
The posterior part of the lens should be smooth, not cutting anything, and the sharp part along its sides tapers towards the length as you can see, so that the posterior part rests on the peritoneum, and the face of the sharp cut is against the bone, then strike the cut from one side.
Use a small hammer to cut through the bone gently as it rotates, and be secure that the membrane is not damaged in any frightening way. Even if the healer is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even while asleep.
If anything remains necessary for the bone from the membrane in some places of the bone, scrape it off with the same lens cut itself and release it gently, for it will be freed without harm or fear.
If the bone fracture did not penetrate the membrane, and what broke off from the bone still has a rough surface with fine fragments remaining, you should smooth that surface and remove those fragments using delicate instruments of various sizes, so that you can use them in each place most suitably according to your work.
And the same broken bone and its shape, you should first use the widest instruments in your set to smooth it, then use narrower ones afterwards, consistently proceeding with increasing precision until you reach the thinnest and most delicate ones.
And as for the rest of the small cracks and delicate fractures in the skin, it is necessary to use a treatment for each according to what leads to its healing, which is something that is not hidden from anyone with even a little experience in this art who has studied our writings and explanations regarding major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when removing the bone, one should take a piece of cotton cloth suitable for the wound and dip it in rose fat (or some other suitable oil) and place it over the wound. Then take another cloth, either folded or cut into a triangle shape. Dip it in a drink and rose fat, place it on the first cloth, making this as light as possible to not burden the peritoneum. Then use a wide bandage and only tighten it enough to secure the wound.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the hot swelling subsides, then remove the bandage and apply medications that have a drying effect, such as dried iris roots, asafoetida flour, cardamom seeds, ziraund, and similar substances, by making fine powders from these drugs.
Sprinkle the wound with these dry substances, taking care to use a treatment that clears and does not cause irritation. Strive to keep the wound clean during treatment, free from fat, dirt, and ensure pus does not form.
Because when pus collects on the brain membrane, it corrupts and spoils it, causing great harm to the patient. It may occur in the skull's pericranium when the bone becomes exposed, especially if treatment is neglected.
If you observe a blackness on its surface, and the patient exhibits the symptoms we mentioned, know that death is inevitable. If the blackness occurs due to a treatment applied, that treatment's potency to cause such blackness is what leads to the patient's demise.
One should take a portion of honey and three parts of rose fat, mix them well, apply the mixture to the pericranium, and then treat the patient with all the necessary remedies until they recover.
Chapter III
In treating a broken nose.
Know that the nose is broken when both upper cracks (or one) are affected, as they are bone. The lower part is cartilage and does not break, but rather it bends, deforms, or cracks.
If one of the cracks is broken, you should insert your small finger into the nostril and straighten the break from inside with your index finger and thumb from outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. This should be done gently, taking care not to cause any further pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and the finger did not enter it, it should be smoothed with an iron rod slightly thicker than the bone, for if the fracture is on both sides, do the same, and hasten to set it on the first day of the fracture.
If possible, and otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, then enter a cotton thread into the nose hole, one thread for a single fracture or two threads for fractures on both sides.
The threads should be thick enough to fill the nose hole, and some of the first surgeons recommended greasing them with lard and changing them every day, although I do not agree with this. Instead, I suggest soaking the threads in a mixture of egg white and flour powder.
Then, leave the threads until the bone is healed and the cartilage hardens. You can also insert a duck feather pipe into the nose after wrapping it with soft cloth, which will help hold the fracture together and prevent the patient from having difficulty breathing.
This is not strictly necessary, but if you wish to prepare threads, you can do so. If a hot swelling appears during your work, apply a compress of wax or a cotton ball soaked in vinegar and olive oil, or some other diachylon ointment.
If a hot tumour is not present, it should be dressed from the outside with fine flour and powdered candelilla, mixed with egg white. Then place a soft pad on top and do not tie the nose to anything, especially if the nasal bones break in small pieces.
If the tumour breaks apart, it should be incised and removed with the appropriate instrument, then the cut should be stitched and treated with suitable healing ointments. If there is an internal nasal wound, treat it with threading and use lead pipes. so that it heals.
Chapter IV.
In the repair of lower jaw fractures.
If the lower jaw breaks and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, and it is only broken on one side, without collapsing inward, then its condition is easily recognizable.
If the fracture is on the right side, insert the index finger of the left hand into the patient's mouth and similarly, if the fracture is on the left side, insert the index finger of the right hand and gently lift the broken piece away from the inside towards the outside.
With your other hand, control its alignment from outside the bone. If a fracture of the jaw is split into two parts, it should be pulled straight from both sides to enable its alignment; if it has affected the teeth, they will straighten.
If the split is wide, use a gold, silver, or silk thread to tighten it, then apply a layer of asphalt to the broken jaw and place a folded cloth over it, followed by a large, tight splint or a piece of leather from a shoe that is the length of the beard. Then secure it from above in the best way you know how to keep it in place, ensuring it does not come undone. Instruct the patient to rest and avoid movement, providing them with soft porridge as food. If you notice any change in the shape of the jaw after three days, unfasten it immediately.
Then secure it from above in the best way you know how to keep it in place, ensuring it does not come undone. Instruct the patient to rest and avoid movement, providing them with soft porridge as food. If you notice any change in the shape of the jaw after three days, unfasten it immediately. Then, prepare a new dressing with flour from a mortar, mixed with egg white or fine semolina, after removing the asphalt. Place soft thread over the dressing and secure it with tape. As long as the dressing sticks well and does not change the position of the bone, leave it in place.
Do not remove the dressing until the fracture has healed and strengthened, as this fracture often takes three weeks to heal completely. If a hot swelling appears during this time, use the methods we have mentioned repeatedly to relieve it.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, if a piece or pieces of bone have become detached, be gentle in removing them, using whatever means are appropriate for their extraction from the body. If the opening of the wound is narrow, widen it with the scalpel as much as you need.
Then, if you have removed the shards and nothing remains, outline the wound with a line if it is wide, or apply one of the appropriate ointments to it. And stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter V.
In the treatment of a broken clavicle.
The clavicle most often breaks in the front, near the shoulder joint, and it can break in one of three ways: either it snaps into two pieces without any shards, which is the easiest to set, or it may break into three pieces with a fragment, or it may shatter into multiple pieces.
Either the fracture may occur with shards, which is more difficult to set, or it could be accompanied by a wound. If there is no wound, have two assistants help: one holds the arm adjacent to the broken clavicle, while the other gently stretches the neck towards the opposite side.
Then, align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, without any bumps or gouges. If more extension is required, place a ball of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit.
And you should make the bone as needed, extending and raising the clavicle, and pressing the ball with your hand until the fracture is aligned as it should be. If you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outward due to its deep position, the patient should lie on their stomach.
And a pillow should be placed under the patient's elbow, and the server should press the patient's elbow downward, so that the clavicle, which was deep, rises upward. Then, align and shape the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has broken off and is moving, you should pry it out gently. If the fragment is stuck in the bone, use one of the cutting tools prepared for this purpose to dislodge it. After the clavicle is secured, use an instrument to hold the abdominal cavity, which can be made of wood or iron. This is its shape: [image or description omitted as per instructions].
It resembles a spoon without serration, with a width determined by the size of the bone and a length suitable for your work, as you can see, one end is wider and the other narrower.
If the wound laceration that you have made while removing a bone fragment is wide and you have secured the hot edema, then close the wound by stitching it. If the laceration is slight or you fear the hot edema, then enlarge the wound with additional incisions and bandages according to the depth of the wound.
If there is a hot edema, moisten the wound with rose fat and vinegar or wine, and apply it. For a bone that is not fractured or cut, wrap it with a bandage made from flour and egg white. Then place a soft pad on it, and if necessary, place a ball under his armpit. Then take a long cloth, about a span wide, and wrap it around the splint and bandage. Then take a thin board as a brace. It should be three fingers wide and long, then insert it into a cloth, and then insert that cloth with the brace into the place on the wrap where the fracture is located, then tighten the wrap around the fracture. Apply it to his neck, under the correct armpit and under the affected armpit, and pull it back and forth on both sides. As you can see, the tension secures the fracture firmly, which is self-explanatory.
Be sure not to remove the brace from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day. As soon as the bandage relaxes and you see that the splint has been removed, repair it and tighten the bandage. Then make the patient sleep on his back and place a small pillow under his arm when he sleeps at night.
So that his arm may be lifted from his side, thus raising the collarbone fracture with the movement of the shoulder, or his arm may be bound to his neck. Do not loosen the bandage unless there is some change in the condition at the site after 12 days.
Then renew the bandage if you see that it is necessary. And it responds to compression, leaving it until the fracture of the collarbone heals and solidifies, becoming stronger and more powerful in 28 days. And it may be in some people in less than that.
Chapter VI.
In the treatment of shoulder fractures, it is rare for the entire shoulder to fracture at once; rather, only certain parts or perhaps a portion in the middle may break. This can be determined by touch, and according to the shape of the fracture, the treatment should be tailored accordingly.
Restoreth it to its natural form by every possible means, then apply a dusting of millstone with egg white and soft lint, and place a pad of folded cloth on top. Afterwards, apply a broad splint of a plank.
Make it as thin as possible, or slightly wider than the shoulder, and if there is a depression under the splint in one of the shoulder areas, correct that depression with soft lint until the splint rests evenly, then tighten it with a long bandage.
Tighten it securely and ensure the splint remains in place, checking the bandage daily. If it slackens, tighten it and adjust the splint as needed. The patient should lie on their healthy side, and the shoulder will heal in approximately 20 days.
After 20 or 25 days, if this period is complete, loosen the bandage with confidence, for it is a bone that is unlikely to break or crack. If a fragment does protrude and irritates under the skin, make an incision and extract it. Follow the same procedure for treating a fracture of the clavicle, applying the necessary measures to soothe any hot inflammation that may occur.
Chapter VII
In Treating Rib Fractures.
The chest may fracture in the middle, but this is rare, and its extremities are more prone to fractures and dislocation. Among its symptoms, if it fractures in the middle, it leans downwards, causing severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
If it bleeds, the deformity of the broken bone may be clearly visible to the touch. To treat it, the patient should lie on their back, with a pillow placed between their shoulders. Then, press the elbows and gently arrange the ribs from both sides, smoothing out the bone as much as possible.
