And align it until the bone returns to its proper shape. Then, apply a splint and a brace made from a thin board like willow or heather, wrapped in a cloth for comfort. Then, gently secure it to the broken bone to prevent displacement. Wrap the bandage around the back in a circular motion several times and tighten it firmly. Check the bandage periodically, adjusting it as needed, and tightening it whenever it loosens.
If, however, it needs to be removed due to discomfort, pain, or swelling in the area, dislodge the bandage, address any issues that arise during treatment, and then reapply the bandage if necessary. Face and bind it tightly until it heals.
Chapter VIII
In the case of a broken rib, know that the fracture typically occurs in the stiff parts adjacent to the back or at the ends from the front, due to compression. This is because they are cartilaginous and easily susceptible to pressure during an impact. This knowledge is readily apparent upon examination with the fingers.
Set the broken rib by aligning the fracture with the fingers on the firmest part, until the shape is even as it should be. Then bandage and bind the broken bone with a splint if necessary. If the broken ribs are tilted inward, it will cause the patient severe pain.
And the stab wound, which exposes those with a bruise, as the bone punctures the pericardium, also causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and spitting up large amounts of blood, which is difficult to treat. The ancients used many tricks in this regard.
Some said that the patient's diet should include foods that produce gas and wind, to cause abdominal distension and expansion, allowing the broken bone to push outward. We discourage this, as it may lead to the formation of a hot swelling if it hasn't already occurred, and if it has, it will only worsen and confirm its presence.
Some say that a cup should be applied to the site and then sucked out with force, similar to measurement, except that there is a fear that the cup might attract curiosity to the site due to its weakness. Some say the site should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
The dressing should be placed between the ribs until it fills, making the bandage even when wrapped around in a circular manner, and then treat the patient with the treatment of an inflammation using food and medication. If the illness is severe and exhausting, it cannot be endured. The bone pierces the peritoneum painfully, and we are concerned for the patient's condition. Therefore, we should make an incision at the site, expose the broken rib, and then place underneath the instrument that preserves the peritoneum (as described earlier) and carefully cut and remove the bone.
Then, we gather the edges of the wound if it is large and sew them together. We treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot tumor appears during this time, act quickly by soaking dressings in rose oil and applying them to the site. Treat the patient internally as well to alleviate the tumor.
The patient should lie on the side that provides the most comfort and allows for easier sleeping until recovery.
Chapter IX
In the treatment of spinal and neck injuries.
However, if the cervical bones are fractured and such injuries are rare, they are most commonly caused by a blow or impact, and similarly, the vertebrae of the spine. If you encounter such a case and wish to know whether the patient will recover or not. Look, if you see that his hands have become weak, numb, and lifeless, and he is unable to move them or flex or clench them, and if pricking or poking them with a needle causes him no sensation or pain, then know that recovery is unlikely in most cases, and he will perish.
If he is able to move them and feels sensation when pricked or poked, then know that the spinal cord remains intact and the patient will recover with treatment. If a similar injury occurs in the spine, you can determine whether recovery is certain.
Look at his legs; if they have become weak and show similar symptoms to the hands, then when he lies down on his back, gas and feces escape involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine escapes without control. If he lies down on his back and tries to urinate but cannot, then know that he is beyond help, and there is no need to treat him. If none of this occurs, the condition is milder, and treatment should focus on alleviating the hot swelling.
By placing rose fat or fat from boiled egg whites on the affected fistula, apply it three times a day until the hot tumor subsides. Then, apply one of the strengthening, drying dressings to the area and secure it with a bandage, instructing the patient to rest and remain still.
He should not sleep on the affected side until the pain subsides, and if a fracture or something that has healed away is present, it should be cut into the skin and the bone removed. Then, if the wound is large, close its edges with stitches and treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the tailbone (os coccygis) breaks, insert the thumb from the left hand into the anus.
Adjust the broken bone with the other hand as best you can, then apply a dressing and a splint if necessary, and secure it tightly.
If you feel a broken fragment inside, cut into it to remove the splinter and treat the wound as previously described until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On the reduction of a hip fracture.
If the hip bones break, and they only crack at their extremities, or split along their length and bend inward, this causes pain for the patient in that area, numbness, and paralysis of the leg on the broken side.
If the repair requires passing your hand over it to assess the fracture, and it is only a crack at the extremities, then correct that fracture as best you can so that it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is along its length or it has bent inward, lay the patient on their stomach to assess how to set the bone. Once set, apply a bandage and then place a splint made of wood or leather over it, securing it tightly.
Do not worry about the fracture shifting or the splint coming loose, and correct any gaps in the hips using padding until the pressure is even. Then instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on the healthy side. If a hot swelling appears, take appropriate measures.
Cease to extend or adjust the fracture until the hot swelling subsides, and apply what has been mentioned previously to relieve it. Then return to setting and tightening the splint as appropriate. If any fragments or detachement from its ends are seen in the bone, they should not be removed.
It should not be touched from within, but rather smoothed from without, as we have said, and its tension should be left until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
In the setting of a humerus fracture.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder. If it breaks, treat it by using a curved, smooth stick of medium thickness, as shown in the figure.
Tie two straps at the ends, then hang it from a high place and have the patient sit on a chair. Then place the broken arm on the stick so that the armpit is in line with the middle of its curve, and hang a heavy object from above.
Or, a servant can extend it downwards, and the physician can adjust the fracture with both hands to return it to its proper place. Alternatively, have the patient lie on their back and hang the arm from their neck with a strap.
Then command two servants: one to hold the upper part of the fracture with both hands, and the other to hold the lower part, extending each towards their respective sides. If you wish for greater tension, tighten the bandage below the fracture and above it, and have each servant extend their arm towards their side.
If the fracture is close to the side of the shoulder joint, the bandage should be placed under the armpit and the other end beneath the fracture towards the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, the bandage should be applied at that point and also around the elbow itself.
Then, the fracture should be gently adjusted without force until it is aligned correctly and united well. If there is no sign of a hot swell, tighten the bandage. If a hot swell appears, do not tighten the bandage until the seventh day, applying a compress moistened with vinegar and rose oil.
Once the swelling subsides, then tighten the bandage. The method for tightening it involves placing a compress over the fracture, covering it with a new cloth wrap, and then wrapping the arm around the elbow, keeping the hand open on the shoulder.
Carry the cloth and tighten it around the elbow and arm, ensuring that the arm functions as a splint. However, if there is any obstacle or change in the fractured bone, consider using a splint instead.
This involves placing a splint directly on the fracture, which should be wider and stronger than other splints. Ensure there is a gap of one finger width between each splint, and adjust the length of the splints to exceed the fracture by three fingers on each side.
Then apply the binding force to the bandages as mentioned at the beginning of this book.
And it should be that the force applied to the fracture site is greater.
And the more distant the fracture, the less force should be applied; if you observe the placement of the bandages and binding as we mentioned when you reduced the member in its time, then do so.
If you fear the formation of a hot swelling, leave off the binding and bandages until the seventh day, as we mentioned; then examine the dressing every three days to ensure there is no itching, puffiness, or blockage of nutrition to the member due to excessive tightness.
If you are certain that none of these issues will occur, do not remove the binding for several days; the patient should lie on their back, with their hand on their stomach and a moderate amount of wool under the arm.
Examine at all times, day or night, to ensure the shape of the fractured member does not change or the binding becomes loose; correct any issues with your best effort. Make the patient's food mild as mentioned previously, so that when the bone begins to harden, it will be strengthened.
It is necessary to stiffen his diet, for the arm and leg usually harden within forty days, at which time they should be resolved and the patient bathed and treated with ointments suitable for the purpose, especially if the fracture is severe and dislocated.
Do not remove the bandage from him for fifty days or two months.
Chapter XII.
In setting a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the humerus, one smaller than the other, with the smaller one attached to the thumb, and the larger one placed below it. It is possible for either the larger or smaller bone to break alone, or both may break simultaneously.
When the smaller upper humerus breaks, it is easier to set and heals faster, but when the lower humerus breaks, its fracture is poor and healing is more difficult. The worst case is when both bones break simultaneously; if the bone attached to the thumb (the smaller one) breaks, then the healing process will be quicker.
When a doctor sets a fracture, they should make the healing process gentle but firm to align the bones. If only the larger humerus is broken, the extension should be more forceful; if both humeri are broken, the extension should be even stronger.
The hand should be positioned during its setting and stretching with the palm extended on a cushion, with the thumb above all the fingers and the little finger below all the others, with the patient seated comfortably on their own.
And let the cushion be at his side in terms of height so as not to burden the patient.
Then, the servant extends the arm from below with his hand or a bandage, and another servant extends it from above as well. The doctor then adjusts the bone until it is restored to its best form possible. If there are fragments in the broken bone, they are removed.
Return each fragment to its place with effort. If a fragment is exposed and penetrates the skin but cannot be set, make an incision and extract it as described earlier. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, a separate chapter has been dedicated to it.
If, during your initial treatment, a hot swelling appears, apply a compress with rose petal and beeswax oil, medium between thickness and thinness, and gently secure it until the swelling subsides.
Remove the compress and apply a bandage prepared with flour, then take up the splints. The splint placed directly on the fracture should be slightly wider and stronger, and note that in most cases there will be six splints for the arm.
When the fracture is in one clavicle or in both, then make your tension on the site of the fracture stronger and more intense. And whenever you rotate the tension upwards or downwards, it should be slightly looser as mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
The bandages wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, not too stiff. The thread used for tension should be made of special cotton, intermediate in softness and hardness, as described earlier. Check the member and ligament after several days.
If something requires adjustment, such as an itch appearing on the member, it should be soaked in warm water to relieve the itch. The member should then be left unstrained for a night to rest, after which the tension is resumed.
If the tension relaxes and the bone moves or similar issues occur, use all your effort to correct them. Also, check if the member is deprived of food due to excessive tension; in that case, you should loosen it slightly and leave it for several days until blood and nutrients can reach it, then tighten it again.
If the patient does not exhibit any of the issues mentioned, treatment should not be initiated before 20 days or so. Then, hang the patient's arm from their neck, keeping it straight and guarding against sudden movements. The patient should sleep on their back.
Know that this fracture of the arm will heal in thirty days, or in thirty-two, and perhaps even in twenty-eight days; all depending on the conditions of the humours and the strength.
Chapter XIII.
In setting a fracture of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpal bone of the hand and the phalanges of the fingers are rarely fractured, but they are often bruised; so, if a fracture or bruising occurs in the hand, it is necessary to pay attention.
The patient should sit cross-legged, with a chair of equal height in front of him.
Then, he places his hand over it, extends it, and the surgeon straightens the broken bone until it is properly aligned. If there is no swelling or heat, a splint should be applied, followed by a cast above, according to the location of the fracture.
He should be wrapped in a soft cloth, and if the fracture is towards the palm, make a semi-sphere of cloth and have the patient grasp it with his injured hand, then secure it with a long strip of cloth.
The splint should be made of soft leather that conforms to the bend of the hand and secures it as needed. If the fracture is outward, a splint should be applied above and another below the hand to keep it open and extended.
Then, the tension should be carried as the hand rotates, and the fingers should be interwoven with bandages. If the fracture affects one of the phalanges, the thumb should be positioned as needed, then secured along with the hand. If desired, a small, separate splint can be used to stabilize the fracture.
If the fracture does not shift and affects other fingers like the middle, index, ring, or pinky finger, they should be aligned and secured with the adjacent correct finger or all tied together, which is preferable. Alternatively, a small, separate splint can be used to support the fracture.
As we have mentioned regarding the thumb, inspect during the splinting process and after to ensure it is free from excessive heat swelling. Adjust accordingly as needed, based on the previously described methods.
Chapter XIV
In the splinting of a thigh fracture.
The thigh bone is often broken and easily felt because it can shift forward or backward. Its splinting involves tying a strap above the fracture and another below, with the patient lying on their face.
Then, each servant tie should be extended to its side with moderation, especially if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is close to the origin of the thigh, a soft tie should be tightened, either from wool or similar material.
When the fracture is near the origin of the thigh, extending up, and another tie below the fracture, as well as if the fracture is near the knee, the tie should be placed near the knee to extend downward; then the physician should adjust the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural shape.
The bone will heal well when, in the absence of swelling, one should apply bandages and tension. If a hot swelling occurs, leave it for several days until the hot swelling subsides before proceeding with treatment.
As for the tension, one should wrap a wide, solid cloth around the fracture twice or three times, leaving some excess, then twist the leg until the heel is at the origin of the thigh, and insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, with the ends of the thread extending upwards on both sides.
Then, wrap the excess cloth around the leg and thigh, place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture, create a single brace on the leg bone, and fill the space between the thigh and leg with soft rags to ensure even tension.
Then begin by tightly binding the middle of the wound three or four times, and as you move further away from the fracture with the bandage, make your tension lighter and less tight.
Then, proceed to the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg, and tie it to the subsequent bandages above, then pass the ends of the thread downwards until it reaches the heel of the man, and also tighten the ends of the bandages on the other side with it.
In order to prevent the bandage from shifting from its position, leave the tension on it as long as it does not cause pain or swelling or any other symptom to the member. If anything of this kind occurs, then unbind it and correct what has happened, as we have mentioned many times before.
If there is a splinter in the bone that protrudes, you should smooth it out if possible, otherwise make a cut and extract it, and treat the wound as mentioned before until healing occurs. This fracture of the thigh may be secured without adding the leg with bandages.
As we mentioned regarding the upper arm and forearm, except that its fracture should not cause a patient to limp. However, if it is only bound without adding the leg, it is inevitable that the patient will always limp.
Know that the thigh will heal in fifty days or slightly more, or slightly less, all depending on the variation of humors and other conditions.
Chapter XV.
On the fracture of the knee joint.
The knee joint is rarely subjected to fracture, but it may be bruised frequently; if a fracture does occur, it is either a tear or a dislocation of its parts, with or without a wound.
All of this should be judged by touch, and it should be reduced using the fingers until the parts are aligned, then a dressing should be applied and, if necessary, a rounded splint should be used.
The bandage should be tightened according to the reduction, and then all subsequent conditions, such as swelling or heat, should be treated with appropriate remedies until healing occurs.
Chapter XVI.
On setting a fracture of the leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick, called the shin bone, and the other thin, called the fibula, and they suffer from types of fractures similar to those of the arm bones. Therefore, its treatment is the same as that of the arm, and the procedure is identical; if both bones are broken, the leg turns in all directions, and if the thinner bone breaks, the leg turns forward, and if the thicker bone breaks from below, it is something you are well aware of.
Then, use compression, shaping, and binding according to these principles, which are the same, except that if the leg fracture is severe with numerous fragments, the pressure should be less and lighter, and you should exercise caution in its treatment.
In the leg, there is an additional step in the process, as after you have shaped the splints and finished all your work, take two lengths of pine wood used for flattening rooms, or palm fronds or similar materials, to fill any gaps between the boards.
Choose a length that is slightly thick and not too thin, matching the length of the leg from the knee to below, then wrap each one with cloth twice around its length, placing one along the leg and another on the opposite side.
Make them extend from the knee to below the foot, then tie the two lengths together in three places on each side and in the middle; this harness prevents the leg from tilting to the right or left and ensures a good fit. A wooden splint of the same length as the leg can also be used.
And it is placed there to preserve it from movement, and it is most necessary to do this in fractures if there is a specific wound, then inspect the leg every day and pay close attention to it regarding swelling or distension or any other issues. If such a thing occurs, treat it as necessary until it heals, and the leg bone will heal in around thirty days.
**Chapter XVII**
**On Fractures of the Leg Bones and Fingers**
As for the heel, it never suffers a fracture, while the leg bones can suffer fractures, and the fingers even less often, as they are more prone to bruising in most cases.
If a fracture occurs in the leg bones and you see that some of them are pressing against each other, the patient should place their foot on the ground straight like walking, then you place your foot on what is raised from those bones and step on it, smoothing it out.
Until they return to their proper positions, then take the bandage and splint, placing a small board with two flat ends beneath the foot, after wrapping it in cloth and using all necessary materials.
If three or four days have passed, then release the bandage, for you will find the bones either levelled after a fracture or realigned after being dislocated. The procedure is as you have been taught for setting and straightening the fingers.
Then, make a splint for the broken finger that extends along the finger but is slightly narrower, and place this board under the foot as described, securing it tightly. If two or three or more bones are broken.
Make a splint for each finger, graduated in size and placed in a soft cloth, then secure the board beneath the foot, with each end of the board's head extending outside the sole of the foot to ensure proper support.
You must not forget to follow all that has been mentioned for the treatment of other fractures, addressing each symptom as it arises with the appropriate method.
Chapter XVIII
In this chapter, the text discusses the treatment of fractures specific to women's and men's bodies, including the fragility of a woman's pelvis (vagina) and a man's pubic bone.
When the woman's vagina is fractured, have her sit in a squatting position, gradually bending her towards her back. A midwife should grasp her from behind and insert cotton into the vagina until it fills and assumes the shape of a ball, providing support and comfort.
Then, the woman is shaken and her body raised slightly by slightly, so that this cotton emerges until it becomes like a ball at the entrance of the vagina, and the fracture of the bone returns. Then, she places a pad on her back. Whenever she wants to urinate, she removes the cotton gently until she urinates.
It is then returned to its original position, and she returns to her first pad, doing this for seven days or so, and it will heal. If you wish to use a goat's bladder, you can tighten the mouth of a tube made from a reed and insert the entire bladder into her vagina.
Then, you blow into the tube strongly so that the bladder inside the vagina expands. This will help the fracture heal. Then, fill it with cotton and maintain this position for several days as described until it heals. For a fracture of the pubic bone in a man or woman, this method is also used for healing.
This method should also be applied to the hip bone, and the correct approach to these rare fractures is clear to anyone familiar with this text. In most cases, the fracture itself provides a clear indication of the extent of the injury.
Follow the correct procedure for its repair and stabilization. If a man's phallus breaks, insert a duck's esophagus into it, wrap it with cloth, bind it, and leave it for about three days or so until it heals.
Chapter XIX.
In the treatment of fractured bones with an associated wound.
For one who presents with a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the femur, humerus, or similar, one should immediately proceed to reduce the fracture if the conditions for reduction as we have previously provided permit.
If the wound is bleeding, one should immediately apply powdered glass (or any available material) to stop the bleeding, and then proceed with reducing the fracture on that same day without delay.
If a hot swelling does not occur, but a warm swelling does, one should postpone reducing the fracture until the ninth day, when the warm swelling subsides, and under no circumstances should one attempt to reduce it on the third or fourth day, as it may result in adverse effects.
If the fractured bone is exposed through the skin, one should attempt to reduce and set it using their hands with gentle pressure and a slight movement. If it is not possible to do so manually, use this tool, which is made of iron, about seven or eight fingers long. and position it according to the size of the wound.
Therefore, the surgeon should select three or four, depending on what is needed for treatment in each type of fracture, and they should be rounded with a slight thickness to prevent them from bending when manipulated during surgery.
And they should be sharp at the end with a bend at that end, and above it should be slightly thick, and from its middle to below very thin, and this is its form.
They are called in Greek "perim" which refers to a small lever. Therefore, it should be so that its sharp, bent end rests on the side of the bone to be moved, and with one push, it drives the bone back into place, and when the bone has slightly straightened, the surgeon shapes the ends of the fracture against each other with a chisel.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grip it well, then you should cut off that bone end so that the instrument can reach it. If, under no circumstances, the bone can be straightened as described, then cut it with a suitable cutting tool that matches its shape.
Or use a saw to cut it as best you can, then remove any remaining roughness and thin scales from the bone. If, after pushing back the bone, the patient feels severe, painful pain, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can return it to its natural position, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. Once you have successfully set the bone, dip a cloth in a contracting black liquid, especially during the summer, and do not place any bandages or greasy substances on the wound.
To prevent it from becoming foul or rotten, then use bandages when you have finished setting the bone and leave the wound open by making a hole in the gauze with scissors to match the size of the wound.
Be extremely careful not to tighten the wound with the fracture, as this has often been done by ignorant physicians, causing either death, gangrene, or a cold. Let your tightening be gentle and relaxing, contrary to the tightness of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or it is a large wound and you fear some of the bad symptoms we described, and it experiences pain in the affected area that is disturbing, do not place bandages on it and make for it rolls of stiff cloth in the place where the bandages would be, and tighten it with these.
If, after a day or two, you see that the wound has begun to form pus, remove the cloth you put on it with the liquid, then use twine and ointments as we usually treat wounds, such as the four-ingredient ointment and similar ones, and you should loosen the bandage.
Examine the wound every morning and evening until it heals. The member should be positioned to allow pus to drain easily downwards, for if the wound remains infected after many days without closing or pus ceasing, know that there are small pieces of bone within it.
You should inspect the wound with a probe. If any of the fragments are protruding, extract and remove them. If others are not yet ready to come out but cause pain in the member, cut them into pieces and extract them as best as you can.
If the wound develops a cold, or an infection, or any other form of corruption and putrefaction, it should be treated with the same method of cure described for its kind in its respective chapter.
And among what you should pay attention to and keep in mind: if a large bone breaks and is situated over a member, such as the thigh or upper arm bone, do not attempt to pull or remove it. Often, this leads to death. Leave it alone until it putrefies, for after about twenty or thirty days, it may fall off by itself.
Then, treat the wound if you see a place that can be treated, otherwise leave it alone.
Chapter XX.
In the treatment of the complication that occurs as a result of certain fractures.
This complication often occurs after the fracture has healed, particularly in those near joints, disfiguring the member and possibly preventing it from performing its natural function.
If the complication is soft, use drugs that contract, such as myrrh, gum arabic, frankincense, and other similar substances, taking some or all of them and mixing them with a contracting solution, or egg whites, or vinegar.
Apply this mixture to the complication, holding it firmly for a while, then leaving it for several days without releasing the pressure, and repeating this process until the complication disappears, or attach a tight plate of lead to it.
Lead has the property to dissolve all substances in the body. If the complication has hardened, and its removal is necessary, make an incision from above and cut off or scrape away the protruding fragment until it disappears, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter XXI.
In the treatment of fractures, if the bone heals and the member subsequently becomes softer than its original state, it is necessary to apply further treatment.
If a bone fracture heals, but the member remains soft and weak, it may be due to numerous reasons. One of the reasons for this may be either due to excessive use of ties and binding them incorrectly, or over-tightening the ties preventing nutrition from reaching the member, or excessive immobilization, or improper movements at the wrong time, or lack of blood in the patient's body and its weakness.
The treatment involves providing proper nutrition to the patient, enriching their body to increase blood volume, utilizing baths, promoting happiness and joy, and then applying tar to the member to attract a large amount of nutrition or maintaining its immobilization with warm water. until nutrition is facilitated and it returns to its original shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of broken bones that heal in a twisted position, preventing them from functioning as they should.
When a member has been set and healed with a deviation or curvature of the broken bone, or has become complicated and disfigured, the result may be unsightly. However, if the member does not prevent its natural function, one should not accept the claim that it must be broken from the root; many ignorant physicians and repairmen have done so in our country.
This action is highly reprehensible, leading to great uncertainty, and among its consequences is damage; but if the curvature and complexity are still fresh, it should be treated with water in which relaxant herbs have been boiled, such as plantain leaves, mint, and rosemary.
It is bandaged with relaxing compresses, such as well-prepared diachylon, or one may use the juice of plantain leaves mixed with chicken fat and goosefat, or take ripe figs, pounded with pigeon droppings, among other remedies known as "narcotic agents.
The complexity dissolves with continuous, gentle friction using the hands, and by moving the member in all directions at all times. If the deviation has become old, severe, and rigid, and requires iron treatment.
One should then make a cut above it, releasing the bone connection and cutting what remains of the complexity or bone with delicate cuts, using gentleness and care, and then treat the wound as mentioned before until healing occurs.
Chapter Twenty-Third.
The jaw, when it deviates from its joint and becomes dislocated, obstructs movement, distorts the shape of the member, and causes severe pain and distress to the patient. Therefore, whenever either of the jaws is displaced, one should immediately seek to reduce it.
It should not be delayed under any circumstances, for if swelling at the site delays its reduction, it becomes difficult to restore the jaw. Therefore, no delay or movement should be made once swelling occurs, as it often causes severe spasms and painful contractions in the patient.
However, if this occurs, one should immediately seek to drain the swelling, then allow it to subside slightly, after which the member is bathed in hot water and grease, and then gently reduced. Each member should be treated as described in its respective section.
The chapters on the jaw have also been arranged according to the order of the fracture, from the upper part of the body to its lower part, as previously mentioned.
Chapter Twenty-Fourth.
In the treatment of lower jaw fractures.
Fractures of the jaws rarely occur, and their treatment depends on whether they are displaced or not. If slightly displaced, they may heal by relaxing into place. If completely dislocated, they must be reduced to their normal position for healing. When the fracture involves the lower jaw, the patient may have difficulty controlling his saliva, making it impossible for him to close his mouth or speak clearly. If the dislocation is minor, in most cases the jaw will return to its normal position with minimal effort.
If the dislocation is complete, it is essential to reduce the fracture as quickly as possible. A helper should grasp the patient's head, while the surgeon inserts one finger into the mouth, at the base of the jaw on the affected side.
If the fracture involves both sides of the jaw, the surgeon should insert both thumbs into the mouth and all other fingers from outside to adjust them. The patient is instructed to relax his jaw, allowing the fingers to guide it back to its normal position.
If the reduction is difficult, especially with both jaws involved, apply hot compresses and fat to facilitate their return. Once both jaws are back in place and the patient's mouth is closed, a mixture of lint and beeswax can be applied for protection.
Then, gently bind it with a loose bandage, so that the patient lies on their back with their head resting between two pillows to prevent any movement to the right or left and to avoid the effort of chewing.
Until the pain disappears and the jaw unclenches, he may eat what he pleases, but he should do so gently and not force his mouth open while eating, drinking, or yawning, until the jaw heals.
If the jaw fails to return to its position after unclenching, it often leads to fever, persistent headache, and even abdominal pain and vomiting. If these symptoms occur, know that the patient is in a critical state.
And often, it leads to death within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Fifth.
On the reduction of the jaw, shoulder blade, and collarbone.
As for the shoulder blade, it does not unclench from the inside due to its connection to the chest. It may dislocate outward, which is evident to the touch. To reduce it, the patient should lie on their back and extend their arms.
Then press the site firmly with your hand, and it will recede. Apply a dressing and bandages, and tighten them. As for the end that is next to the shoulder and connected to it, it will only come loose rarely; if it does, it should be returned and smoothed as we have mentioned, and what is suitable for you.
Then apply a dressing, bandages, and traction, and instruct the patient to remain motionless and at rest until they recover. With this very treatment, the end of the shoulder will also be returned if it becomes detached again.
Chapter Twenty-Six
In the restoration of the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder joint becomes detached in three ways: one is towards the armpit downward, the second is towards the chest, and it may detach upward over the shoulder, which occurs rarely.
It does not detach towards the back of the shoulder or in front of the nerve, but it most often detaches and protrudes downward towards the armpit, especially in those with little flesh, as it comes out quickly and re-enters quickly.
As for those with plenty of flesh, it is the opposite; it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty. Sometimes, a person might experience a blow or fall that causes the shoulder to swell up hot and inflamed, leading one to think it has detached; therefore, you should examine it to confirm its condition.
When treating, the shoulder joint should be examined by comparing the dislocated and healthy joints. You will find a visible difference between them, and there is a depression at the top of the shoulder joint when touched, resembling an egg.
The patient is unable to raise their hand to their ear or move it through all movements, and if it dislocates towards the chest or above, this is clearly visible to the touch and does not go unnoticed.
If the joint is soft or the patient is a child, it can be reduced by having an assistant raise their hand above their head, then place their thumbs under the armpit and lift the joint forcefully back to its position while the patient extends their arm upwards.
Then, by gently lowering it, it will quickly return; if it does not return as mentioned, and the joint has been dislocated for many days, the patient should take a hot bath and use a relaxant that softens the muscles, such as cooking roots of fennel, cumin, and rosemary in water.
The patient should lie on their back, and a woolen ball should be placed under the armpit, firm enough to provide support but not too tight. The physician then places their hand over the ball and takes the patient's hand, extending it gently downwards.
If one pushes the ball with their foot, they will be pulled back to their original position. Alternatively, you can achieve this by having a taller man assist, where he stops on the side and places his elbow under the patient's armpit, lifting the armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. Another assistant pulls the patient's hand towards their lower abdomen. If the patient is light, a weight should be attached to them to make them heavier, so that the jaw dislocates naturally. It may also heal in another way.
This involves focusing on the ground a long wooden rod with a round head, not too thick nor too thin, and placing it under the patient's armpit after covering its top with a soft cloth, while the patient stands upright along the length of the wood.
Then they extend their hand downwards on the opposite side, and also stretch their body from the other side with force, causing the joint to return to its place quickly. If it is difficult to retrieve using all mentioned methods, employ this treatment: take a wooden rod approximately two arms' length in length. It should be approximately four fingers in width and two fingers in thickness, with a round head to facilitate its depth within the armpit, as illustrated.
Then, a soft cloth is tied around the round head to prevent any harm to the wood, and it is placed under the armpit. The hand and arm are extended downwards onto the wood, and the wooden rod is secured to the upper arm, forearm, and wrist.
Then the arm is placed on a support. Move the arm across and extend the hand downwards, while the rest of the body is suspended on the other side. Thus, the joint will enter naturally, and if it enters correctly, a moderate-sized sphere of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared bandage, made from flour and mixed with frankincense and egg white, is spread over the entire shoulder as it rotates overhead. The ball under the armpit is then tightly secured, and the bandage is wrapped around it and tied above, with the hand hanging down to the neck.
He should keep his hand immobile for seven days, and the patient's diet should be light to strengthen the member, as this aids faster recovery. After seven or five days, the bandage is removed, and the joint is moved through its range of motion. If it holds up without relaxing, the patient is considered healed.
If the joint keeps dislocating due to moisture or other issues, the three black peppers should be applied as mentioned in the chapter on cauterization. After preparing it in this way and removing the bandage after seven days, if the joint does not stabilize, the treatment is incomplete.
If the bandage and pressure are applied repeatedly, but the joint does not stabilize, falls, and becomes relaxed, unable to be raised above, then know that the nerve in the shoulder joint has either been severed, stretched, or damaged. In this case, the joint will never hold its position again.
And as for the jaw that is inclined towards the chest and breast, and when it is pushed and pulled with the hands, it returns to its place, all the pulling and treatment methods are used in it until it heals, if after healing a sensitivity appears in the member and movement slows down.
The patient should take many baths until the sensitivity in the member diminishes and returns to its original state.
Chapter XXVII
In the treatment of the elbow joint.
The elbow joint disconnects with difficulty and returns with difficulty as well, it disconnects in all directions, particularly to the front or rear, its dislocation is evident to the eye and touch, for it is located under direct observation and tactile sensation, to whatever shape it has separated.
When you compare the dislocated elbow joint with a healthy one, it becomes evident due to the indentation in the joint; the arm cannot be bent and the shoulder cannot reach it. You must act promptly to reduce the dislocation before a hot swelling occurs.
If a hot swelling appears, reducing it becomes difficult, and in some cases, it may never heal, especially if the joint is pushed back. In such cases, it is the most severe form of dislocation and often leads to intense pain, sometimes even resulting in death.
Reduce the dislocation if possible by extending both hands and arms of the patient, and the surgeon's hand from above and below the joint, using the thumbs of both hands or the palm's base to push the joint back into place.
If the joint is displaced forward, it can be reduced by bending the hand once and striking the acromion with the base of the palm, facing the joint. If this does not work, use a strong pull involving both assistants to extend the arm.
The patient is also held by two assistants to prevent dislocation during the pull, then the arm is turned in both directions after being wrapped with a folded cloth or wide bandage. The surgeon should grease the joint with a lubricant to facilitate its sliding.
Then, push the joint firmly until it returns to its place. After its return, apply a compress with starch and egg white, tighten it well, hang the arm from the patient's neck, and leave it for several days.
Then, unfasten it, and if the joint remains stable in its place, release the bandage and leave it. If the joint does not strengthen sufficiently, reapply the compress and bandage and leave it for additional days until it strengthens, then release it again.
In case of difficulty in movement, use moisture in the bath and gentle rubbing and tapping until it softens, or place a fat goat skin over the joint, tie it there for a day and night, then remove it and immerse it in the bath. If it perspires, rub the joint gently.
Then repeat the application of the skin in the bath two or three times until it softens. If you prefer, you can moisten cow dung with butter and apply it, repeating this several times; it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight
On the Treatment of Wrist Dislocation.
The wrist joint is often more prone to dislocation and its reduction is easier than other joints, except that it is necessary to quickly reduce the dislocation within an hour of its occurrence before swelling or a hot bruise develops.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board, have a helper extend the affected hand, and the physician places his hand on the joint, pushing it back into its proper position. It is important to ensure that the dislocation has returned completely to the inside of the hand.
Let the patient place the visible part of his hand on the board during flexion and extension, and if the jaw protrudes to the visible hand, let him place his inner hand on the board so that the physician's hand may fall on the same joint surface. If he immediately returns, otherwise, tighten it with a soothing bandage for the swelling.
Leave it, do not touch it again, for it is impervious to treatment, and after several days have passed, unless the joint remains in its normal position and does not cause any harm to the patient, except if the hand loses its strength and cannot be clenched.
If, therefore, you learn that the nerve is severed or injured, there is no remedy but to apply heat therapy, which may or may not be effective. Once the wrist is successfully restored, apply the dressing described and secure it tightly, leaving it for five days.
Then, it relaxes and the hand is moved. If movement is difficult or there is any discomfort, soften it with warm water and friction several times until it becomes flexible.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the treatment of finger dislocation.
If a finger separates and points in any direction, when it turns to face the palm or the back of the hand, extend the finger and push the joint with your thumb until it returns to its place. Then, bind the tip of the finger and hang it towards the side it had separated to.
So leave it for two days, then release it and stretch it until it stands upright on its own that day. If at night, tie it as described before; in the daytime, undo it and move it, and at night, tie it. Do this for several days until it becomes strong.
Similarly, if it detaches inside the hand, tie it in the same direction and perform your initial procedure until it heals, and do the same when it detaches from other sides.
Chapter Thirty.
Treating the dislocation of the back bead.
When a bead from the beads of the back or neck is completely dislocated, or many beads have fallen out of place, there is no treatment as death swiftly approaches the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's excreta comes out without their will, unable to hold it, and often some of their members relax, either both legs or both arms or one of them; and if just one bead has come loose from its place, it often falls out completely.
It can come loose in four directions: if it comes out behind, it is called a hump. Its treatment involves examining; if the hump occurred from childhood, there is no cure for it and it will never heal entirely. However, if it happened due to a fall or blow, etc., then...
They [the ancients] mentioned these treatments in long discourse, much of which offers little practical benefit. I have condensed from it what barely covers the essentials, and depicted the instrument differently than they described and explained it.
I say that the hump that occurs at the front of the chest, or on both sides, is incurable and offers no relief; however, it can be treated, specifically, by positioning the patient face-down.
On a flat bed placed near a wall, with a soft mattress below to prevent strain on the chest, place a vertical wooden pole embedded in the ground at one end of the bed, towards the head, and another pole at the other end, towards the feet.
Have a servant hold the pole, unsecured from the hole, while another holds the other pole in the same manner. Then, wrap the patient's chest and armpits with tight but soft cloth, extending the cloth to the headpole and securing it there.
Then, secure another cloth over the hips and above the knees and at the ankles. Gather all the bindings and attach them to the other pole at the feet, while each servant holds their respective poles, ensuring they remain in place.
Unless they are documented as we have said, the surgeon places his hands firmly on the hernia and pushes until it returns, or he places a board on it and then leans on the board with his legs until it retracts. If this treatment does not work, take a board that is about three arms' length long.
And dig in the wall, as we mentioned, a space for the end of the board to enter, then place the middle of the board on the hernia, and the doctor puts his legs on the other end, and tightens it well until it compresses the hernia.
And it returns to its place. If you prefer, make it with a spiral that is twisted by hand, by driving two wooden rods into the ground at the head of the patient in the far end of the bed, and also at his feet, another two rods.
After that, at a distance of one palm between each wood, make a hole in every wood through which the spiral will pass, and secure all the woods firmly in the ground so that they do not move. Insert a rounded stick, which is the spiral with the tie wrapped around it, into the holes in both woods.
At the end, make a hole through which insert a stick of one palm length that is twisted. Do the same in the other two woods. Then, tighten the strap that was tightened on the patient's chest in the spiral at his head and the straps that were tightened on his legs in the spiral at his feet.
Then he stops at each spiral, twisting his hand with the twist that twists the spiral, while the doctor aligns the hump as we have mentioned. And this is an image of the spiral, the stand, and the patient.
Then, after the spine returns and the position is levelled, one should carry the dried bandage made with egg white, then the padding, and then place a splint above the bandage, which should be a board of a width of three fingers or so, and its length should be as much as takes up the hump's place.
And on some proper beads, then tie it with the necessary bandage and let the patient use soft food until healed. If there is some remaining hump in the end of healing, then one should use loosening and softening medications along with the board we described before.
One might also use a lead plate, and sometimes a bump may remain in the last beads of the spine, leading one to think it is dislocated, and that could be an extra bone that has grown, so do not expose it to this treatment, as it might cause death.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the elbow joint only involve dislocation, not the slight displacement or malalignment that occurs in other joints. The hip joint can be dislocated in four ways.
That is because it detaches inward and outward, to the front and back, but most often inward, and seldom forward or backward; and the sign of its detachment inward is that if you compare the healthy leg of the patient with the sick one, the former will be longer.
The knee will be bent more than it should, and the patient cannot flex their leg at the thigh; and the area immediately following the thigh will be visibly swollen before the head of the femur reaches that position.
The signs of a detachment outward will have symptoms opposite to these; and for a detachment forward, the patient will fully extend their leg, except they cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if they attempt to walk forward, they will be unable to do so.
They will retain their urine, and their thigh will be tight, and when walking, they will bear weight on the heel; and for those whose kneecap detaches backward, they cannot fully extend their knee or bend it before the thigh muscle contracts, and their leg will also be shorter than the other.
The thigh muscle will be relaxed, and the head of the femur will be clearly visible at the hip; and regarding the correction of this detachment, if you observe that the kneecap has been old and attached to its partner and did not try to return and remain in its state, there is no cure for it at all.
It is not proper to attempt to reduce it, and if the dislocation is recent and of any of the four types of the jaw, then immediately twist the joint and extend it inward and outward, moving it right and left, for perhaps...
If it returns [to its proper position] without further treatment, then the patient should be assisted with a strong helper who extends his leg from below using his hands or a cloth wrapped around his leg above the knee, and another assistant pulls from above.
by inserting his hands under the patient's armpits, then tightening a soft cloth around the hip joint, and holding the end of the cloth by a third assistant, who pulls either from the front towards the shoulder blade or from behind towards the back.
They should all be pulled at once until the patient raises his body from the ground and remains suspended, for this type of pull is common to all four types. If the jaw returns as we described, otherwise each type requires the specific treatment mentioned.
For the specific treatment if the jaw is dislocated inward, the patient should lie on his right side, then a cloth is wrapped around the hip joint between the thigh bone and the area below the knee.
Then, the bandage is extended upwards from the thigh to the upper part of the body towards the shoulder blade, and another assistant, supported by his arms, embraces the thick portion of the diseased thigh and extends it outward with a powerful pull; then it returns to its place.
And this is the easiest type of treatment for returning this member to its place, if it does not respond to this type of treatment at all, then both legs of the patient should be bound together with a strong, flexible bandage around the ankles and knees.
And each one should be separated by the width of four fingers, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by two fingers, then the patient is suspended from a beam in the house, with a distance of two arms' length from the ground.
Then you command a strong boy to embrace the thigh and another boy to hold onto the patient, and the other boy should push the one embracing the thigh with force; then the joint will quickly return to its place. If, however, the jaw is pushed outward, it has its own return.
Therefore, the patient should lie on their side on the bed as described for the hunchback, tightening the bandage especially on the affected leg and chest. Then, place one beam at the patient's feet and another at their head, and add an additional beam in the middle of the bed, secured very tightly.
A moist bandage is wrapped around it to prevent harm to the patient, with a stick placed between his thighs to prevent it from sagging downward during the tide. Then each attendant stretches towards their side, and the physician aligns the hip with his hands, if it responds to the adjustment.
If it does not respond, place a board on it and press it as we mentioned on the hump, except that the patient should be lying on his right side. If the dislocation is forward, extend the patient's leg once while he is in this position on the table.
The physician places the right hand on the diseased thigh and presses it with the left hand, while extending the pressure downward towards the knee. If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be pulled downward.
It should be elevated above the ground, placed on a firm surface, and according to what we mentioned, the patient's hip should be dislocated outward while he is facing down on the table with his bindings tightened.
As we mentioned earlier, the compression with a board should also be applied to the area where the joint has dislocated, and if the hip joint returns to its place as it should, and its return is evident, marked by the extension of the patient's leg.
If you observe them to be level, and the patient contracts and extends the leg without difficulty, then know that the member has returned to its proper place. At this point, join the thighs and carry the bandage, tightening it with a turban so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain motionless for three or four days, then loosen the bandage and padding, and measure the leg against the other. If they are of equal length, know that the fracture has healed, and release the patient to walk. If there is any sign of lingering relaxation, however, further rest is required.
Then return and dress the wound again, securing it as before, and leave it for three more days. After this, untie it and allow the patient to walk slowly on it for several days until it gains strength, God willing.
**Chapter II and III**
**On the Treatment of Knee Dislocations**
The knee dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (meaning behind), but never forward. The sign of its dislocation is to command the patient to bring his leg close to his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated. To reduce it, have the patient sit down with his legs extended if he is strong enough, and have a servant sit behind him, gripping his middle and gently twisting it backward. Then, sit on his thighs.
Attach your back to his face, place his leg between your legs, then rest your hands on his knee and intertwine your fingers with his knee joints. Then, with firm pressure from your hands, and assistance from another helper extending his leg, the knee is returned to its proper position.
And know that its return is signified by the leg adhering to the thigh with a gentle, non-coerced movement; then bind and press it against the thigh, and wrap them together with a bandage for three or four days, after which release them, and only allow limited walking for several days until it strengthens.
If the return described above is not possible, then use the strong tension of bindings as described in the treatment of the hip to achieve its return.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
In the treatment of the ankle joint.
The ankle joint may move slightly or completely dislocate, and its dislocation is either inward or outward. The sign of its dislocation is the swelling and prominence of the ankle bone towards the side it has moved to.
As for the treatment of its slight movement, it is easy to return it with gentle manual pressure and adjustment. However, if it has completely dislocated, the patient should sit down, and a strong helper should grasp them by the back, mid-way, in order to stabilize them.
Then grasp his foot with your right hand at the top and with your left hand at the bottom, in the area of the ankle. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand, repeat this twice. Then stretch the foot with your left hand and push the front of the foot towards the leg with your right hand without force.
Do this twice as described, then in the third time, push the front of the foot towards the leg while pulling the ankle, if it returns once or twice with these characteristics and you see the foot straightened, then repeat the procedure, for it will return.
If the foot does not return as described, lay the patient face up on the ground and drive wooden pins into the earth tightly between his legs, wrapping a cloth around them to prevent injury, then have a servant hold the patient's thigh.
Then another servant extends the leg with his hands or a strap tied around the patient's neck, extending each servant in the opposite direction of the other and the pin holding the patient's legs together prevents the body from slipping downwards during the extension, then the surgeon adjusts the jaw with his hands.
Another servant holds the healthy leg downwards, if the jaw returns quickly, then take up the bandage and splint, tightening them with strong bands, and tie the foot to the leg downwards, being careful of the nerve above the heel from behind.
To prevent the bandage from becoming too tight and causing harm, leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tighten it again and then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If the patient wishes to walk before this period, there is a risk that the fracture may reopen and be difficult to treat afterward. If a hot tumor appears, the remedies described in other sections for relieving pain should be used until it disappears.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of dislocated toes.
It is necessary to adjust the dislocated joints with a slight stretch, without force, as this is easy and not difficult. If the fracture is in one of the toes on the sole of the foot, the patient should rest their foot on a level surface.
Or, they can stand on a board and you, with your foot, place pressure on the joint until it returns to its proper position, without any protrusion being visible.
Then, place a board under the sole of the foot, covering the entire foot, with two ends. Secure it tightly for three days and then release it, taking care to protect the patient from walking for many days until the fracture heals completely, so that there is no chance of recurrence, God willing.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the types of the jaw that are with a wound or with a fracture or both.
When such things happen, and the jaw is wounded or fractured, its treatment and reduction often result in death.
Therefore, one should not undertake the treatment of such a condition unless they are skilled in the craft, well-experienced, delicate, compassionate, and patient, avoiding haste and excessive confidence, and using, at first, only mild sedatives to calm the inflammation before proceeding.
To God alone belongs the will, except for what we hope for his safety from harm with a light illness and some appearance of hope, so reduce it in its initial moment before the hot inflammation occurs.
If the member returns as you intended, use measures to calm the hot inflammations and treat the wound with suitable drying ointments. If the jaw has a fracture with pieces of bone that have become separated, reduce them by extraction. Follow, in this regard, what we have mentioned about simple diseases in their respective places.
Strive to exert yourself and purify your actions from venturing into uncertain paths, as I have advised you. This will preserve your honor and protect your dignity, God willing.
